Preface

As a university computer science researcher and a veteran entrepreneur, I was profoundly impressed by experiencing firsthand the logical reasoning capabilities emerging from ChatGPT. Although many celebrate the efficiency enhancements in multimodal content creation brought by generative artificial intelligence (AI), the reasoning abilities displayed by ChatGPT are often underestimated. This capability enables ChatGPT to serve not only as the core of a new generation of human–computer interaction but also as an intelligent agent to build automated and semiautomated workflows. It can even merge with industrial control or robotics fields, thereby triggering profound social changes.

Many underestimate the impact of this transformation. Given the current pace of R&D and commercial application iterations, I expect that this transformation will gradually permeate all aspects of human life and production over the next 3–5 years, greatly enhancing existing productivity and thereby initiating a series of changes. If asked to pinpoint the last era called a “major technological transformation,” many would unhesitatingly refer to the dawn of the internet. This transformation will also reshape business models related to content production, change existing work methods, and even drive changes in production methods. Of course, this still depends on whether the next generation of large language models can achieve breakthroughs in controllability of content output.

Main Content of the Book

This book is designed to help readers deeply understand ChatGPT and its related technologies. It consists of 11 chapters that comprehensively explore various aspects.

Chapter 1 provides an in-depth analysis of the technological evolution of large language models, supporting technologies, and technology stacks, and discusses their significant impact on society.

Chapter 2 elaborates on the theoretical foundations and main components of the Transformer model, revealing the principles and applications behind these technologies.

Chapter 3 delves into the generative pretraining process and principles of GPT.

Chapter 4 primarily explores technologies such as layer normalization, orthogonal initialization, and reversible tokenization in GPT-2, and provides a detailed analysis of GPT-2 autoregressive generation process.

Chapter 5 introduces GPT-3 sparse attention mechanisms, metalearning, and content-based learning concepts, and discusses the application of Bayesian inference in conceptual distributions.

Chapter 6 details the pretraining datasets and data processing methods for large language models, as well as distributed training models and architectures.

Chapter 7 deeply analyzes the fundamental principles of the proximal policy optimization (PPO) algorithm.

Chapter 8 focuses on the fine-tuning datasets of reinforcement learning with human feedback (RLHF) and the application of PPO in InstructGPT, discussing the capabilities of multiturn dialog and the necessity of human feedback reinforcement learning.

Chapter 9 explores how to transfer large language models to specific domains at low resource costs.

Chapter 10 primarily introduces the middleware technologies involved in the development of large language models.

Chapter 11 predicts and prospects the future development trends of large language models.

Target Audience for This Book

  • • Product managers in the AI field: For product managers looking to incorporate AI features into their products, understanding the basic principles and operational mechanisms of large language models like ChatGPT is crucial. From this book, they can learn about the design philosophies and construction methods of large language models, as well as how to integrate these models into their products. They can also better understand the performance bottlenecks of their products, which aids in more precise product planning.
  • • Researchers in AI-related fields: For AI researchers, this book can serve as a textbook for a deep understanding of large language models. Whether it's the details of the Transformer model or tips on training and optimizing GPT models, this book provides thorough explanations. More importantly, this book explores some of the cutting-edge research areas, such as human feedback reinforcement learning and bootstrap labeling algorithms.
  • • Engineers specializing in large-scale data processing and analysis: For engineers facing challenges such as efficiently processing large-scale data or building distributed training architectures, this book offers many valuable suggestions and ideas. For example, Chapter 6 delves deeply into data processing and distributed training patterns.
  • • AI enthusiasts and technologically savvy individuals in everyday life: If you are an AI technology enthusiast or someone who uses technology to improve everyday life, this book is also suitable for you. The introduction to large language models in this book is easy to understand, providing a comprehensive overview of this powerful technology. More interestingly, this book offers many practical usage tips and case studies that can be directly applied to your life or work.

Chapter 1 A new milestone in artificial intelligence—ChatGPT

Abstract

This chapter provides an in-depth exploration of the evolution, capabilities, and impact of ChatGPT, a groundbreaking artificial intelligence (AI) application developed by OpenAI. The chapter traces the development history of ChatGPT from its early predecessors like GPT-1 and GPT-2 to the more advanced GPT-3 and GPT-4 models. It highlights the technological advancements that have made ChatGPT a powerful tool capable of complex tasks such as language comprehension, code generation, and multimodal reasoning. The chapter also discusses the architecture underpinning large language models (LLMs), focusing on the transition from traditional natural language processing techniques to Transformer-based models. Additionally, it addresses the significant computational and data requirements for training these models, alongside challenges such as model interpretability, biases, and privacy concerns. The chapter concludes by examining the broader implications of LLMs across various sectors and anticipates future trends in AI development.

Keywords

ChatGPT; OpenAI; transformer model; natural language processing (NLP); artificial general intelligence (AGI); GPT series; human feedback reinforcement learning (HFRL); large language models (LLMs); computational power; model limitations

In November 2022, OpenAI introduced ChatGPT, an artificial intelligence (AI)-powered chat application with diverse functionalities. This application displays intellectual prowess that rivals and sometimes surpasses human performance across numerous professional and academic measures. Upon its launch, ChatGPT garnered widespread acclaim within the technology sector, marking a significant advancement in AI.

1.1 The development history of ChatGPT

For more than 50 years, the quest to enable computers to communicate like humans has been a significant focus in technology. The general public is acquainted with chat applications, tracing back to 1966 when MIT Professor Joseph Weizenbaum created ELIZA, the first program of its kind. Over the decades, advancements have been evident from Apple’s Siri and Microsoft’s Xiaoice to smart speakers and intelligent assistants used in various industries. These AI-driven products have become intricately woven into the fabric of daily life.

These products share a distinctive trait: they are easily recognized as AI, highlighting a noticeable disparity between their conversational abilities and human interaction. Yet, ChatGPT distinguishes itself in this realm. It not only conducts complex, multiturn text dialogues but also possesses the ability to generate code, marketing materials, poetry, business strategies, and film scripts. While it is not without flaws and can occasionally err, its robust capabilities position it as one of the AI tools closest to passing the Turing test.

ChatGPT was developed by OpenAI, a nonprofit AI research institute established in San Francisco in 2015. Its founders include entrepreneur Elon Musk, Sam Altman, president of the American startup incubator Y Combinator, and Peter Thiel, cofounder of the global online payment platform PayPal, among others. Based in California, USA, OpenAI has garnered substantial financial support from several prominent Silicon Valley figures, securing an initial investment of up to 1 billion dollars.

The Transformer model, first introduced by the Google Brain team in their seminal 2017 paper “Attention is All You Need,” is now recognized as a pivotal advancement in AI. While it has not entirely supplanted previous frameworks such as recurrent neural networks (RNNs) and convolutional neural networks, it has shown exceptional efficacy in diverse domains including natural language processing (NLP) and computer vision. This model has become a fundamental architecture in these fields. As depicted in Fig. 1.1, OpenAI has extensively pursued NLP research using the Transformer model, which ultimately culminated in the development of ChatGPT.

The timeline starts with the Transformer model in 2017. In 2018, GPT1 was developed with a 5GB training corpus and 100 million parameters using pretrain and finetune methods. In 2019, GPT2 was introduced with a 40GB training corpus and 1.5 billion parameters, utilizing zero shot learning. GPT3 followed in 2020 with a 570GB training corpus and 175 billion parameters, employing in context learning. In 2021 2022, Codex was fine-tuned on GitHub code data from the GPT3 model with 12 billion parameters. In 2022, InstructGPT was fine-tuned based on human feedback instructions. Codedavinci002, focused on coding capabilities, was developed in April 2022. Textdavinci002, focused on textual capabilities, was developed in May 2022. Textdavinci003, aimed at better performance on public NLP tasks, was developed in November 2022. Finally, ChatGPT was introduced in November 2022.

In 2018 OpenAI introduced its first generative pretrained model, GPT-1. This model significantly advanced the language processing capabilities of AI systems by leveraging large-scale unlabeled data for pretraining. It also showcased its ability to generalize across multiple tasks through supervised fine-tuning, marking a notable evolution in AI language models.

In March 2019, OpenAI transitioned from a nonprofit organization to a capped-profit entity, unveiling OpenAI LP and introducing Microsoft as a strategic investor. In July of that year, Microsoft made a significant investment of 1 billion dollars in OpenAI, thereby securing commercialization rights to OpenAI’s technologies and integrating its developed products with Microsoft’s offerings. Concurrently, OpenAI released the GPT-2 model, which utilized a larger dataset called WebText, comprising about 40 GB of text data from 8 million documents. This version increased the number of model parameters to an impressive 1.5 billion, enhancing the model’s accuracy. Furthermore, GPT-2 introduced zero-shot learning capabilities, underscoring the effectiveness of unsupervised learning and the success of pretrained models in downstream NLP tasks.

By June 2020, OpenAI had released the GPT-3 language model and launched its first commercial product, the OpenAI application programming interface (API), signifying the organization’s official venture into commercial operations. In September of the same year, OpenAI granted Microsoft an exclusive license to utilize its GPT-3 model, positioning Microsoft as the first company globally to adopt GPT-3 technology. Unprecedented in scale, the largest version of GPT-3 boasts 175 billion parameters—117 times that of GPT-2. Additionally, GPT-3 introduced in-context learning, a method that uses prompts to adapt to a broader range of downstream tasks and displays various emergent abilities, such as chains of thought.

In 2021 Microsoft deepened its collaboration with OpenAI through an additional investment, propelling their partnership into a new phase. In December 2022, supported by Microsoft, OpenAI introduced ChatGPT, which is built on GPT-3.5. This iteration employs reinforcement learning with human feedback, akin to InstructGPT, to better align with human needs. Remarkably, just 2 months after its release, ChatGPT surpassed 100 million active users globally. At the start of 2023, OpenAI launched the ChatGPT Plus subscription service alongside the GPT-4 model, which possesses multimodal data processing and reasoning capabilities, providing ChatGPT Plus users access to a version of GPT-4 enhanced for NLP tasks.

1.2 The capability level of ChatGPT

In a demonstration video released on March 15, 2023, GPT-4 successfully identified an image of a balloon, as depicted in Fig. 1.2, and predicted the outcome of severing the attached string: “The balloon would fly away.” This demonstration underscores GPT-4 proficiency in interpreting and reasoning with multimodal data, such as images. Possessing an extensive array of parameters, GPT-4 transcends mere surface-level image interpretation to infer deeper significances. This advancement has elicited global astonishment, positioning GPT-4 closer to achieving artificial general intelligence (AGI). Furthermore, this development prompts an ongoing debate regarding the potential of the GPT series to progress towards strong AI, possibly culminating in a capability that surpasses human intelligence.

The text on the right side of the image reads, What would happen if the strings were cut? followed by the balloons would fly away.

Since the 1970s, scholarly definitions of intelligence have emphasized that intelligence should not be restricted to specific tasks. Over the past few decades, the application of AI has expanded significantly across various domains, exemplified by AlphaGo’s mastery in the game of Go, AlphaFold’s protein structure predictions, and the Yolo series’ contributions to computer vision. These advancements, however, represent systems that are intelligent within their respective domains. This has led to the development of the concept of AGI, which is distinctly differentiated from the earlier concept of “weak AI.” Despite its prominence, both the academic and industrial sectors continue to grapple with establishing a universally accepted definition of AGI.

While the primary function of ChatGPT is conversational interaction, its applications extend far beyond simple dialogue. This AI model is capable of writing and debugging computer programs, emulating the writing styles of well-known figures for the creation of commercial brochures, and generating diverse content such as music, television dramas, fairy tales, student essays, and poetry. Moreover, ChatGPT can emulate Linux operating systems, simulate chatrooms, engage in games like tic-tac-toe, and even mimic the operations of an ATM machine.

As illustrated in Fig. 1.3, GPT-4 performance on a range of professional and academic benchmark tests has achieved levels comparable to human capabilities. Notably, GPT-4 placed in the top 10% of candidates in a simulated bar exam and achieved a score of 330 on the graduate record examination (GRE), surpassing the majority of participants. In additional tests, GPT-4 performance exceeded that of other AI systems. These high scores not only reflect enhanced realistic and logical reasoning skills but also signify advanced problem-solving abilities.

The horizontal bar graph is titled as GPT4 VS HUMAN TESTS MAY2023. The y axis lists various tests and exams, while the x axis represents scores from 0 to 100. For the Biology, USA Biolympiad USABO Semifinal Exam 2020 test, the human expert average was 50, while GPT4 scored 99.5. For the SAT test, the human expert average was 50, while GPT4 scored 94.0. For the Wine tasting, Advanced Sommelier theory knowledge test, the human expert average was 50, while GPT4 scored 77.0. For the Economics, George Mason University Economics 321 Midterm by Prof Caplan test, the human expert average was 30, while GPT4 scored 66.0. For the Quantum computing, UT Austin Quantum Information Science exam test, the human expert average was 74, while GPT4 scored 73.0. For the Medicine, Medical Knowledge Self-Assessment Program test, the human/expert average was 50, while GPT4 scored 75.0. For the AI IQ benchmark MMLU test, the human expert average was 35, while GPT4 scored 86.4. For the Commonsense inference HellaSwag test, the human expert average was 96, while GPT4 scored 95.3. For the Knowledge, commonsense WinoGrande test, the human expert average was 94, while GPT4 scored 87.5.

Although GPT-4 has demonstrated proficiency in a range of exams, assisted in translation and programming tasks, and generated innovative ideas, the methodology for evaluating its intelligence level continues to be a subject of intense debate. Given that GPT-4 has been trained on vast datasets encompassing nearly all information available on the internet, it poses a significant challenge to discern whether it has simply memorized content related to specific tasks or has achieved a true understanding of them. This distinction is crucial for accurately assessing its level of intelligence.

From the perspective of evaluating human intelligence, a key attribute is the understanding of multidomain tasks. In this context, the assessment of GPT-4 intelligence can be examined through several critical aspects:

  • • Task performance: This metric is crucial for evaluating GPT-4 capabilities. Notably, GPT-4 has excelled in various tasks such as exams, translation, programming, and generating innovative ideas, often surpassing the average human performance in many professional and academic tests.
  • • Knowledge understanding and application: It is essential to determine whether GPT-4 genuinely comprehends the knowledge it has acquired and how it applies this knowledge across different contexts. A truly intelligent system should demonstrate the ability to understand and flexibly utilize knowledge in diverse scenarios. GPT-4 has shown remarkable proficiency in applying knowledge to a wide range of activities, including writing and debugging programs, emulating writing styles for commercial brochures, composing music, and scripting television shows.
  • • Generalization ability: An intelligent entity’s performance on new tasks and with unseen data—its generalization ability—is a vital measure of its intelligence. Despite potential timeliness issues with its training data, GPT-4 continues to offer innovative solutions to novel problems, showing it does not solely rely on preexisting knowledge and patterns.
  • • Autonomous learning and reasoning: GPT-4 has proven its ability in autonomous learning and reasoning by incorporating new knowledge through prompts and examples. It can independently discover knowledge and formulate reasoning across various tasks and contexts.

Through a comprehensive evaluation of GPT-4 capabilities, encompassing task performance, understanding and application of knowledge, generalization ability, and autonomous learning and reasoning, it is clear that GPT-4 epitomizes the zenith of current AI research. It stands as the most advanced approximation to AGI thus far. The implications of such advancements are profound, promising to significantly alter human lifestyles in the near future. While the extent of these changes may be underestimated by many, the reality is that the future envisioned by these technological advancements is already unfolding.

1.3 The technical evolution of large language models

1.3.1 Symbolism versus connectionism

One of the fundamental capabilities of ChatGPT is NLP. Initially, NLP utilized symbolic representations to interpret the real world, relying on predefined rules for knowledge-based reasoning. This method primarily involved querying a knowledge base using established rules and executing logical operations for reasoning. However, symbolic approaches often struggle to effectively manage the diversity and ambiguity inherent in language. Furthermore, they exhibit limited robustness and generalization abilities. As linguistic phenomena grow in complexity, the quantity of manually crafted rules increases exponentially, complicating the management of language’s diversity and statistical properties.

The advent of machine learning and deep learning heralded a paradigm shift toward a data-driven approach in NLP technologies. During the machine learning phase, it was feasible to identify optimal mappings within a predefined function space based on training data for specific NLP subtasks. However, this approach did not entirely eliminate the need for rule design. Feature engineering emerged as a crucial determinant of outcomes in machine learning applications, yet the predefined function spaces often exhibited limited robustness and generalization capabilities for specific subtasks.

The introduction of deep learning models catalyzed a transformative development in NLP. Initially, NLP text generation predominantly utilized traditional RNNs, long short-term memory networks (LSTMs), or gated recurrent units (GRUs). These models, based on recurrent architectures, are adept at managing serialized NLP tasks due to their sequential processing capabilities.

RNNs incorporate a memory mechanism to address dependencies within sequence data (whether sequential or temporal), integrating the concept of memory into neural networks and learning contextually relevant patterns through training. Nonetheless, RNNs are challenged by their short memory spans. To overcome this limitation, LSTMs and GRUs were developed. Their internal gating mechanisms manage the information flow, preserving essential data in the sequence while eliminating nonessential elements. This allows significant information to persist in long-sequence processing tasks.

Nevertheless, RNN architectures, including LSTM and GRU, encounter several challenges that compromise their suitability as foundational structures for large language models (LLMs):

  1. 1. Insufficient parallel computing capability: The inherent need for RNNs to process each time step sequentially in sequence data impedes their ability to fully leverage the parallel computing capabilities of modern GPUs during training. This limitation significantly affects the efficiency of model training.
  2. 2. Long-range dependency issues: Despite the improvements made by LSTM and GRU over basic RNNs in managing long-range dependencies, they still face difficulties with very long sequences.
  3. 3. Model capacity limitations: LSTMs and GRUs possess relatively limited model capacities, which restrict the scalability of models in training LLMs. This constraint hinders their ability to capture more complex semantic information and construct intricate representations.

1.3.2 Transformer

The GPT series are based the Transformer model, a highly prevalent deep learning framework in NLP tasks, which was introduced by Vaswani et al. in 2017. At the heart of the Transformer model lies the multihead attention mechanism—an enhancement of the self-attention mechanism. This mechanism computes query, key, and value vectors for each input element within its architecture. It determines the relationships (probabilities) between elements via matrix operations and generates the most probable sequence, thereby effectively capturing long-range dependencies within the input sequence.

In contrast to RNNs, the Transformer model’s self-attention mechanism is capable of processing the entire sequence concurrently (refer to Fig. 1.4), maximizing the use of parallel computing capabilities to boost training efficiency. This architecture enables direct capture of dependencies between any positions in the sequence, thus effectively addressing long-range dependency challenges. With its excellent scalability, the Transformer model facilitates extensive model expansion during large-scale training, enhancing its capability to assimilate more information and develop more intricate representations. Consequently, it has become the de facto foundational architecture for applications in NLP, visual processing, and multimodal processing.

On the left, there are two encoders labeled Encoder number 1 and Encoder number 2. Each encoder consists of a Self-Attention Layer followed by Residual Connections and Layer Normalization, then a Feedforward Network and again Residual Connections and Layer Normalization. The input to Encoder number 1 is labeled as x sub 1 end sub Thinking and the input to Encoder number 2 is labeled as x sub 2 end sub Machines. On the right, there are two decoders labeled Decoder number 1 and Decoder number 2. Each decoder consists of a Self-Attention Layer, followed by Residual Connections and Layer Normalization, then an Encoder-Decoder Attention layer, followed by Residual Connections and Layer Normalization, then a Feedforward Network and again Residual Connections and Layer Normalization. The output of Decoder number 2 goes through a Linear Layer and then a Softmax layer.

It is essential to highlight that while the original Transformer model incorporates an encoder-decoder architecture, GPT exclusively employs the decoder component (refer to Fig. 1.5). Both the encoder and decoder consist of N identical layers, each stacked sequentially. The source and target sequences, which may be images or text, are initially processed through an embedding layer to standardize the data to the same dimensionality. Each encoder layer features a multihead attention module (with Q, K, V inputs) and a feedforward network module. The decoder layer commences with a multihead attention module, succeeded by another multihead attention module that interacts with the outputs from the encoder stack (i.e., the queries Q originate from the decoder, while the values V and keys K are derived from the encoder), and concludes with a feedforward network module. The output stage is processed through a softmax classifier, which determines the category or vocabulary with the highest probability.

The GPT model includes 12 transformer layers, a word embedding dimension of 768 and 117 million parameters. It features masked multi-head attention, layer normalization and a feedforward network. The GPT-2 model has 48 transformer layers, a word embedding dimension of 1600 and 1.5 billion parameters. It includes masked multi-head attention, layer normalization, residual connections and a feedforward network. The GPT-3 model consists of 96 transformer layers, a word embedding dimension of 12888 and 175 billion parameters. It also features masked multi-head attention, layer normalization, residual connections and a feedforward network. Each model uses positional encoding for text input and outputs text predictions.

1.3.3 Unsupervised pretraining

All models in the GPT series undergo an unsupervised pretraining phase; however, they differ markedly in model size and the volume of textual data utilized. This phase leverages extensive corpus statistics to capture either unidirectional or bidirectional contextual information, which is sensitive to the relative relationships of data before and after in the sequence. As a generative model, GPT aims to predict the next word in a given context, consistently striving to generate text that is most relevant and coherent with the context, thereby maximizing the conditional probability P(target|context). As illustrated in Fig. 1.6, during the pretraining phase, GPT employs a unidirectional (masked self-attention) language model, predicting solely based on the left-side context without utilizing right-side context information.

On the left side, there is a box labeled GPT Series containing multiple decoders stacked from 1 to N. On the right side, there is a representation of masked self-attention. The masked self-attention mechanism is depicted with an arrow pointing upwards from a circle, indicating the focus on the left-side context without utilizing right-side context information. Below the masked self-attention, there are several blocks arranged horizontally.

The pretraining mechanism of the GPT model acquires linguistic and world knowledge from vast text corpora. Linguistic knowledge includes lexical, morphological, syntactic, and semantic aspects, which facilitate the understanding of natural language by both humans and machines. World knowledge encompasses factual knowledge (events occurring in the real world) and common-sense knowledge. Transformer-based language models are capable of learning basic linguistic knowledge from corpora ranging from tens of millions to billions of examples. However, acquiring factual knowledge requires significantly larger-scale training data. Unlike the relatively stable and finite linguistic knowledge, factual knowledge is not only abundant but also continuously evolving. Current research indicates that as training data volumes increase, the performance of pretrained models on various downstream tasks consistently improves, suggesting that these models primarily acquire world knowledge from the incremental training data. This expansion in training data enables models to more effectively address challenges in NLP tasks, thereby enhancing their performance and accuracy across different application scenarios.

1.3.4 Supervised fine-tuning

Although the pretraining phase endows GPT models with a broad spectrum of linguistic and world knowledge, achieving exemplary performance on specific tasks often necessitates supervised fine-tuning. In this phase, the model utilizes a labeled dataset to refine its parameters via supervised learning, thereby better aligning with specific task requirements. Typically, for the GPT series, this process is applied to NLP tasks such as question answering, dialogue generation, and sentiment analysis. However, not all GPT models are subjected to supervised fine-tuning due to the complexities and high costs associated with constructing training datasets for downstream tasks. Moreover, as the number of model parameters increases, the cost of training escalates exponentially. In the GPT series, while models like GPT-1 and GPT-3.5 and above have undergone supervised fine-tuning with a limited number of samples, their core competencies primarily derive from the extensive pretraining phase. These models are further adapted to more downstream tasks through contextual learning.

1.3.5 Human feedback reinforcement learning

Supervised fine-tuning can improve model performance on specific tasks, yet challenges may persist. To further refine the model, versions GPT-3.5 and above have incorporated human feedback reinforcement learning (HFRL). As depicted in Fig. 1.7, this process involves evaluating the responses generated by the model using human reviewers, whose assessments serve as reinforcement learning signals to enhance model performance. Consequently, ChatGPT can progressively improve the quality of its responses through interactions with humans.

The components are: Reinforcement Learning Algorithm, Reward Prediction and Environment. The Reinforcement Learning Algorithm generates a Predicted Reward which is sent to Reward Prediction. Human Feedback is provided to Reward Prediction. The Reward Prediction sends Observation to the Reinforcement Learning Algorithm. The Reinforcement Learning Algorithm sends Action to the Environment. The Environment sends Observation back to the Reinforcement Learning Algorithm.

Specifically, questions are randomly selected from a dataset, and human annotators provide high-quality answers, which are then utilized to fine-tune the GPT model. Through this supervised learning phase, the model is trained to produce answers that more accurately meet human expectations. HFRL comprises three steps: initial supervised fine-tuning; subsequent training of a reward model (RM), where human annotators rank the outputs, and these rankings are employed to train the RM; and finally, using the trained RM to refine the strategy through reinforcement learning, updating the parameters of the pretrained model based on feedback from the RM. By iteratively repeating the last two steps, a higher-quality model is developed, better aligning the generated content with human requirements.

1.4 The technology stack of large language model

LLMs such as ChatGPT are rapidly evolving into essential infrastructural elements. They encompass a varied technological stack that incorporates features like containerization, performance monitoring, business intelligence, event processing, cloud services, microservices, and analytical tools. This technological stack can be systematically categorized into five distinct layers, ascending from the foundational level as depicted in Fig. 1.8.

  1. 1. Infrastructure layer: This layer is fundamental in providing the necessary infrastructure for the execution of both training and inference tasks, which includes the use of public clouds, private clouds, and specialized computational hardware. At this level, the efficiency of AI-specific hardware, such as GPUs and tensor processing units, often becomes a critical bottleneck. The computational efforts for most extensive language models predominantly occur on these devices within cloud environments, serving both model providers and research laboratories for training purposes, as well as businesses engaged in model deployment or fine-tuning. Since the transition from CPUs to GPUs as the primary computing units in AI, the field has confronted renewed challenges associated with the immense demands for computational power, marking a significant challenge over the past decade.
  2. 2. Framework layer: This layer includes advanced deep learning and distributed training frameworks that are essential for the training and deployment of models. Prominent frameworks such as PyTorch and TensorFlow, along with distributed training systems like Pathway, Megatron-LM, and DeepSpeed Zero, fall within this category.
  3. 3. Model layer: This layer comprises a variety of open-source and proprietary models, which typically support higher layers, such as the application layer or middleware, through APIs.
  4. 4. Middleware layer: This layer involves the encapsulation of interfaces for LLMs, optimization prompts, integration of external knowledge, and interfaces for additional third-party tools. It provides critical middleware services equipped with long-term memory and proxy capabilities, which meet diverse workflow requirements from the user layer. This layer simplifies workflow decomposition, tool invocation, and the connection between the application and model layers, thereby easing the interaction between developers and models and reducing barriers to the practical utilization of LLMs.
  5. 5. Application layer: This final layer integrates AI models into end-user applications. Many businesses incorporate LLMs into their client-facing applications or search engines, either via the middleware layer or through direct model invocation. This integration empowers products with the advanced collaborative and generative capabilities of LLMs, exemplified by Microsoft’s New Bing.

The layers are as follows: Application Layer, Middleware Layer, Model Layer, Framework Layer and Infrastructure Layer. The Application Layer includes Online Platform Application and Client Application. Online Platform Application refers to LLM-type applications based on public network platforms, single-platform applications with huge traffic volumes. Client Application refers to LLM applications providing client interaction. The Middleware Layer includes Middleware Framework, which refers to middleware frameworks such as LangChain and HuggingGPT. The Model Layer includes Proprietary Pre-trained Models, Model Hub and Open-source Project. Proprietary Pre-trained Models refer to commercialized pre-trained models accessible through API calls. Model Hub refers to open-source model sharing platform. Open-source Project refers to pre-trained models with open-source code and weights, generally available for free use. The Framework Layer includes Training Framework, which refers to deep learning frameworks such as PyTorch and TensorFlow, as well as distributed training frameworks like Pathway, Megatron-LM and DeepSpeed Zero. The Infrastructure Layer includes Cloud Platform and Computing Hardware and Chips. Cloud Platform refers to open computing platforms for training or cloud deployment, such as Azure, AWS, GCP, Alibaba Cloud, etc., as well as private clouds. Computing Hardware and Chips refer to computing acceleration chips and storage chips for LLM training and deployment, including CPU, GPU, DSA (e.g., TPU), memory, NVMe, Infiniband.

Moreover, some proprietary models, such as OpenAI’s ChatGPT, are directly embedded into upper-layer applications. Other organizations, motivated by data privacy or specific business needs, may adapt open-source large models to develop bespoke solutions tailored to unique industry requirements, thereby offering customized services to end users.

1.5 The impact of large language models

The success of ChatGPT has had profound implications for NLP and related fields. Looking forward, technological development trends in this area may exhibit the following characteristics:

  1. 1. The independent research significance of NLP subfields may gradually diminish. As the scale of LLMs expands, the performance of various subtasks within the NLP domain is expected to improve significantly. Traditional fine-tuning methods based on small models may gradually be replaced by approaches leveraging large models and content learning techniques, such as prompting. Many issues previously considered “domain-specific” may primarily stem from a lack of domain knowledge. With sufficient domain knowledge, these problems can potentially be resolved. The path to achieving AGI might be more straightforward than anticipated: it may simply require providing LLMs with more domain-specific data to enable self-learning.
  2. 2. LLMs are anticipated to become multimodal and serve as general human–computer interfaces. As illustrated in Fig. 1.9, the application domains of LLMs are expected to extend beyond NLP to include image processing, video, and speech tasks. The demonstration during the release of GPT-4 has already showcased the application scenarios of multimodal LLMs. These models have the potential to evolve into general human–computer interfaces supporting multimodal input and output. This trend may start with operating systems and commonly used software tools, gradually permeating software across various industries, ultimately achieving widespread application.
  3. 3. The training and deployment of large models with low computational power will become a focal point of research and application. Given the substantial financial investment required to develop LLMs, only a few major enterprises are likely to continue releasing LLMs that can compete with ChatGPT. Many companies operating in specific vertical domains may attempt to customize open-source versions by leveraging domain-specific data to establish their own competitive “moats.” However, the capabilities of LLMs generally become evident only after reaching a certain scale threshold. Thus, achieving ideal results through small-scale models with domain data fine-tuning may not be effective. Consequently, low computational power training and deployment of large models will inevitably become a prominent area of research and application.
  4. 4. Middleware based on LLMs will integrate enterprise workflows. Many small and medium-sized enterprises may utilize middleware based on LLMs (see Fig. 1.10) to integrate their workflows, thereby enhancing their competitiveness, despite potential compromises to the security of domain data. Large models like ChatGPT not only demonstrate efficiency in processing and generating multimodal data but also exhibit complex reasoning capabilities and robustness to out-of-distribution events. This enables LLMs to function as intelligent agents that integrate existing enterprise workflows, thereby automating processes and improving efficiency. This could lead to a concerning spiral effect: the more domain data is monopolized by LLMs, the faster their capabilities will iterate. Consequently, other small and medium-sized enterprises, to avoid falling behind in the competition, will increasingly rely on the efficiency improvements brought about by integrating their workflows with middleware based on LLMs.

At the top, there is a box labeled Summarize the content of the following article connected to a General-Purpose interface box. Below this, there are three boxes labeled Language, Vision and Multilingual. Below these, there are various examples, a picture of a robot with the question How many robots are in the picture? and the answer One, another picture of multiple robots with the same question and the answer Three. There is also a picture of a flower with the question Q: What is this? and the answer Plum blossom, labeled as Visual Question Answering. Other examples include Determine the emotion: Great ball skills with the answer Positive labeled as Instruction-guided Fine-tuning Model, New CPU, choose AMD, GPU, choose... What is the topic? with the answer Electronics labeled as Classification and Répondre aux questions en anglais: What fruit is good to eat when you are sick? with the answer Bananas labeled as Cross Lingual Question Answering. On the right side, there is a section labeled Multi turn Dialogue with questions and answers about MetaLM, general interface, audio, AGI, pre-training task and semi-causal language modeling.

The process starts with Setting Objectives which leads to Definition and Sequencing of Tasks. If the Objective Not Achieved, it loops back to Definition and Sequencing of Tasks. Otherwise, it proceeds to Task Execution. Task Execution is followed by Evaluation. If the evaluation is successful, the process ends. If not, it loops back to Definition and Sequencing of Tasks. The Task Execution is supported by AI Large Pre-trained Language Model (GPT-4), Network, and Other Tools.

1.6 The challenges of training or deploying large models

1.6.1 Computational power

Before the emergence of LLMs like GPT-4, there were few applications that required such powerful GPU computing capabilities. The computational cost of these LLMs mainly involves two aspects: the initial training of the model and subsequent operational maintenance. For companies planning to train or deploy LLMs like GPT-4, their demand for computing power is mainly reflected in the following two stages:

  1. 1. Pretraining and fine-tuning stage: In this stage, the model undergoes pretraining from scratch using vast amounts of general data, simultaneously validating the effectiveness of the pretrained model to establish its foundational knowledge. Subsequently, the model is fine-tuned with specific data tailored to particular commercial applications or other scenarios to enhance response accuracy. While specific data for GPT-4 at this stage has not been disclosed, estimates can be made based on other LLMs. For instance, the Meta LLaMA model with 65 billion parameters was trained on 1.4 trillion tokens over approximately 21 days using 2048 Nvidia A100 GPUs, incurring a training cost of about 1 million GPU hours.
    For a model like GPT-3, with 175 billion parameters, the training requires 3.14E23 FLOPS. Assuming the use of NVIDIA 80GB A100 GPUs (theoretical performance of 312 TFLOPS) and employing tensor parallelism and pipeline parallelism technologies to achieve over 50% utilization, the estimated requirement is 758 A100 GPUs for a training duration of 1 month. Although specific parameters for ChatGPT 3.5 and 4.0 have not been disclosed, they are presumed to be on a similar scale to GPT-3, with 175 billion parameters. Based on available data from the ChatGPT website, the memory usage is estimated to be between 350 and 500 GB. However, training might require the computational power of over 1000 A100 GPUs to achieve results within an acceptable timeframe. It is also reported that Microsoft and OpenAI constructed a supercomputer with over 10,000 GPUs for training GPT-3, with a single training session costing around $5 million.
  2. 2. Inference and deployment stage: Once fine-tuned, the LLM can be deployed in real-world production environments. Compared with the training stage, the computational demand during the deployment stage is relatively lower, but the scale is significantly larger. For extensive online interactions, the deployment stage incurs substantial server and chip costs, potentially surpassing those of the training stage. This is because numerous servers are needed in a cluster to provide parallel network services, primarily involving extensive matrix computations and storage management. In specific scenarios, such as edge applications, there may be unique requirements for hardware cost-effectiveness and response latency, where traditional GPUs might not suffice.

According to a recent interview with OpenAI CEO Sam Altman, we can make the following assumptions: If OpenAI adopts the dense model architecture of GPT-3, with 175 billion parameters, a hidden layer dimension of 16k, a sequence length of 4k, an average token count of 2k per response, and 15 responses per user for 13 million daily active users, considering 50% hardware utilization due to idle times and a cost of $1 per GPU per hour (with performance twice that of FasterTransformer), the daily computational hardware cost for ChatGPT is approximately $690,000. OpenAI would require about 3617 HGX A100 servers (totaling 28,936 GPUs) to provide ChatGPT services, with each query costing around 0.36 cents.

1.6.2 Data

The training of LLMs relies on vast amounts of data, which enable them to learn and master the various knowledge encapsulated in natural language. At the same time, considering the quality of LLMs, these data must be effectively cleaned, annotated, organized, and stored. Therefore, the cost of the infrastructure, tools, and data engineers required for these tasks, as well as data management and processing, can rapidly increase.

For example, the LLaMA model utilized a training dataset totaling 4.6TB, comprising 1.4 trillion tokens. As illustrated in Fig. 1.11, the pretraining text data for GPT-3 amounted to a staggering 45TB. These massive data volumes highlight the resources needed for model training and underscore the importance of data quality. It has been reported that the world’s largest Chinese corpus, WuDaoCorpora, stores 3TB of Chinese text data, with 200GB made publicly available.

The table consists of three main sections: Pre-trained Dataset (Basic Training), Training Dataset for Supervised Fine-Tuning (Cognitive/Expressive Training) and Training Dataset for PPO (Domain Generalization). The Pre-trained Dataset section has four columns: Dataset, Quantity (Tokens), Weights in Mixed Training and Epochs Required for Training 300B Tokens. It reads as follows: Row 1. Common Crawl (filtered): 410 billion, 60 percent, 0.44. Row 2. WebText2: 19 billion, 22 percent, 2.9. Row 3. Books1: 12 billion, 8 percent, 1.9. Row 4. Books2: 55 billion, 8 percent, 0.43. Row 5. Wikipedia: 3 billion, 3 percent, 3.4. The Training Dataset for Supervised Fine-Tuning section has three columns: Category, Corpus Source and Count. It reads as follows: Row 1. Training Dataset: Annotator, 11295. Row 2. Training Dataset: Client, 1430. The Training Dataset for PPO section has three columns: Category, Corpus Source and Count. It reads as follows: Row 1. Training Dataset: Annotator, 11295.

For training a Chinese version of GPT-3.5, only a few vertical industry companies, apart from giants in the search and social sectors, have access to sufficient data. However, a significant legal and ethical issue arises regarding whether the text data acquired during business operations can be used for model training. Even Codex, which was trained on open-source data from GitHub, faced considerable criticism. Notably, the multimodal training dataset for GPT-4 comprises both images and text, potentially including various types such as chart reasoning, physics exams, image understanding, paper summarization, and comic text.

1.6.3 Engineering

The development of LLMs relies on specialized researchers and engineers who are responsible for creating the architecture and correctly configuring the training process. As illustrated in Fig. 1.12, the architecture of the model determines how it learns and generates text. Designing, implementing, and managing these architectures require expertise in various fields of computer science. The salaries for engineers and researchers responsible for publishing and delivering cutting-edge research results often reach hundreds of thousands of dollars. It is important to note that the skill set required for developing LLMs may differ significantly from the traditional skill set of machine learning engineers, as depicted in Fig. 1.2.

The diagram illustrates various components of a model architecture. The components are Configuration, Data Collection, Data Validation, Machine Resource Management, Service Infrastructure, Supervision, Feature Extraction, Process Management Tools, Analytical Tools and ML Code.

Moreover, training LLMs imposes high standards on engineering practices. This includes data cleaning, the engineering implementation of large-scale distributed training, and techniques for maintaining training stability with a large number of parameters and data volumes.

1.7 The limitations of large language models

While LLMs like GPT-4 have demonstrated significant capabilities in context-based dialogue, programming, and even in understanding images and analyzing data graphs, they are still far from achieving AGI. These models face several critical limitations:

Authenticity and accuracy: LLMs often lack robust guarantees of authenticity and accuracy. For example, in areas not extensively covered during training, GPT-4 may not exhibit human-like common sense or robust logical reasoning, sometimes generating incorrect or misleading answers. Despite improvements over previous models, GPT-4 can still produce inappropriate suggestions or inaccurate data, making it unsuitable for use in high-risk sectors like legal and medical fields.

Interpretability: The internal workings of LLMs like GPT-4 are complex and not fully transparent, making it challenging to guarantee the safety of their outputs. While there have been advancements in reducing error rates, the potential for harmful outputs poses a risk, potentially leading to legal issues for developers and users.

Social and ethical implications: These models can inadvertently generate biased, false, or offensive content due to their “black box” nature. Despite efforts to mitigate risks, there remains a possibility for misuse in creating misleading information or harmful content, such as fake news or propaganda.

Privacy concerns: LLMs are trained on vast amounts of data, some of which may include sensitive personal information. This raises concerns about privacy breaches, as the model could inadvertently learn and reproduce private data.

1.8 Summary

This chapter conducts a thorough analysis and discussion on the evolutionary trajectory of ChatGPT AI, highlighting its significant influence. Tracing the development from the initial GPT-1 and GPT-2 models to the more sophisticated GPT-3 and GPT-4, it emphasizes the pivotal role each version has played in propelling the field of AI forward.

The chapter explores the functional breadth of ChatGPT, detailing its proficiency in language comprehension and generation, the accuracy of its sentiment analysis, and its adeptness at answering questions and crafting poetry. It also offers a detailed examination of the shift from early NLP technologies to contemporary Transformer models, with a focus on the architectural design of large-scale models.

Further, the text delves into the extensive impact of LLMs across various sectors including commercial, academic, and policy realms, while also addressing significant concerns such as the immense data and computational demands required for these models. Critical challenges such as model hallucinations, biases, interpretability issues, and privacy concerns are also discussed.

In summary, this chapter provides a holistic understanding of the operational mechanisms, potential, and challenges associated with ChatGPT and similar LLMs. Future chapters will further dissect the principles underlying ChatGPT to better comprehend its influence and prospective advancements.

Chapter 2 In-depth understanding of the transformer model

Abstract

This chapter offers a comprehensive exploration of the Transformer model, a pivotal innovation in deep learning, particularly in natural language processing. The chapter begins by outlining the model’s core components, including the self-attention mechanism, multihead attention, feedforward neural networks (FNNs), residual connections, layer normalization, and positional encoding. It delves into the self-attention mechanism’s ability to capture long-range dependencies by computing attention weights among input elements. Multihead attention is discussed as an enhancement that enables simultaneous processing of multiple aspects of input sequences. The chapter also covers the role of FNNs in capturing nonlinear features, the importance of residual connections for gradient flow, and layer normalization for training stability. Positional encoding is highlighted as crucial for incorporating sequence order information. Finally, training and optimization techniques such as loss functions, optimizers, and learning rate strategies are discussed to enhance model performance.

Keywords

Transformer model; self-attention mechanism; multihead attention; feedforward neural networks; residual connections; layer normalization; positional encoding; natural language processing (NLP); training optimization

The Transformer model represents a significant shift in deep learning paradigms, primarily through its utilization of the self-attention mechanism. This innovation has fundamentally altered the conventional approaches typically associated with recurrent neural networks (RNNs) and convolutional neural networks, thereby reshaping the landscape of natural language processing (NLP). Currently, the Transformer stands as a cornerstone in NLP research and development, underpinning a variety of prominent models including bidirectional encoder representations from transformers (BERT) and generative pre-trained transformer (GPT). This chapter will provide a comprehensive examination of the Transformer model’s essential components.

2.1 Introduction to the transformer model

As depicted in Fig. 2.1, the fundamental components of the Transformer model encompass the multihead attention mechanism, feedforward neural networks (FNNs), residual connections coupled with layer normalization, and positional encoding.

On the left, there is a sequence of operations starting with Input Embedding followed by Positional Encoding and then multiple layers of Add and Norm with Multi-Head Attention and Feed Forward blocks, repeated N times. On the right, there is a similar structure starting with Output Embedding followed by Positional Encoding and multiple layers of Add and Norm with Masked Multi-Head Attention, Multi-Head Attention, and Feed Forward blocks, also repeated N times. The output probabilities are obtained through a Linear layer followed by Softmax. The diagram also zooms into the Multi-Head Attention mechanism, showing Scaled Dot-Product Attention with inputs V, K, and Q passing through Linear layers, concatenated and then processed through another Linear layer. Another zoom-in shows the Scaled Dot-Product Attention process, where Q and K are multiplied (MatMul), scaled, optionally masked, passed through SoftMax and then multiplied with V (MatMul).

Central to the Transformer architecture is the self-attention mechanism, which assesses relationships between each element of the input sequence and every other element. Specifically, it generates “key,” “query,” and “value” representations for each input element, employing these to execute a weighted summation that produces the output sequence. An elaboration of self-attention, the multihead attention mechanism, allows the model to simultaneously address various contexts within the input sequence. It partitions the input into multiple subspaces, each processed through independent self-attention calculations. The concatenated results of these subspaces formulate the composite output sequence.

Additionally, the FNN is a pivotal element within the Transformer. Each FNN within the Transformer is composed of two linear layers with an intervening activation function. Outputs from self-attention undergo processing through the first linear layer, proceed through the activation function, and continue through the second linear layer, a sequence repeated across every Transformer layer.

To accelerate training and improve model efficacy, the Transformer architecture incorporates residual connections and layer normalization. Residual connections facilitate the integration of the input sequence by adding it element wise to the outputs of the self-attention and FNNs, thus forming a modified sequence. Subsequent layer normalization of this sequence across its dimensions aids in hastening the training process.

The inherent design of the self-attention mechanism does not capture the sequential order of elements, necessitating the introduction of positional encoding within the Transformer model. This encoding utilizes sine and cosine functions to create unique positional encodings for each element, which are subsequently added to the elements of the input sequence, thereby embedding information about the sequence order.

For training, the Transformer predominantly employs the maximum likelihood estimation method, aiming to optimize model parameters by minimizing the cross-entropy loss. To further enhance the training speed, the Transformer commonly uses the Adam optimizer, alongside techniques such as learning rate warmup and gradient clipping.

2.2 Self-attention mechanism

The conceptual foundation of the attention mechanism draws inspiration from human visual and cognitive processes. When assimilating visual or linguistic data, individuals tend to concentrate on specific segments of the input, disregarding extraneous details. This selective focus mitigates computational and cognitive loads, thereby bolstering the efficiency and accuracy of information processing.

2.2.1 The calculation process of self-attention

Self-attention, a specialized variant of the attention mechanism, facilitates interactions among all elements within an input sequence to ascertain their respective attention weights. This capability allows the self-attention mechanism to incorporate all elements when processing sequential data. The principal rationale of self-attention is to allocate a weight to each sequence element, dictating the model’s focus intensity on other elements during the processing of a specific one. A significant advantage of self-attention is its proficiency in capturing long-range dependencies within the sequence, thus augmenting the model’s representational capacity. In the Transformer, the self-attention computation involves several steps:

Initially, the input sequence—comprising elements like words, characters, or other types—is converted into vector representations via an embedding layer. Subsequently, for each element, vectors known as queries, keys, and values are derived typically through multiplication with distinct weight matrices.

As illustrated in Fig. 2.2, an input sequence denoted as xi=1,2,3 is transformed through an embedding layer to yield token embeddings ei=1,2,3. Three transformation matrices, WQ, WK, and WV, are employed to convert these embeddings into vectors qi=1,2,3, ki=1,2,3, and vi=1,2,3, respectively, representing the tokens’ semantic features in a latent space.

It includes embeddings, multiplications and softmax operations. At the bottom, there are three input vectors labeled x sub 1 end sub, x sub 2 end sub and x sub 3 end sub, which are transformed into embeddings e sub 1 end sub, e sub 2 end sub and e sub 3 end sub, respectively. These embeddings are then processed through weight matrices W superscript Q end superscript, W superscript K end superscript and W superscript V end superscript to produce q sub 1 end sub, k sub 1 end sub, v sub 1 end sub, q sub 2 end sub, k sub 2 end sub, v sub 2 end sub, q sub 3 end sub, k sub 3 end sub and v sub 3 end sub. The attention scores at sub 11 end sub, at sub 12 end sub and at sub 13 end sub are computed using these vectors. These scores are then passed through a softmax layer to produce st sub 11 end sub, st sub 12 end sub and st sub 13 end sub, which are multiplied by wt sub 11 end sub, wt sub 12 end sub and wt sub 13 end sub, respectively. The results are summed and added to b sub 1 end sub. The diagram includes arrows indicating the flow of operations: embedding, multiplication and softmax.

Subsequently, vector q1 is dot-multiplied with each ki=1,2,3 to compute initial attention scores, at1i. Notably, at1i is scalar. These scores are normalized using a softmax function to derive st1i. Each st1i then multiplies the corresponding value vectors vi=1,2,3 to generate vectors wt1i. Softmax normalization of the dot products, adjusted by the square root of the vector dimension, ensures that the sum of attention weights is unity, with individual weights ranging from 0 to 1. The summation of all wt1i vectors yields the output vector b1, embodying the self-attention mechanism’s essence: each input token’s output vector is computed similarly. This procedure is replicated across the input sequence to generate all output vectors b2, b3. Unlike RNNs, the computation of self-attention does not rely on prior hidden states but includes positional information in the embeddings, allowing for the model to recognize the relative positions of tokens and facilitating the parallelization of computations.

As depicted in Fig. 2.3, by concatenating the vectors e1,2,3 into matrix “E,” matrix-based self-attention operations are enabled. Multiplying “E” by the parameter matrices WQ, WK, and WV results in matrices “Q,” “K,” and “V.”

The process starts with an input matrix x with dimensions n by d, where n is the number of tokens and d is the embedding size. The input x is multiplied by three weight matrices W sub q superscript T end sub, W sub k superscript T end sub and W sub v superscript T end sub to produce matrices Q, K and V, respectively. The dimensions of W sub q superscript T end sub and W sub k superscript T end sub are d by d sub q end sub and the dimension of W sub v superscript T end sub is d by d sub v end sub. The resulting matrices Q and K have dimensions n by d sub q end sub and V has dimensions n by d sub v end sub. The matrix Q is then multiplied by the transpose of matrix K (K superscript T end superscript) to produce an attention matrix with dimensions n by n. This attention matrix is passed through a softmax function to produce matrix A with dimensions n by n. Finally, matrix A is multiplied by matrix V to produce the output matrix Z with dimensions n by d sub v end sub.

The attention score matrix is calculated by multiplying “Q” with the transpose of “K.” This matrix is then row-normalized using softmax to produce a normalized attention score matrix “A,” where each row sums to one. This matrix “A” of size n*n is multiplied with “V” to produce the final matrix “Z,” which dimensions ndv indicate “n” tokens, each with a value vector of length dv. The self-attention formula is expressed as

ATQ,K,V=softmaxQKTdkV. (2.1)

(2.1)

Here, “Q,” “K,” “V” are hidden state matrices derived by multiplying the input matrix “X” with three parameter matrices, and dk represents the number of columns in matrix Wk.

The division by dk addresses potential vanishing gradient issues. When dkd_kdk is large, the variance in the attention scores can be significant, leading to diminished gradients as extreme values are squashed by the softmax function. Normalizing by 1dk reduces this variance from dk to 1, thereby stabilizing the gradients.

2.2.2 The essence of the self-attention mechanism

The matrices “Q,” “K,” and “V” represent linear transformations of the input matrix “X” and can be conceptualized as the semantic representations of “X” in a latent space. For simplification, these matrices may collectively be referred to as “X.” Omitting the normalization factor 1dk leads to a modified version of Eq. (2.1), expressed as

AT(Q,K,V)=softmax(XXT)X. (2.2)

(2.2)

In this formulation, XXT symbolizes the product of matrix “X” and its transpose, an operation in vector space representing the inner products between vectors. This reflects the similarity of the input vectors. The geometric interpretation of an inner product between two vectors is indicative of the angle between them and the projection of one vector onto another. Consequently, the higher the similarity between two vectors, the greater the projection value; conversely, when two vectors are perpendicular, indicating linear independence, their inner product equates to zero.

As demonstrated in Fig. 2.4, consider an input sequence x1 represented by elements (x1,x2,x3). Should the embedding layer utilize one-hot encoding, the input matrix “X” assumes a specific configuration. The product of this matrix with its transpose effectively serves as an attention weight matrix. This configuration facilitates the straightforward computation of attention weights, deriving from the similarities and dissimilarities among various input elements, thereby simplifying and enhancing the efficiency of the computational process within the attention mechanism.

The first matrix X has elements: row x1 (1, 0, 1, 0), row x2 (0, 1, 0, 1) and row x3 (0, 1, 1, 0). The second matrix X superscript T end superscript has elements: row x1 (1, 0, 0, 0), row x2 (0, 1, 1, 1) and row x3 (1, 0, 1, 0). The result of the multiplication is the Attention Matrix with elements: row x1 (2, 0, 1), row x2 (0, 2, 1) and row x3 (1, 1, 2). The softmax operation is then applied to the Attention Matrix, resulting in a matrix with elements: row x1 (0.67, 0.09, 0.24), row x2 (0.09, 0.67, 0.24) and row x3 (0.21, 0.21, 0.58).

The attention weight matrix exemplifies the essence of inner product calculations, which is to gauge similarity. For instance, consider the first row, which calculates the similarity of the token x1 with all other tokens in the sequence. If tokens A and B cooccur frequently, their similarity is generally high. In this example, the similarity of x1 with itself and with the token x3 is notably high, suggesting a closer relationship between x1 and these two tokens compared to x2.

Subsequently, these results are normalized using the softmax function to produce a normalized attention weight matrix. As depicted in Fig. 2.5, this matrix, once normalized, functions as a coefficient matrix. This matrix is then right-multiplied by the input matrix X, resulting in Xˆ. This operation involves multiplying each row of the coefficient matrix by each column of X, effectively computing the values for each row of the output matrix Xˆ. Each row in Xˆ represents the weighted word embedding of the corresponding token, influenced by the attention weights.

The table consists of three matrices: alpha, X and X hat. It reads as follows: Matrix alpha. Row 1. x sub 1: 0.67, 0.09, 0.24. Row 2. x sub 2: 0.09, 0.67, 0.24. Row 3. x sub 3: 0.21, 0.21, 0.58. Matrix X. Row 1. x sub 1: 1, 0, 1, 0. Row 2. x sub 2: 0, 1, 0, 1. Row 3. x sub 3: 0, 1, 1, 0. Matrix X hat. Row 1. x sub 1: 0.67, 0.33, 0.91, 0.09. Row 2. x sub 2: 0.09, 0.91, 0.33, 0.67. Row 3. x sub 3: 0.21, 0.79, 0.79, 0.21.

In summary, the self-attention mechanism, given an input matrix X, yields an output matrix Xˆ by computing attention weights among the input elements. This process involves calculating dot products between the query vectors and the key vectors to assess the similarity between two elements. These attention weights are subsequently utilized to execute a weighted summation of the value vectors, generating an output vector for each input element. These output vectors may either serve as inputs to subsequent layers or, in combination with other layers, contribute to the model’s overall output.

As illustrated in Fig. 2.6, the matrices “Q,” “K,” and “V” are all derived from linear transformations of the input matrix X. In theory, these transformations could be accomplished with a single matrix to achieve attention weighting. Nonetheless, utilizing three distinct matrices to generate query, key, and value vectors is instrumental in the attention mechanism. The query vector acts as the “question” posed by the current element during attention weight computation, evaluating its focus on other elements by comparing with their key vectors. The key vector serves as the “answer” for each element in the input sequence, with attention weights calculated based on the similarity between query and key vectors. The value vector carries the actual information of each element in the sequence. These attention weights are then used to aggregate the value vectors, producing the final output vector.

In the first example, matrix X is multiplied by matrix WQ to result in matrix Q. In the second example, matrix X is multiplied by matrix WK to result in matrix K. In the third example, matrix X is multiplied by matrix WV to result in matrix V.

2.2.3 The advantages and limitations of the self-attention mechanism

Self-attention is a key component of the Transformer model, focusing on calculating attention weights among elements within the input sequence to highlight essential information. Unlike traditional sequence models like RNNs and long short-term memory networks (LSTMs), which process sequences element by element, the attention mechanism can directly evaluate the interactions between any pair of elements, enhancing its ability to handle long-range dependencies. The visibility of attention weights also adds a layer of interpretability: by examining these weights, one can discern the focal points of the model when dealing with specific elements, aiding in model analysis, debugging, and optimization. Furthermore, self-attention can be computed in parallel, making it highly effective on parallel processing units like GPUs. This contrasts sharply with RNNs and LSTMs, whose sequential operations do not fully leverage parallel hardware capabilities.

Self-attention also encounters certain limitations, particularly with processing long sequences. While it excels at capturing long-distance dependencies, the computational and memory demands can become overwhelming with very long sequences. To mitigate this, researchers have developed various optimization techniques such as hierarchical, sparse, and low-rank attention, which help reduce both computational load and memory usage. Additionally, self-attention models typically require substantial amounts of training data to perform optimally, which can restrict their effectiveness in scenarios with limited data or in specialized domains.

2.3 Multihead attention mechanism

The multihead attention mechanism is a sophisticated expansion of the self-attention system, designed to let a model simultaneously process and understand multiple aspects of the information presented in an input sequence. This capability allows for the creation of more nuanced and varied interpretations, significantly boosting the model’s effectiveness.

With its ability to grasp a range of information, the multihead attention mechanism uses distinct “attention heads” to focus on different facets of the input. For instance, in translating languages, one head might analyze grammar, while another delves into the meanings behind words. This approach not only yields translations that are both accurate and fluid but also enriches the model’s understanding.

Furthermore, by adding more attention heads, the mechanism does not just enhance the model’s capacity for dealing with complex tasks—it also increases its overall expressiveness without needing more layers. In addition, the structure of multihead attention is inherently suited for parallel processing, making it exceptionally efficient on systems with advanced hardware like multiple CPUs or GPUs.

2.3.1 Implementation of multihead attention

Fig. 2.7 illustrates the inner workings of the multihead attention mechanism. To set this system up, the query WQ, key WK, and value WV matrices from the traditional self-attention system are divided, with each section aligning with an individual attention head. Each head then processes self-attention independently using its designated subset of weights, ensuring that each head’s queries, keys, and values are distinctly produced.

It is divided into two main parts: Multi-Head Attention and Scaled Dot-Product Attention. On the left, under Multi-Head Attention, there are multiple Linear blocks feeding into a Scaled Dot-Product Attention block, which then connects to a Concat block and another Linear block. The inputs are labeled as V, K and Q. On the right, under Scaled Dot-Product Attention, the process starts with MatMul, followed by Scale, Mask (opt.), SoftMax and another MatMul block. The inputs are labeled as Q, K and V.

The process involves calculating the dot products of the query and key matrices, scaling these products, applying a softmax function for normalization, and then multiplying the result with the value matrix to produce the output for each head. This methodology enables each head to latch onto various slices of information from the input sequence.

However, more heads do not always mean better results. The foundational Transformer study suggests that eight heads are optimal. Increasing to 16 or 32 heads doesn’t necessarily improve performance and may, in fact, lead to diminishing returns, especially when compared to having too few heads, like 1 or 4, which can degrade the model’s effectiveness.

Once all heads have processed their inputs, the resulting vectors are merged into a larger vector, as depicted in Fig. 2.8. For instance, with eight heads, each produces an output vector, and these are then joined together. This combined output then passes through a linear layer (marked as “W” in Fig. 2.8), which readjusts the dimensions back to match the original input while blending the information from all heads into a cohesive whole. This shared linear layer plays a crucial role in harmonizing the diverse insights gathered by the individual attention heads.

The image shows an illustration of merging vectors Z1, Z5, Z8 into a larger vector Z. The vectors Z1, Z5 and Z8 are depicted as matrices. These matrices are multiplied by matrix W, resulting in the larger vector Z.

2.3.2 The role of multihead attention

In the Transformer architecture, the multihead attention layer’s output isn’t just used for subsequent layers—it helps build the complex structure of the model itself. Each head within this layer zeroes in on a unique aspect of the input sequence, enhancing the model’s depth of analysis. Studies on Sparse Transformers have shed light on how each head plays a specialized role, focusing on aspects such as syntax, contextual relationships, and the emphasis on rare words. For instance, the syntax-focused head blocks grammatically incorrect words from influencing the outcome, while the context-focused head parses the sentence, paying close attention to nearby words. Meanwhile, the head that hones in on rare words picks out key elements of sentences.

Fig. 2.9 offers a visual demonstration of how different heads within an encoder influence the attention matrix, with this particular study focusing on an encoder configuration composed of four layers, each labeled from 0 to 3, and each containing six heads, labeled from 0 to 5. In this visualization, each row correlates with one of the encoder layers, and each column corresponds to one of the heads within these layers.

The visual demonstration of how different heads within an encoder influence the attention matrix, with this particular study focusing on an encoder configuration composed of four layers, each labeled from 0 to 3 and each containing six heads, labeled from 0 to 5. The image shows a grid with layers on the y-axis and heads on the x-axis. Each cell in the grid contains a pattern representing the attention matrix influenced by the corresponding head and layer.

The diagram illustrates the dynamics within the layers: while some heads tend to align and focus on similar features—like heads 1 through 4 in both the second and third layers—others exhibit distinct and independent behaviors. For example, in the second layer, head 1, and in the third layer, heads 0 and 5, stand out for their unique choices, indicating a divergence in how they process and prioritize information. This diversity among heads highlights the nuanced capabilities of multihead attention in capturing a broad spectrum of features in the input sequence.

The multihead attention mechanism illustrates that, despite sharing the same method for residual gradient backpropagation, different attention heads manage to develop unique ways of selecting features. This diversity in feature selection could stem from variations in initial configurations and the use of Dropout strategies. Studies have specifically examined how different initialization strategies can decrease variability between layers, leading to more effective training results. Moreover, it has been suggested that employing distinct Dropout strategies for each head can allow them to capture and learn varied features, enhancing the overall functionality of the model.

2.3.3 Optimization of multihead attention

Given the substantial number of parameters in multihead attention, each with its distinct “Q,” “K,” and “V” matrices, the model faces considerable computational and memory challenges. Researchers have developed several strategies to mitigate these issues:

  1. 1. Local multihead attention: This strategy limits each attention head to focus only on a specific portion of the input sequence rather than the entire sequence. This approach significantly reduces computational demands and memory usage while retaining the key benefits of multihead attention.
  2. 2. Sparse multihead attention: This technique reduces both computational complexity and memory usage by having each attention head focus only on select elements of the input sequence. Employing sparse connections can dramatically decrease the resources needed.
  3. 3. Layered multihead attention: In this structure, attention heads are organized into multiple layers, with each layer focusing on different sections and types of information from the input. This arrangement allows the model to grasp more complex contextual details and enhances performance, especially in processing lengthy sequences.

2.4 Feedforward neural network

The FNN, a pivotal component of the Transformer model, may constitute only a fraction of the model’s structure, yet it is essential for extracting nonlinear features and capturing local details from input sequences. As illustrated in Fig. 2.10, the FNN fundamentally operates as a multilayer perceptron, composed of several layers, each populated with numerous neurons.
