In this setup, the input layer takes in the raw data, the hidden layers process this data to extract features, and the output layer is tasked with producing the final predictions. The connections among these layers are directional and form a loop-free path, ensuring that information flows through the network in one direction only.

It consists of three layers: an input layer, a hidden layer and an output layer. The input layer has three neurons labeled as Input, each receiving an input signal. The hidden layer contains three neurons, each connected to all neurons in the input layer. The output layer has three neurons, each connected to all neurons in the hidden layer. Arrows indicate the direction of signal flow from the input layer to the hidden layer and from the hidden layer to the output layer. The output layer neurons are labeled as Output.

In the Transformer model, the FNN is fundamentally composed of two linear layers and a nonlinear activation function. This design allows the network to capture nonlinear characteristics within the input sequence, significantly boosting the model’s ability to express complex ideas. The first linear layer, often referred to as the fully connected layer, projects the input data into a higher dimensional space. The subsequent layer, the output layer, then brings this high-dimensional representation back down to the original dimension. Positioned between these layers, the activation function injects nonlinearity into the process, enabling the network to discern intricate patterns in the data.

Activation functions are vital in FNNs as they convert the outputs of linear transformations into nonlinear outputs, adding necessary complexity to the model. The effectiveness of the network often hinges on the specific activation function used. Popular choices include Sigmoid, Tanh, and ReLU (Rectified Linear Unit), with the Transformer model frequently employing the GELU (Gaussian Error Linear Unit) function. GELU is mathematically expressed as

GELU(x)=0.5x(1+tanh(2πx+0.044715x3)). (2.3)

(2.3)

As depicted in Fig. 2.11, GELU behaves almost linearly when inputs are around zero but tapers off towards zero with larger or smaller inputs. This property makes GELU particularly effective in deep neural network training, helping to prevent gradients from vanishing.

The x-axis ranges from negative 3 to 3 in increments of 1. The y-axis ranges from negative 0.5 to 3.0 in increments of 0.5. The curve starts in the negative x quadrant slightly below the x-axis. It descends gradually, reaching its lowest point around x equals negative 0.5, where it's approximately -0.1 on the y-axis. From there, the curve begins to rise. As the curve enters the positive x quadrant, it increases more steeply. Around x equals 0.5, the curve crosses the x-axis and continues to rise. The slope becomes increasingly steep as x increases. By x equals 1, the y-value is approximately 0.5. At x equals 2, y is about 2.0. The curve continues its upward trajectory, reaching about y equals 3.0 at x equals 3. The shape of the curve resembles that of an activation function used in machine learning, specifically the Gaussian Error Linear Unit (GELU) function, as mentioned in the provided context information.

In a FNN, each connection is assigned a weight that determines how strongly an input signal is passed to the next layer. These weights are fine-tuned through gradient descent during training to minimize prediction errors. Additionally, each neuron has a bias value that adjusts its activation threshold, which is similarly tuned during training to optimize network performance.

Despite its relatively small footprint within the Transformer model, the FNN is pivotal in capturing local information and unlocking nonlinear features. Its contributions to the Transformer model are notably significant in several areas:

  1. 1. Local information extraction: Positioned within each Transformer layer, the FNN processes outputs from the self-attention mechanism, adeptly extracting local features. This functionality enables the model to grasp short-range dependencies within the input sequence, substantially boosting its expressive power.
  2. 2. Nonlinearity introduction: Equipped with nonlinear activation functions, the FNN adeptly identifies nonlinear features within the input data. This capability is crucial for handling complex natural language sequences, which often contain inherently nonlinear patterns and structures.
  3. 3. Generalization enhancement: By harnessing both local and nonlinear features, the FNN significantly enhances the model’s ability to generalize effectively when encountering new and previously unseen data.

2.5 Residual connection

Residual connections, a type of shortcut linkage in neural networks, allow for the direct transmission of layer inputs to later stages. This architectural feature is crucial for alleviating the vanishing gradient issue, thereby boosting both the training process and the overall model efficacy. These connections were pioneered by Kaiming He and colleagues in 2015 within the framework of deep residual networks to tackle the prevalent problems of vanishing and exploding gradients during deep neural network training. Within the Transformer architecture, residual connections are extensively employed, enhancing the multihead attention mechanisms, position-wise FNNs, and the various sublayers of the decoder.

Fig. 2.11 depicts the core concept of residual connections, which involve introducing a shortcut in a neural network layer that effectively skips over certain layers. This design allows information from a preceding layer to bypass others and proceed directly to further layers. In mathematical terms, if x is the input to a layer, and it undergoes a nonlinear transformation denoted by F(x), the output becomes F(x) + x. Here, x is the input signal, and F(x) is the transformation applied by the layer. Adding x to F(x) results in combining the original input with the residual changes learned by the layer. This configuration is particularly beneficial during the backpropagation process as it facilitates smoother gradient flow, effectively addressing the issue of vanishing gradients and thus improving the training process (Fig. 2.12).

The left diagram shows a standard learning block with an input x, two weight layers each followed by a ReLU activation and an output y equals H(x). The right diagram shows a residual learning block with an input x, two weight layers each followed by a ReLU activation, an intermediate output F(x) and a final output y equals F(x) plus x. The residual learning block includes a shortcut connection that adds the input x to the intermediate output F(x) before the final ReLU activation.

Residual connections are a key feature in the Transformer model, widely applied across its components to speed up training and boost performance. In the multihead attention sublayer, once the input undergoes self-attention processing, the resulting output is combined back with the original input through a residual connection. This setup ensures that the model maintains the initial information while seamlessly integrating it with the insights derived from the self-attention.

Similarly, in the FNN sublayer, the input goes through two stages of linear transformations followed by an activation function. The output from this sequence is then added back to the original input, establishing another residual connection. This arrangement preserves the original input data and melds it with the new information learned through the FNN, fostering a richer understanding and better retention of input features.

In both the encoder and decoder components of the Transformer, each layer includes one or more sublayers equipped with residual connections. These connections are crucial for allowing information to flow effectively through the depth of the network, ultimately contributing to improved performance of the model.

In summary, the Transformer model incorporates residual connections across its multihead attention mechanisms, position-wise FNNs, and both encoder and decoder layers. These connections effectively address the challenges of gradient vanishing and exploding by offering a shortcut that allows for direct gradient flow to earlier layers. This feature is crucial for preventing common issues associated with training deep models, thereby boosting overall model performance. Residual connections also expedite the training process by eliminating unnecessary calculations, streamlining the training duration. Moreover, by enabling the learning of more complex representations, residual connections enhance the model’s ability to generalize, improving its performance on test datasets.

2.6 Layer normalization

Layer Normalization is a proven effective strategy for stabilizing neural network training and enhancing model performance. Introduced by Jimmy Ba et al. in 2016, it is extensively utilized in Transformer models. The core process involves normalizing the outputs of each layer’s neurons to have consistent means and variances.

This normalization works by first calculating the mean and standard deviation across all outputs of a layer’s neurons. Each neuron’s output is then adjusted by subtracting the mean and dividing by the standard deviation. This results in normalized outputs, described by the equation:

yi=xi−μσ2+ϵ. (2.4)

(2.4)

Here, xi represents the output from the i-th neuron, μ and σ2 are the computed mean and variance, and is a small positive number added for numerical stability. With zero mean and unit variance, these normalized outputs significantly expedite the training process and enhance the model’s efficiency.

In the Transformer model, layer normalization plays a pivotal role in optimizing the training process. By normalizing neuron outputs, it accelerates the convergence of gradient descent, enhances training efficiency, reduces covariance shifts between layers, and prevents gradient explosions or disappearances. This normalization significantly reduces the model’s sensitivity to variations in input data, improving its performance on new datasets.

Layer normalization is strategically deployed across the Transformer architecture:

  1. 1. Multihead attention mechanism: It is used to normalize attention scores, improving the efficacy of the attention process.
  2. 2. Position-based feedforward networks: Here, it normalizes the outputs of neurons, facilitating faster training and boosting overall model performance.
  3. 3. Encoder-decoder architecture: Layer normalization is applied both to each sublayer’s output and to the final outputs of the encoder and decoder, stabilizing training and enhancing generalization capabilities.
  4. 4. Residual connections: It works alongside addition operations to ensure outputs maintain consistent means and variances postaddition, further enhancing performance and training speed.

Collectively, residual connections and layer normalization are critical to the Transformer’s architecture. Residual connections introduce skip links that alleviate gradient vanishing, boost training efficiency, and strengthen generalization. Layer normalization, by standardizing outputs within layers, speeds up training and stabilizes the model. These technologies are integral to the multihead attention, position-based feedforward networks, and the sublayers of the encoder and decoder, underpinning the Transformer’s robust performance.

2.7 Position encoding

Position encoding is a fundamental component of the Transformer model (as illustrated in Fig. 2.13), equipping the model with the capability to manage the positional information of words in sequences. Given that the self-attention mechanism lacks the ability to detect the positions of words, position encoding is crucial for understanding the order within sequences.

It starts with the input sequence I love my country. Each word in the sequence is converted into an embedding vector: v0 equals embedding vector (I), v1 equals embedding vector (love), v2 equals embedding vector (my) and v3 equals embedding vector (country). These embedding vectors are then added to their corresponding position vectors from the positional encoding matrix: P0 equals position vector (I), P1 equals position vector (love), P2 equals position vector (my) and P3 equals position vector (country). The result is the positional encoding layer output: y0 equals positional embedding (I), y1 equals positional embedding (love), y2 equals positional embedding (my) and y3 equals positional embedding (country).

2.7.1 Design and implementation of positional encoding

The self-attention mechanism leverages matrix multiplication to assess relationships between words, ignoring their sequential positions—an essential factor in processing ordered data such as text. To address this, position encoding represents each word’s position in the input sequence and integrates this data with the original word representations. Position encoding in Transformers is produced using predefined sine and cosine functions. The encoding for a given position p and dimension i is calculated as follows:

PE(p,2i)=sin(p10,0002i/dmodel) (2.5)

(2.5)

PE(p,2i+1)=cos(p10,0002i/dmodel). (2.6)

(2.6)

This setup, where d_model denotes the dimension of word embeddings, offers several benefits:

  • • The encoding varies in frequency across different positions and dimensions, aiding the model in discerning complex positional relationships.
  • • The cyclical nature of the sine and cosine functions allows the model to process longer sequences beyond the training data.
  • • Position encoding is adaptable, permitting the model to tailor the significance of positional information based on specific task needs.

To implement position encoding, a vector of dimension d_model is generated for each position using the equations above. These vectors are then added to the word embeddings of the input sequence, creating enriched word representations with embedded positional information. These enriched representations are then utilized in subsequent layers, including multihead attention and feedforward networks, enhancing the model’s ability to interpret and process sequences.

Consider a sentence of sequence length 4, “I love my country,” with each word depicted as a two-dimensional vector. This requires generating a 4×2 matrix for position encoding. In Transformers, this encoding is derived from sine and cosine functions, creating distinct positional signals. In these formulas, i denotes the position within the sequence (starting at 0), j is the dimension index, and dmodel is the dimensionality of the input vectors. For this example, dmodel is 2. Using Eqs. (2.5, 2.6), the position encoding matrix for this example, with dimensions 4×2, can be calculated as follows:

PE0,0=sin010,000∧02=sin0=0

PE0,1=cos010,000∧02=cos0=1

PE1,0=sin110,000∧02=sin1≈0.84

PE1,1=cos110,000∧02=cos1≈0.54

PE2,0=sin210,000∧02=sin2≈0.91

PE2,1=cos210,000∧02=cos2≈−0.42

PE3,0=sin310,000∧02=sin3≈0.14

PE(3,1)=cos(3/10,000∧(0/2))=cos(3)≈−0.99.

These calculated values are compiled into the position encoding matrix as follows:

00.840.9110.54−0.420.14−0.99

Subsequently, this position encoding matrix can be added to the original word vectors. This addition enables the model to integrate positional information into its processing, as depicted in Fig. 2.13. This figure illustrates the process of calculating the position encoding matrix and its application to sequence data. It’s important to note that this example is a simplified representation. Typically, in real-world applications, the dimensionality of dmodel is considerably larger, often set to 512.

2.7.2 Variants of positional encoding

While the Transformer’s original architecture relies on fixed sine and cosine functions for generating position encodings, researchers have developed alternative methods for conveying positional information:

  1. 1. Learned position encoding: This approach involves initializing position encodings with random values that are subsequently optimized through gradient descent during training. This method enables the model to dynamically refine its positional representations to meet the specific demands of the task.
  2. 2. Relative position encoding: Focusing on the relative distances between words within a sequence rather than their absolute positions, relative position encoding aids the model in learning more universal positional patterns. This facilitates better adaptation to sequences of varying lengths and can be implemented by altering the self-attention mechanism to account for the relative distances between queries and keys.
  3. 3. Combined position encoding: This method merges various positional representation techniques, including fixed sine/cosine functions, learned position encodings, and relative position encodings. By integrating these diverse sources of positional information, the model can draw on a broader range of insights to improve its predictive capabilities.

2.7.3 The advantages and limitations of positional encoding

Position encoding significantly boosts the ability of neural networks to process sequence data by providing essential positional information in specific ways. This capability allows models to recognize and leverage the relationships between words, enhancing the handling of sequential data such as natural language. Thanks to the periodic properties of sine and cosine functions, position encoding efficiently manages long sequences and facilitates generalization beyond the lengths encountered during training. Additionally, models can improve their adaptability by learning to adjust the importance of positional information based on the requirements of specific tasks.

However, it is important to note that for tasks not reliant on sequence order, introducing position encoding could add unnecessary computational complexity. Fixed sine and cosine position encodings might also fail to detect some complex positional patterns within sequences, particularly regarding local structures. When sequences exceed the maximum length processed during training, the model might struggle with effective generalization using position encoding. In these instances, relative position encoding or other expandable methods of position encoding might offer a more effective solution.

2.8 Training and optimization

Training and optimizing a Transformer model involves selecting appropriate methods, such as loss functions and optimizers. This section explores training techniques and strategies for optimizing Transformers.

2.8.1 Loss function

At the heart of training is defining a loss function that quantifies the discrepancy between the model’s predictions and the true labels. The cross-entropy loss function is often used in Transformers. It computes a loss by comparing the probability distributions from the model’s output against the actual labels. The training objective is to minimize this loss.

Cross-entropy measures differences between two probability distributions and is crucial in classification tasks. In Transformers, the output layer converts scores into probabilities using the softmax function. Cross-entropy then assesses the discrepancies between these probabilities and the true distributions. Its formula is

H(p,q)=−∑i=1np(xi)log(q(xi)) (2.7)

(2.7)

where p is the actual distribution and q is the predicted one. Cross-entropy’s main benefit is its direct measurement of the prediction accuracy against true labels, although it may not be as effective for less probable categories.

Alternative loss functions can be adopted based on the task’s needs. For instance, regression tasks might use mean squared error or mean absolute error, while generative tasks could use negative log-likelihood or Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence.

2.8.2 Optimizer

Minimizing the loss function requires selecting an appropriate optimizer to update the model parameters. In Transformers, Adam is a preferred choice. Adam, or Adaptive Moment Estimation, is an optimizer that automatically adjusts the learning rate based on both the first and second moment estimates of gradients. This feature allows Transformers to be optimized with varying learning rates at different training phases.

Adam combines the advantages of fast convergence and high computational efficiency, and is generally less sensitive to the choice of hyperparameters. However, it can sometimes compromise generalization performance.

Other available optimizers include Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD), AdaGrad, and RMSprop. SGD is straightforward but might be slow to converge in certain scenarios. AdaGrad and RMSprop, which adaptively adjust the learning rate based on the first and second moments, respectively, can lead to premature learning rate decay in prolonged training sessions. Each optimizer comes with its own set of pros and cons, necessitating a choice that best fits the specific requirements of the task and dataset.

2.8.3 Learning rate adjustment strategy

In the original Transformer paper, the authors introduced a novel approach to learning rate scheduling known as “Noam scheduling.” This method initially increases the learning rate linearly, followed by a gradual decrease, enhancing early training convergence and later preventing overfitting. The formula for this strategy is

lr=dmodel−0.5⋅minstep_num−0.5,step_num⋅warmup_steps−1.5 (2.8)

(2.8)

where dmodel is the model’s dimension, step_num is the current step in training, and warmup_steps is the number of initial steps before regular training pacing begins. This approach balances the model’s convergence speed and stability effectively.

Learning rate strategies are critical as they influence how quickly and effectively a model learns and generalizes. The Noam schedule, specifically, promotes a faster initial convergence and helps in maintaining model stability, although it may require fine-tuning of warmup steps and other hyperparameters.

Other strategies like exponential decay, cosine annealing, and piecewise constant learning rates also exist. Exponential decay reduces the learning rate steadily over time, while cosine annealing adjusts the learning rate cyclically, reflecting the cosine function’s periodic nature. The piecewise constant strategy divides the training into phases, each with a consistent learning rate. Choosing the right strategy depends on the specific requirements of the task and the data characteristics.

2.8.4 Regularization

To combat overfitting, a range of regularization techniques are utilized during the training process. In Transformers, weight decay (also known as L2 regularization), dropout, and layer normalization are prevalent strategies. Weight decay boosts generalization by incorporating the L2 norm of parameters into the loss function, which helps control the scale of the parameters. Nonetheless, this approach might sometimes overly penalize certain parameters.

As depicted in Fig. 2.14, dropout is a technique used during training to randomly deactivate a subset of neurons, effectively reducing the model’s tendency to overfit to particular training examples and thus improving its ability to generalize. However, one downside of dropout is that it can slow down the model’s convergence rate.

Diagram (A) is labeled Standard Neural Net and shows a fully connected neural network with three layers of neurons. Each neuron in one layer is connected to every neuron in the adjacent layers with arrows indicating the direction of connections. Diagram (B) is labeled After applying dropout and shows the same neural network with some neurons deactivated, indicated by circles with an X inside them. The connections to and from these deactivated neurons are also removed.

Layer normalization standardizes the activations within each layer of a neural network, speeding up training and enhancing generalization. However, it can sometimes adversely affect specific tasks.

Moreover, there are additional regularization methods like L1 regularization, data augmentation, and early stopping. L1 regularization incorporates the L1 norm of parameters into the loss function, creating a sparse matrix of parameters, though this might sometimes lead to excessive penalties on certain parameters. Data augmentation improves generalization by variably transforming training data to enlarge the dataset, but this can extend the training duration. Early stopping, which halts training when no significant improvements are seen on the validation set, helps prevent overfitting but may result in an underfit model.

2.8.5 Other training and optimization techniques

Training and optimizing Transformers involve various additional techniques such as learning rate warmup, gradient clipping, weight initialization, dropout, label smoothing, gradient accumulation, and mixed precision training, which will be elaborated upon next.

The learning rate warmup strategy starts with a low learning rate at the onset of training and gradually increases to a preset maximum. This approach is designed to prevent the instability that can occur from large updates to parameters early in training. Typically, Transformers employ a linear warmup strategy where the learning rate progressively increases from zero to the established maximum before reducing according to a specific scheme like inverse square root decay, as illustrated in Fig. 2.15.

The x-axis represents time, ranging from 0 to 200 in increments of 25. The y-axis represents the learning rate, ranging from 0 to 0.0010 in increments of 0.0002. The line starts near zero and rapidly increases to a peak of about 0.0010 at around time 25. This represents the warmup phase where the learning rate increases from zero to a maximum value. After reaching the peak, the line gradually decreases in a curved manner, illustrating the decay phase. The decay follows what appears to be an inverse square root pattern, as mentioned in the context information. By the end of the graph at time 200, the learning rate has decreased to approximately 0.0001, which is about 10 percent of its peak value. This graph visually represents the typical learning rate strategy employed in Transformer models, with an initial linear warmup followed by a decay phase, as described in the context information provided.

Gradient clipping is a technique employed to counteract the issue of gradient explosion. In neural network training, if gradients become too large, they can destabilize the model by causing disproportionately large updates to weights. By establishing a maximum limit for gradient values, these excessive updates can be curbed. Should gradients exceed this limit, they are scaled back to match the established threshold.

Weight initialization plays a critical role in both the speed of training and the ultimate performance of the model. Transformer models typically use random initialization techniques with either uniform or normal distributions, such as Glorot (also known as Xavier) or He initialization.

Dropout is another potent regularization strategy used to combat overfitting. It works by randomly disabling a subset of neurons during training, which is particularly common in the Transformer’s multihead attention and feedforward layers.

Label smoothing is a technique to combat overfitting by blending true labels with a controlled amount of noise, thereby boosting the model’s generalization abilities.

Gradient accumulation collects gradients from several batches before updating the model’s parameters. This method effectively mimics training with larger batch sizes, enhancing both the stability and the speed of convergence of the model, while not requiring additional memory resources.

Mixed precision training combines single precision (float32) and half precision (float16) computations to reduce memory demands and computational load, thus speeding up the training process. As demonstrated in the comparative experiment shown in Fig. 2.16, with identical hyperparameters but different data types (float32 (FT32) or mixed precision), the training loss curves (left image) are nearly the same, yet the training speed of the mixed precision model is noticeably quicker (right image).

The left graph is titled Training curves for FP32 and Mixed Precision. The x-axis represents the Number of Iterations ranging from 0 to 250000 in increments of 50000. The y-axis shows Training Loss with values from 0 to 0.08 in increments of 0.02. Two lines are plotted: one for FP32 and one for Mixed Precision. Both lines start high and quickly drop, then stabilize at a low value, with the Mixed Precision line slightly lower than the FP32 line. The right graph is titled Speedup Ratio of FP32 and Mixed Precision. It shares the same x-axis as the left graph, representing Number of Iterations from 0 to 250000. The y-axis shows Number of Iterations (which appears to be mislabeled and likely represents the speedup ratio) ranging from 0 to 1.8 in increments of 0.2. Two lines are plotted, labeled as FP32 and Mixed Precision in the legend. The Mixed Precision line remains consistently higher, around 1.6, while the FP32 line stays close to 0.2 throughout the iterations. Both graphs demonstrate a comparison between FP32 and Mixed Precision methods across training iterations, with Mixed Precision showing lower training loss and higher speedup ratio.

These techniques are crucial in the training and optimization of Transformer models. Tailoring these methods to the specific needs of the task and dataset can significantly improve the training results and model performance. Generally, training and optimizing Transformer models involve selecting the right loss functions, optimizers, learning rate adjustments, and regularization techniques. For each task and dataset, selecting the appropriate settings and fine-tuning hyperparameters are essential to maximize model performance. Each method has its pros and cons in real-world applications, necessitating careful evaluation and choice.

2.9 Summary

This chapter thoroughly explains the fundamental principles and applications of the Transformer model and its essential components. It starts with a succinct overview of the Transformer model, then dives deep into each critical component. The self-attention mechanism is covered extensively, detailing its principles, benefits, and limitations. The chapter also delves into multihead attention, discussing its rationale, detailed implementation, practical uses, and various enhancements and adaptations.

Future chapters will further explore the progression of large language models, examining enhancements to the Transformer model across different GPT versions and discussing its uses in areas like NLP, generative models, and reinforcement learning.

Chapter 3 Generative pretraining

Abstract

This chapter delves into Generative Pretraining (GPT), a foundational technique in natural language processing (NLP) that underlies the GPT series of models. GPT pretrains on extensive unlabeled text data to learn a generative probability distribution, enabling it to predict subsequent words in a sequence based on prior context. The chapter explains the architecture of GPT, which is built on a Transformer decoder with masked self-attention mechanisms, allowing the model to focus solely on previous words during generation. The process of pretraining is followed by supervised fine-tuning, where the model is adapted for specific NLP tasks using labeled data. This dual-phase approach significantly enhances the model’s performance across various applications, making GPT a powerful tool in language understanding and generation.

Keywords

Generative pretraining; natural language processing; transformer decoder; masked self-attention; supervised fine-tuning; language model; unsupervised learning; text generation

Generative Pretraining (GPT) is a crucial technology in natural language processing (NLP) that also underpins the GPT series of models. The core concept revolves around pretraining on extensive text data to grasp the generative probability distribution of language models. These pretrained models are then utilized across various downstream tasks. This chapter offers an in-depth exploration of the principles and techniques behind GPT, equipping readers with a thorough understanding of the GPT model’s technical nuances.

3.1 Introduction to generative pretraining

Pretraining techniques have demonstrated remarkable effectiveness across many tasks in computer vision and natural language processing (NLP). These methods leverage extensive amounts of unlabeled data to uncover the hidden structures of the underlying data.

Generative Pretraining (GPT) aims to develop a language model focused on learning a generative probability distribution. This means it predicts the likelihood of the next word based on a given context sequence, such as words, phrases, or sentences. GPT uses a neural network, specifically a Transformer model equipped with self-attention mechanisms, to model these probabilities. This allows it to capture the long-distance relationships in text sequences. As depicted in Fig. 3.1, at the heart of GPT is a multilayer Transformer encoder, each layer composed of a masked multihead attention sublayer and a feedforward neural network sublayer. Masked self-attention, a special type of self-attention, captures dependencies across different positions in the input sequence and ensures that the model during pretraining only considers information from previous words (this is done by introducing a masking matrix during self-attention calculations, which excludes subsequent words in the sequence).

At the heart of GPT is a multilayer Transformer encoder, each layer composed of a masked multihead attention sublayer and a feedforward neural network sublayer. The diagram shows the flow starting from Text and Positional Encoding at the bottom, followed by Masked Multi-Head Attention, Layer Normalization, Feedforward Network and another Layer Normalization. This sequence is repeated 12 times. The output is directed towards Text Prediction and Text Classification.

In the training phase, GPT seeks to maximize the data likelihood by minimizing the cross-entropy loss, enhancing the chances of accurately predicting the next word. Optimization of this loss improves the precision of the generative probability distribution. Popular optimization techniques include Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD) and variants like Adam and Adagrad. The model’s performance is heavily influenced by how the learning rate is adjusted and regularization techniques employed. Typically, strategies like warming up the learning rate and gradually reducing it help stabilize the training process.

3.2 Generative pretraining model

The foundation of the GPT model is the decoder architecture of the Transformer, which is renowned for its self-attention mechanism that effectively captures long-distance dependencies and boosts parallel computing power. As depicted in Fig. 3.2, the essence of this model is a series of Transformer decoder layers, each comprising a self-attention sublayer and a feedforward neural network sublayer. In this setup, the self-attention sublayer comes first, focusing on identifying dependencies across various positions in the input sequence, while the subsequent feedforward sublayer handles nonlinear transformations at each position.

The diagram illustrates a series of Transformer decoder layers, each comprising a self-attention sublayer and a feedforward neural network sublayer. The process starts with the input, which goes through Input Embedding and Positional Encoding, followed by Dropout. This is then fed into Transformer Block Layer 1, followed by subsequent Transformer Block Layers, up to Layer L. Each Transformer Block consists of a LayerNorm, followed by a self-attention mechanism with multiple heads (Head 1 to Head H), each involving Matmul, Mask, Softmax, Dropout and another Matmul. The outputs of these heads are combined through a Linear layer. This is followed by another LayerNorm, a Linear layer, Gelu activation, another Linear layer, Dropout and a residual connection. The final output of the Transformer Block is passed through another LayerNorm, a Linear layer and Softmax to produce the final output.

To ensure that the model generates text based solely on the context it has seen and not on any future information, GPT uses a technique called masked self-attention. In practice, this means that during the self-attention scoring process, the model ignores any words that appear after the current position in the sequence. This is accomplished by integrating a mask matrix into the self-attention calculation, which guarantees that only preceding information is taken into account.

The core principle of masked self-attention is that the model’s focus is restricted to the current and preceding positions when calculating self-attention weights. For instance, in an input sequence x=(x1,x2...,xn), the self-attention weight at position i considers only the elements from position 1 to i. This approach ensures that the text generated is informed only by the context already observed and not by any subsequent information. To achieve this, the attention matrix used in self-attention calculations is modified by masking, where weights for future positions are set to negative infinity. When processed through a softmax function, these weights effectively become zero, thereby enforcing causal masking.

The masked self-attention mechanism is crucial in GPT tasks. As illustrated in Fig. 3.3, during the pretraining phase, the model is tasked with learning a broad representation of language. Masked self-attention ensures that the model concentrates solely on the current position and the preceding context when generating text, aiding in adherence to the natural patterns of language generation. During the fine-tuning phase, this mechanism continues to be essential, helping the model produce outputs that align with the previously observed context.

It shows 4 stages of weights and masking for the words: Deep, learning, algorithms, excel, in, Natural, language, processing, tasks. Each stage has a graph of weights, followed by a masked sequence. The first stage focuses on the word Deep, the second on learning, the third on processing and the fourth on tasks. The right side of the image shows a grid labeled 'Using [Masked Self-Attention] to Ignore Subsequent Words' with attention weights and zero weights.

It’s important to note the distinct differences between the GPT model and the original Transformer model. In the original Transformer, the encoder’s self-attention is not masked, allowing the model to focus on any position within the sequence. This setup is advantageous for some NLP tasks like machine translation. However, for GPT, the lack of masking can result in text that improperly relies on future information, violating the principles of natural language generation.

Additionally, in the traditional Transformer model, both the encoder and decoder are crucial, with the encoder encoding the input sequence and the decoder producing the target sequence based on the encoder’s output. In contrast, GPT aims to learn a universal language representation, not to translate an input sequence to a target sequence. Thus the GPT model exclusively employs the decoder architecture, streamlining its focus on language understanding and generation.

3.3 The generative pretraining process

GPT is a method that uses vast amounts of unlabeled text data to learn how to predict the structure and latent meanings within texts. Its objective is to develop a language model that can produce text sequences resembling those in the training data. Specifically, given a text sequence x=(x1,x2…,xt), the model is tasked with learning the probability distribution P(x) of the entire sequence. This distribution is understood as a series of conditional probabilities:

P(x)=∏(P(xt|C)) (3.1)

(3.1)

where C denotes the context, which includes the previous t−1 words (x1, x2,…, xt−1). P(xt|C) calculates the likelihood of producing the t-th word based on this context.

3.3.1 The objectives of generative pretraining

In the process of GPT, the main goal of the model is to maximize how likely it is to correctly predict words from the training data, a method known as maximum likelihood estimation. Specifically, given a training sample x and the model-generated probability distribution P(x), the objective function is defined as

LLM=∑i=1TlogPθwi|w<i (3.2)

(3.2)

where LLM is the loss function for the autoregressive language model, T represents the length of the training sample, w<i is the sequence of words before position i, and wi is the word at position i. Pθ(wi|w<i) is the conditional probability that the model, using parameters θ, predicts the next word wi.

Autoregressive language models are designed to predict the next word in a sequence based on the previous words. The model calculates the likelihood of a word appearing at the current position using the history of the sequence. Therefore Pθ(wi|w<i) indicates the probability of the word w<i occurring, given the prior sequence wi. The parameter θ is a set of model parameters that influence its ability to make these predictions.

Initially, the input text sequence u is transformed into an embedded representation, where each word is converted into a vector using a token embedding matrix. This matrix assigns each word a fixed-dimensional vector.

Next, to help the model grasp the relationships between words, position information is added to each word’s embedding. This is done through a position embedding matrix Wp, which is combined with the token embedding matrix We to form the initial embedding representation h0.

This representation, h0, is then processed through several layers of a Transformer block, each consisting of self-attention and feedforward layers. The output hl from the l-th Transformer layer is given by:

hl=transformer_blockhl−1∀i∈[1,n] (3.3)

(3.3)

where nnn represents the total number of layers in the Transformer stack. At the end of the Transformer stack, the final output vector hn and a matrix We, containing the embedding vectors for each word in the vocabulary, are used to compute the probability of the next word u as

P(u)=softmax(hnWeT). (3.4)

(3.4)

Here, the softmax function converts the output into a probability distribution, with P(u) indicating the likelihood of each word being the next word in the sequence. The model selects the next word by finding the highest value from the dot product between P(u) and the word embeddings.

The primary objective of training the GPT-1 model is to minimize negative log-likelihood. This approach essentially reduces the errors the model makes in predicting the next word based on the preceding context. To meet this target, the model not only optimizes the parameters of its Transformer structure but also fine-tunes the word embedding vectors. Throughout each training cycle, which includes both the forward pass and backpropagation, the model calculates and minimizes the loss function using the backpropagation algorithm to refine its parameters. Therefore during the unsupervised pretraining phase, the GPT-1 model continually adjusts both its parameters and the representations of words to improve its predictive accuracy.

3.3.2 The error backpropagation process in generative pretraining

All models in the GPT series use a method known as reversible tokenization, which is based on data compression techniques (refer to Section 4.2.3), to facilitate the explanation of the error backpropagation process in GPT. In this chapter, we simplify the initial embedding of words. It is assumed that the model generates its initial embeddings by multiplying the one-hot encoding of the input text sequence with a word embedding matrix W, represented as We:

We=X×W. (3.5)

(3.5)

Here, X stands for the one-hot encoded matrix of the input text sequence, while W is the word embedding matrix. The model then enhances these embeddings by applying multihead attention, residual connections, and layer normalization within each Transformer stack layer to produce contextually enriched embeddings hi:

hl=transformer_blockhl−1∀i∈[1,n]. (3.6)

(3.6)

In the final step, the output from the last Transformer stack layer is multiplied by a weighting matrix and processed through a softmax function to compute the probability distribution for the next word in the sequence.

When calculating its loss function, the model evaluates the difference between the actual next word’s one-hot encoding and its predicted probability distribution to compute the loss. This loss is then sent back through the Transformer stack layers using the backpropagation algorithm, which updates the embedding representations for each word. The gradient calculation for the embedding vector v of any particular word during backpropagation is

∂L∂v=∂L∂P×W. (3.7)

(3.7)

In this formula, L is the model’s loss function, P is the predicted probability distribution of the next word by the model, and W is the embedding matrix. By applying the gradient descent method, the model uses this gradient to update the value of v, thus improving the accuracy of future predictions. Once training is complete, these refined word embeddings are utilized as the initial embedding representations for subsequent tasks.

During GPT, the strategies for adjusting the learning rate and methods of regularization play a crucial role in enhancing model performance. Commonly, strategies such as a warmup phase for the learning rate followed by a gradual decrease are implemented to optimize learning rates, aiming to increase the stability of the training process. Furthermore, to avoid overfitting—a common challenge in model training—techniques like Dropout and weight decay (for instance, L2 regularization) are used to maintain the simplicity of the model structure.

Once pretraining is complete, the GPT model becomes capable of handling various NLP tasks, including text classification, sentiment analysis, and question answering. To tailor the model for these specific tasks, supervised fine-tuning is conducted. In this phase, the model is trained with labeled data pertinent to specific tasks, which allows it to acquire task-specific knowledge. This stage is considered a form of transfer learning, as the model utilizes the extensive linguistic knowledge it gained during pretraining to improve its effectiveness on downstream tasks.

GPT is an approach where language models learn from a vast amount of unlabeled text data through unsupervised learning. The GPT model uses a Transformer architecture powered by self-attention mechanisms and employs a causality mask during self-attention calculations to ensure that the model only accesses information from previous segments. The objective is to maximize the likelihood on the training data (Eqs. 3.2–3.3) to master a generative probability distribution (Eqs. 3.1–3.3). After this phase, the model can undergo supervised fine-tuning to adapt to a variety of NLP tasks. This method of GPT has proven to significantly boost performance across numerous language processing applications, highlighting its substantial potential.

3.4 Supervised fine-tuning

GPT equips the GPT model with a fundamental understanding of grammar and vocabulary. During the next phase, supervised fine-tuning, the model’s parameters are further refined using annotated data from specific tasks. This enables the model to perform specialized NLP tasks like sentiment analysis, text classification, and question answering.

3.4.1 The principles of supervised fine-tuning

This section introduces two new equations to describe the supervised fine-tuning process. Consider a training example (x,y) for a specific task, where x is the input sequence and y is the associated label. The aim during this phase is to minimize the loss function defined as

L′θ=−ElogPy|x;θ. (3.8)

(3.8)

Here, θ denotes the model parameters, E is the expected value, and P(y|x;θ) represents the probability of generating the label y, given the input x and parameters θ. This loss function, while similar to that described in Section 3.3.1 (Eqs. 3.2–3.3), specifically targets the probability of producing task-related labels, not just words. The objective for fine-tuning the model for specific tasks is set as follows:

θ*=argminθ∑L′θ+λR(θ). (3.9)

(3.9)

Here, θ* are the adjusted parameters after fine-tuning, L′θ is the loss function for task-specific data, λ is a regularization coefficient, and R(θ) is the regularization term, usually the L2 norm of the parameters. The goal is to minimize the loss on task-specific data while using regularization to prevent overfitting.

3.4.2 Supervised fine-tuning for specific tasks

In the GPT phase, the GPT model diligently works to grasp the various aspects of natural language, enabling it to produce text similar to the input sequences it receives. At this stage, the GPT operates as a universal language model, capturing extensive knowledge encompassing vocabulary, grammar, and semantics. During the supervised fine-tuning phase, the model is trained with annotated data, allowing it to acquire task-specific knowledge. This resembles the process of transfer learning, where the model leverages the extensive linguistic insights gained during pretraining to boost its performance on specific tasks.

The representations learned during pretraining effectively capture the fundamental structures of the input data. These are then leveraged in the fine-tuning phase to enhance the model’s understanding of task-specific features. For particular NLP tasks, the input sequence must undergo specific modifications to ensure the model focuses on relevant information. These modifications are referred to as task-specific input transformations.

The main purpose of these transformations is to tailor the input sequence to fit the requirements of specific tasks. This typically involves incorporating special tokens within the sequence to emphasize task-related details. For instance, in a question-answering task, both the question and its contextual information need to be combined into a single input sequence to enable the model to generate appropriate answers. Inserting a special separator, such as “[SEP],” between the question and context helps the model to more accurately interpret the input structure and deliver more precise predictions.

Fig. 3.4 illustrates the application of the GPT model to specific NLP tasks. This process is divided into two main stages: first, the unsupervised pretraining stage, where the model learns language models through generative training; second, the supervised fine-tuning stage, where the model is tailored to specific NLP tasks. On the left side of Fig. 3.4, the model learns the structure of language by maximizing the likelihood of input sequences, while on the right side, it shows how model parameters are optimized for specific tasks to improve task performance. The four NLP tasks are highlighted in Fig. 3.4:

  1. 1. Text classification: In text classification tasks, the model classifies input text into one or several predefined categories based on content. Task-specific input transformations are straightforward here, involving only a text segment. The original text is input directly into the model. Post fine-tuning, the GPT model can predict category labels based on the input content. A fully connected layer is typically added to the output layer for task-specific predictions. To help the model distinguish different tasks, a special classification token (e.g., “[CLS]”) might be placed at the start of the sequence.
  2. 2. Natural language inference (NLI): This task involves determining if a hypothesis is derivable from a given premise, with potential outputs being entailment, contradiction, or neutrality. The input sequence includes both the premise and the hypothesis, separated by a special symbol (e.g., “[Delim]”). A special inference token (e.g., “[NLI]”) at the sequence’s start signals the inference task to the model.
  3. 3. Similarity: Here, the model evaluates the similarity between two text sequences. A special delimiter (e.g., “[Delim]”) is typically placed between the texts to be compared, and a similarity marker (e.g., “[SIM]”) might be added at the start to signify a similarity evaluation task.
  4. 4. Multiple choice: This task requires selecting the most appropriate answer from multiple options based on a question. A special delimiter (e.g., “[Delim]”) is used between the question and each option to differentiate them. A multiple-choice token (e.g., “[MCH]”) at the start indicates the task type.

At the top, there are two boxes labeled Text Prediction and Task Classifier. Below these, there is a block labeled Text and Position Embed, which connects to a larger block containing Layer Norm, Feed Forward and Masked Multi Self Attention, repeated 12 times. The tasks shown are Classification, Entailment, Similarity and Multiple Choice. Each task has a sequence starting with Start, followed by various elements like Text, Premise, Hypothesis, Text 1, Text 2, Context, Answer 1, Answer 2 and Answer N, with delimiters and Extract. These sequences are processed by a Transformer and then a Linear layer. For Similarity, there is an additional step where outputs from two Transformers are combined before the Linear layer.

Task-specific input transformations significantly enhance the GPT model’s ability to conduct effective transfer learning across a range of NLP tasks. By incorporating special symbols within the input sequences, the model gains a better grasp of information pertinent to specific tasks, which boosts its performance in downstream applications. It is noteworthy that the precise form of these special markers can vary depending on the design of the model and the requirements of the specific tasks. For instance, the original GPT paper indicates that every task-specific input transformation includes the addition of randomly initialized start and end tokens.

For these transformations to be effective, it is vital to maintain consistency in the vocabulary and encoding schemes used during both the pretraining and fine-tuning phases. This involves setting aside some vocabulary space for special symbols during pretraining and employing the same vocabulary and encoding scheme for encoding input sequences during fine-tuning. Such consistency ensures that the model continues to exhibit a uniform understanding and representation of language throughout the transfer learning process.

In conclusion, tailoring input sequences to different NLP tasks not only helps the model comprehend task-specific information more effectively but also enhances its performance in subsequent tasks. Through these input transformation techniques, GPT models can effectively engage in transfer learning across various NLP tasks, achieving superior NLP performance.

3.4.3 Fine-tuning steps

The supervised fine-tuning process involves several crucial steps:

  1. 1. Target task selection: Determine a specific NLP task based on requirements, such as sentiment analysis, text classification, or named entity recognition.
  2. 2. Training data preparation: Collect annotated data pertinent to the chosen task, typically comprising input sequences (like text snippets) and their corresponding labels (e.g., sentiment or category labels).
  3. 3. Model initialization: Start with the model parameters acquired during GPT to ensure the model has robust language representation capabilities from the beginning of the fine-tuning phase.
  4. 4. Model fine-tuning: Utilize task-related data and the loss function L′(θ) to fine-tune the model. Use optimization algorithms like SGD or others to update the parameters, incorporating regularization techniques such as L2 regularization to adjust the regularization coefficient λ and prevent overfitting.
  5. 5. Model performance evaluation: Measure the fine-tuned model’s performance on a task-specific validation set using metrics such as accuracy or F1 score.
  6. 6. Hyperparameter adjustment: Fine-tune the model’s hyperparameters, like the learning rate or regularization coefficient, based on performance metrics from the validation set to enhance the model’s efficacy.
  7. 7. Model application: Deploy the fine-tuned model to tackle real-world problems, process new inputs, and produce predictions for specific tasks.

The model can be tailored according to the task and data characteristics. For instance, in text classification tasks, a fully connected layer can be added to the final layer to generate task-specific predictions. In named entity recognition, a conditional random field layer might be integrated into the output layer to better capture label sequence dependencies. This adaptability makes the GPT model versatile across various NLP tasks.

GPT’s fine-tuning typically delivers robust performance with relatively few annotated data, benefiting significantly from the rich linguistic insights gained during pretraining. Thus supervised fine-tuning is effectively refining the pretrained model for specific tasks using limited annotated data.

In essence, supervised fine-tuning is pivotal for leveraging GPT models in specific NLP tasks. It involves optimizing the model on task-relevant data using learned language representations to achieve optimal outcomes. This process includes fine-tuning the loss function L′(θ) (Eqs. 3.3–3.7) and employing regularization to prevent overfitting. Furthermore, the model’s architecture can be modified to suit different NLP tasks, enhancing its adaptability.

3.5 Summary

The GPT models that form the basis of the GPT series, discussed in this chapter, have been instrumental in shaping the technological framework of ChatGPT. GPT’s training regimen is structured into two principal phases: unsupervised pretraining and supervised fine-tuning. This dual-phase approach offers an effective training paradigm for ChatGPT. During unsupervised pretraining on vast quantities of unlabeled text, ChatGPT develops a nuanced understanding of language. In the subsequent supervised fine-tuning phase, it is tailored to excel at specific tasks, significantly boosting its efficacy in targeted areas.

The architecture used by GPT, which consists solely of a Transformer decoder and masked self-attention mechanisms, serves as a critical blueprint for ChatGPT’s design. These elements are not only capable of advanced representation learning and efficient parallel processing, but they also ensure that the generation of text is influenced only by the current position and preceding context. This focus helps ChatGPT generate responses that are both coherent and meaningful.

Chapter 4 Unsupervised multitask and zero-shot learning

Abstract

This chapter delves into unsupervised multitask learning (MTL) and zero-shot learning, focusing on their implementation in OpenAI’s GPT-2 model. MTL enhances model generalization by training on multiple related tasks, while zero-shot learning enables task performance without specific training data. The chapter explores the Transformer architecture, contrasting BERT’s bidirectional encoder with GPT-2 unidirectional decoder. Key advancements in GPT-2 include layer normalization for stability, orthogonal initialization for gradient preservation, byte-pair encoding for efficient tokenization, and learnable relative positional encodings for better context understanding. The unsupervised pretraining of GPT-2 allows it to perform various natural language processing tasks without fine-tuning, demonstrating the synergy between MTL and zero-shot learning. The chapter concludes by highlighting GPT-2’s autoregressive generation process and its impact on subsequent models like GPT-3.

Keywords

Unsupervised learning; multitask learning; zero-shot learning; transformer architecture; GPT-2; bidirectional encoder representations from transformers (BERT); layer normalization; orthogonal initialization; byte-pair encoding (BPE); autoregressive generation

Multitask learning (MTL) is a strategic approach in machine learning that focuses on training models on several related tasks at once. By sharing insights across these tasks, the model’s ability to generalize and adapt to new scenarios is significantly enhanced. OpenAI highlighted this technique, alongside zero-shot learning, in their GPT-2 paper.

Zero-shot learning, a subset of unsupervised MTL, allows models to tackle tasks without specific training data. Instead, these models utilize a broad language model learned beforehand. The beauty of zero-shot learning lies in its cost-effectiveness, as it skips the need for task-specific tuning, making the model versatile across unfamiliar tasks. This chapter dives deep into the modifications of the Transformer architecture in GPT-2, explores the principles behind unsupervised MTL and zero-shot learning, and unpacks these concepts in depth.

4.1 Encoder and decoder

In October 2018, OpenAI unveiled GPT, followed 6 months later by Google with BERT. These models are based on the Transformer architecture, which consists of encoders and decoders stacked in multiple layers. Fig. 4.1 illustrates BERT as a pretrained language model built on Transformer’s encoder architecture, while GPT leverages the decoder side.

On the left, the BERT model is shown with input embeddings followed by positional encoding. The architecture includes layers of multi-head attention, add and norm and feed-forward components. On the right, the GPT model is depicted with output embeddings followed by positional encoding. The architecture includes masked multi-head attention, add and norm, multi-head attention, feed-forward layers and output probabilities. Both models have N layers, indicated by N times. The BERT model processes inputs, while the GPT model processes outputs shifted right.

BERT features a bidirectional approach, analyzing the words before and after a target word to predict its context. It employs a strategy known as masked language model, where random words in a sentence are hidden to make the model infer them based on the whole sentence’s context. This technique enhances BERT’s ability to grasp context from both directions, leading to notable improvements across various natural language processing (NLP) tasks.

After BERT’s success, OpenAI introduced GPT-2, an advanced version of GPT. GPT-2 maintains a unidirectional flow from left to right during its unsupervised pretraining. The model size of GPT-2 medium version matches that of BERT large, yet their architectures and training techniques greatly differ, affecting their performance in specific tasks. As depicted in Fig. 4.2, BERT utilizes a bidirectional encoder using self-attention to process text context, excelling in tasks that demand an understanding of sentence structure and contextual relationships, like question answering, entity recognition, and relation extraction.

The left diagram is labeled Self-Attention and shows a rectangular block with an arrow pointing upwards from a central circle. Below the circle, a triangular area is connected to a series of smaller rectangles. The right diagram is labeled Masked Self-Attention and is similar to the left diagram but with some of the smaller rectangles at the bottom right being shaded differently, indicating masking.

Conversely, GPT-2 features a unidirectional decoder that uses a masked self-attention mechanism to focus primarily on learning through language modeling, which might limit its bidirectional context comprehension compared to BERT. However, GPT-2 performs exceptionally well in various language generation tasks, including text summarization, translation, and dialog creation. Despite their differences, both models are recognized as robust tools in NLP, each excelling in specific tasks where they are most effective.

4.2 GPT-2

GPT-2, the follow-up to the original GPT, retains the decoder architecture from the Transformer model but expands massively on the training data used. It was trained on a hefty 40 GB dataset named WebText, gathered by the OpenAI team. Fig. 4.3 highlights the varied sizes of GPT-2 models used during training, ranging from 117 MB (small) to 1542 MB (extra-large) in parameter size. The smallest version comprises 12 decoder layers, needing 500 MB of storage for its parameters. On the other hand, the largest version features 48 decoder layers, with its parameter volume 13 times greater than the smallest, necessitating 6.5 GB of storage. Fig. 4.4 illustrates that GPT-2 architecture has significantly evolved from that of GPT-1, reflecting major advancements in its design and capabilities.

The image compares four GPT-2 models: Small Model, Medium Model, Large Model and Extra Large Model. The GPT-2 Small Model has a model dimension of 768 and includes 12 decoders. The GPT-2 Medium Model has a model dimension of 1024 and includes 24 decoders. The GPT-2 Large Model has a model dimension of 1280 and includes 36 decoders. The GPT-2 Extra Large Model has a model dimension of 1600 and includes 48 decoders.

At the bottom, there is Text and Positional Embedding. Above it, there is a block labeled Layer Normalization followed by Masked Multi-Head Self-Attention. Another Layer Normalization block follows, leading to a Feedforward Network. This sequence is repeated 12 times. Above these repeated blocks, there is a Transformer block. At the top, there is another Layer Normalization block, which branches into Text Prediction and Task Classification.

4.2.1 Layer normalization

Layer normalization (LayerNorm) is a technique used to introduce normalization operations within the layers of neural networks, particularly evident in GPT-2’s Transformer layers. Unlike its predecessor, GPT-1, GPT-2 incorporates a LayerNorm layer at both the input and output of each Transformer layer, ensuring normalization of these points. This method effectively combats common neural network training challenges such as gradient vanishing and explosion, thereby bolstering training stability and speeding up convergence.

Gradient issues, particularly vanishing and exploding gradients, are prevalent in deep neural networks due to the multilayer gradient propagation. Layer normalization counters this by standardizing the input data’s mean and variance to 0 and 1, respectively, ensuring uniformity across scales.

As illustrated in Fig. 4.5, normalization adjusts and balances the input data, setting its mean to 0 and variance to 1, which minimizes scale disparities. This adjustment not only facilitates smoother gradient flow during backpropagation but also stabilizes gradient variations throughout the training phase. Moreover, layer normalization tackles covariate shift—a scenario where training and testing data distributions do not align—by equating the scale of both datasets, thus boosting the model’s generalization capabilities. Ultimately, normalization ensures uniform gradient scaling across all dimensions, aiding optimizers in navigating the optimal direction for weight updates more effectively and accelerating the model’s convergence and overall training performance.

On the left, Batch Normalization is shown with a batch of training examples. Each example has four features and the mean and variance are calculated for each feature across the batch. The mean values are 3, 2, 4 and 3 and the variance values are 3, 2, 3 and 1. A note indicates All Training Examples are the Same. On the right, Layer Normalization is shown with the same batch of training examples. The mean and variance are calculated for each example across all features. The mean values are 2, 3 and 3 and the variance values are 2, 2 and 2. A note indicates All Feature Dimensions are the Same.

4.2.2 Orthogonal initialization

Orthogonal Initialization is a method employed to set up neural network parameters, mainly focusing on the weight matrices. It initializes these matrices as orthogonal matrices to combat issues like gradient vanishing or explosion during the training phase.

GPT-2 follows a similar weight initialization strategy as GPT-1, specifically utilizing orthogonal initialization to ensure gradient stability throughout training. Orthogonal matrices, by design, consist of row and column vectors that are unit vectors and orthogonal to each other, with the property that the inverse of such a matrix is its transpose (A−1=AT). These matrices are known for their robust numerical characteristics, such as having a condition number of 1, ensuring that they do not exacerbate errors in matrix calculations.

The key benefit of orthogonal initialization lies in its ability to stabilize gradients. During neural network operations, gradients need to traverse through various weight matrices. Improper initialization could result in gradients that either vanish (become too small) or explode (become excessively large), adversely impacting model training and convergence. Orthogonal matrices help mitigate this risk because their products remain orthogonal, maintaining consistent gradient norms across different layers.

Fig. 4.6 illustrates an example of orthogonal initialization. The process begins by creating a random matrix with dimensions (n, n), each element drawn from a standard normal distribution. This matrix is then subjected to QR decomposition (A=QR), where Q represents an orthogonal matrix and R an upper triangular matrix. The Q matrix from this decomposition serves as the initialized weight matrix, possessing favorable numerical qualities that contribute to gradient stability.

Matrix A is represented by a square matrix with dots indicating the continuation of elements. Matrix Q is represented by a rectangular matrix with columns q sub 1, q sub 2, through q sub n, where each column is separated by vertical lines. Matrix R is represented by an upper triangular matrix shaded in gray.

4.2.3 Reversible tokenization

All models in the GPT series use a reversible tokenization method based on data compression known as byte-pair encoding (BPE). This algorithm reduces the vocabulary size by combining frequently appearing word pairs into new units. Since the tokenized text can be entirely reconstructed during decoding, this method is described as reversible.

The fundamental concept behind the BPE algorithm is to consolidate commonly occurring character combinations, like words or phrases, into new symbols. This process not only shrinks the vocabulary but also enhances the model’s ability to generalize, efficiently manages rare words and morphological changes, thereby boosting overall performance. The detailed steps involved in the BPE algorithm are as follows:

  1. 1. Vocabulary initialization: Initially, incorporate every word (or character) from the text into the vocabulary, with each entry representing a single character.
  2. 2. Frequency counting of character pairs: Assess the frequency of each adjacent character pair within the text. These frequency counts help decide which pairs to merge into new symbols.
  3. 3. Merge high-frequency character pairs: Find and merge the most frequently occurring character pair into a new symbol. This new symbol is then added to the vocabulary, and the text is updated to substitute the original character pairs with this new symbol.
  4. 4. Iterative merging: Continue the process of counting and merging as needed, until reaching the predefined number of merges or the desired vocabulary size. Each cycle focuses on merging a new high-frequency pair, progressively enlarging the vocabulary to encompass longer subwords or phrases.

Here’s an example to illustrate BPE:

  1. 1. Start with a corpus and count the frequency of each word. The frequencies are noted in the format “[frequency]word_”, where “_” signifies the end of a word. For instance, the corpus might have: Corpus: [7]old_, [3]older_, [9]finest_, [4]lowest_.
  2. 2. Determine the token vocabulary size or the number of iterations to stop the algorithm.
  3. 3. Count the frequency of each character, including the end symbol “_”. Refer to Table 4.1.
  4. 4. Merge the two consecutive characters (or sequences) with the highest frequency. For example, in the first iteration, “e” and “s” might be merged into “es” since they appear together 13 times (9 + 4). Add “es” and its frequency to the vocabulary, and decrease the counts for “e” and “s.”
  5. 5. In the next iterations, “es” and “t” could be merged because “est” appears 13 times, which is the highest frequency. Update the vocabulary to include “est” and reduce “t,” keeping the vocabulary length the same.
  6. 6. Merge “est_,” also appearing 13 times. Merging with the termination marker is crucial. It helps distinguish words like “estimate” and “highest.” Both contain “est,” but one ends with “est_,” and the other starts with it. The algorithm treats “est” and “est_” differently, recognizing “est_” as part of “highest” and not “estimate,” as shown in Table 4.2.
  7. 7. Merge “ol,” which appears 10 times.
  8. 8. If the iteration count is set to four, the final vocabulary would be (_, o, l, d, r, f, i, n, w, est, ol, est_).

Table 4.1
