The description illustrates that the vocabulary length under BPE can increase, decrease, or remain stable. BPE efficiently compresses the original text into a shorter format while preserving patterns that frequently occur between characters. This method shines in NLP tasks where it excels at managing rare words and morphological variations by decomposing them into smaller subword units, improving model performance. In large-scale models like GPT-2, BPE facilitates better handling of diverse NLP tasks, enhancing both the model’s adaptability and effectiveness.

In terms of handling rare words, BPE excels by splitting infrequent words in training data into common subword units, enhancing the model’s ability to grasp their meanings. For instance, a seldom-seen word like “neuroscientific” could be segmented into “neuro,” “scien,” and “tific.” These subwords, appearing more frequently in training data, help the model easily acquire their semantic meanings.

BPE also effectively addresses morphological variations, which can inflate vocabulary sizes and complicate computations in many languages. By breaking down words like the English verbs “running” and “ran” into components such as “run,” “ning,” and “ran,” BPE enables the model to more adeptly learn about word roots and their morphological dynamics, simplifying the learning process and reducing computational demands.

The use of the BPE algorithm with Chinese text is notably more intricate than with English due to the fundamental differences in their vocabularies. In Chinese, each character typically has a distinct meaning, and words are often formed by combining these characters, each adding layers of meaning.

However, BPE faces some challenges with Chinese that it doesn’t with English. Unlike English, where morphological changes often involve clear prefixes and suffixes, Chinese variations are less straightforward, limiting the effectiveness of BPE in some scenarios. Addressing these challenges requires developing models and algorithms that are specifically geared towards the unique properties of the Chinese language.

In essence, the adoption of BPE in GPT series models significantly shrinks the vocabulary size, which in turn reduces the number of parameters the models need to learn. This reduction eases computational demands and accelerates both training and inference processes. Additionally, by leveraging the shared characteristics among subword units, these models gain enhanced capabilities to understand and represent the semantic details of words, boosting their effectiveness across a variety of NLP applications.

Moreover, BPE improves the GPT models’ handling of unfamiliar words. When encountering such words, the models can break them down into recognizable subword components to deduce their meanings, enhancing the models’ ability to generalize and manage new or uncommon words effectively.

In the context of transfer learning, BPE’s integration makes the GPT series especially potent. The models’ ability to learn and utilize commonalities among subword units across different tasks translates into superior performance in transfer learning scenarios, thus bolstering their flexibility across diverse NLP challenges.

For multilingual tasks, BPE’s role is crucial in enabling the GPT models to adeptly manage linguistic diversity. By identifying and utilizing common roots and morphological variations across languages, BPE equips GPT-2 with the ability to grasp and process multiple languages, leading to improved outcomes in multilingual contexts. This capability is particularly beneficial in today’s globally interconnected digital landscape.

BPE significantly boosts model efficacy in NLP tasks by reducing vocabulary size and, consequently, the number of parameters a model needs to master. This reduction not only mitigates the risk of overfitting but also allows models to more effectively grasp the semantic nuances of words. This enhanced understanding proves beneficial across a range of applications, from machine translation to semantic analysis and text generation.

Notably, starting with GPT-2, a variation of BPE known as byte-level BPE (BBPE) is employed. This adaptation extends the traditional BPE approach from character level to byte level. For instance, while traditional BPE struggles with the expansive Unicode character set, BBPE simplifies this by treating each byte as a fundamental processing unit, effectively limiting the basic character set to 256 (28). This approach facilitates vocabulary sharing across languages and reduces vocabulary size. However, a drawback emerges with languages like Chinese, where BBPE can considerably lengthen text sequences. As a result, while BBPE might outperform BPE on the same dataset, it can also lead to longer training and inference durations due to the increased sequence length.

4.2.4 Learnable relative positional encoding

GPT-2, built on the Transformer architecture, incorporates a learnable relative positional encoding strategy to precisely capture positional information within input sequences. This approach uniquely considers both the absolute distances and the relative positions between tokens, greatly improving the model’s ability to understand long-range dependencies.

In GPT-2, relative positional encodings are seamlessly integrated into the self-attention mechanism and used alongside the Q, K, and V matrices to capture token relationships accurately. When calculating self-attention scores, the model evaluates not only the content relevance between tokens (through the dot product of Q and K) but also their relative positions (via relative positional encodings). These encodings are expressed through parameterized functions, such as sine and cosine functions, whose parameters are learned and optimized during the model training process.

To highlight the differences between the relative positional encodings in GPT-2 and the traditional absolute positional encodings in the Transformer, consider an example with the sentence “The cat is cute.” Initially, this sentence is tokenized into [The, cat, is, cute,.]. In the traditional Transformer, this process involves adding a precomputed matrix based on sine and cosine functions to the embedding vectors, which imparts absolute positional information to the model.

GPT-2, however, approaches this differently. It doesn’t simply add positional encodings to the embeddings but integrates relative positional encodings during the self-attention calculations. These encodings are adaptable and based on the relative distances between tokens—for instance, the relative distance between “cat” and “cute” is 2. In the computation of attention weights, these relative distances are combined with the content relevance between tokens, empowering the model to leverage positional information to refine the effectiveness of its self-attention mechanism.

While the traditional Transformer model utilizes a fixed matrix of absolute positional encodings added directly to embeddings, GPT-2 opts for a dynamic, more sophisticated strategy that intertwines learnable relative positional encodings with the self-attention process. This distinction marks a significant evolution in how positional information is utilized to enhance text processing capabilities in models.

4.3 Unsupervised multitask learning

GPT-2’s unsupervised pretraining leverages the extensive WebText dataset, comprising about 450,000 web articles collected via OpenAI’s web crawler. After rigorous cleaning and filtering, this dataset encompasses around 40 GB, equivalent to nearly 8 billion words. This considerable increase in dataset size over GPT-1’s dataset facilitates the development of more robust models.

Illustrated in Fig. 4.7, while multitask supervised learning focuses on predicting labels for unseen data based on labeled examples, GPT-2’s approach involves generating text that mimics the training data, all through unsupervised learning. Although not explicitly detailed in the literature, the core methodologies of GPT-1 and GPT-2 in pretraining are quite similar, both using extensive unlabeled text data within a Transformer framework.

On the left side, there are three rows, each with a Data Input leading to Model 1, Model 2, and Model k, respectively, which then lead to Prediction 1, Prediction 2, and Prediction k. On the right side, a single Data Input leads to Multi-Task Learning, which then branches out to Prediction 1, Prediction 2, and Prediction k.

During its pretraining, GPT-2 employs a maximum likelihood estimation technique, guiding the model to predict subsequent words based on the context provided. This phase is crucial as it allows the model to assimilate vast linguistic knowledge and semantic nuances from the text it processes.

GPT-2’s strategy of unsupervised MTL is designed to enhance the model’s versatility and generalization by extracting and applying shared insights across various tasks from extensive text data. Though inherently unsupervised, GPT-2 often undergoes supervised fine-tuning for specific applications, enhancing its adaptability to particular tasks. This fine-tuning, while beneficial for targeted applications, typically restricts the model’s broader applicability to tasks similar to those it was specifically tuned for.

4.4 The relationship between multitask and zero-shot learning

As depicted in Fig. 4.8, zero-shot learning in sample learning allows a model to tackle tasks using prior knowledge without any task-specific supervised training. GPT-2’s zero-shot learning ability stems from unsupervised MTL during its pretraining phase. In NLP, zero-shot learning can be viewed as unsupervised transfer across different subdomains. This implies that the knowledge the model gains during pretraining can be applied to various NLP tasks without needing specific supervised fine-tuning. However, zero-shot learning generally underperforms compared with supervised fine-tuning because it doesn’t use labeled data for the specific tasks.

In GPT-2, MTL involves absorbing a large amount of natural language text during the unsupervised pretraining phase, capturing rich linguistic structures and semantic information. Zero-shot learning enables GPT-2 to handle various NLP tasks without specific task labels. MTL and zero-shot learning in GPT-2 complement each other. Zero-shot learning leverages the knowledge acquired during the unsupervised pretraining phase, allowing it to manage multiple tasks without additional supervised training. This approach offers GPT-2 broad application prospects. The following sections will explore the applications of zero-shot learning in GPT-2 through three specific tasks: reading comprehension, text summarization, and sentiment analysis.

In a reading comprehension task, the model must generate answers to questions based on provided text. With zero-shot learning, GPT-2 does not receive task-specific fine-tuning. Instead, the question and text are input together, and the model generates the answer. The key lies in organizing the question and text into a sequence effectively. A common method is to concatenate the question with the given text segment, inserting a specific separator between them. For instance:

Question: How many planets are there in the solar system? Text: The solar system includes one star (the Sun) and eight planets. This can be organized as: “How many planets are there in the solar system? <SEP> The solar system includes one star (the Sun) and eight planets.” When this organized sequence is provided to the GPT-2 model, it may generate the answer “eight.”

For a text summarization task, the model needs to extract key information from a longer text to create a concise summary. In zero-shot learning, this can be achieved by clearly specifying the task in the input sequence. For example, given the following text: Text: Computer science is the study of computers and their systems. Researchers in this field study not only the design and development of computer hardware but also programming languages, software design, algorithms, and other theories and practices related to computing.

You would concatenate the task requirement with the text and input it into the model: “Generate a summary of the following text: Computer science is the study of computers and their systems. Researchers in this field study not only the design and development of computer hardware but also programming languages, software design, algorithms, and other theories and practices related to computing.”

At this point, the GPT-2 model might produce a summary like: “Computer science studies computer systems, hardware design, software design, and algorithms.” Note that since the model hasn’t undergone supervised training specifically for text summarization, the quality of the generated summary might not be as high as that of a model trained with supervision. Nonetheless, this still showcases the model’s robust capability to perform multiple tasks without specific task training.

In a sentiment analysis task, the model must recognize and understand the sentiment expressed in text, such as whether it is positive, negative, or neutral. In zero-shot learning, GPT-2 can generate sentences that capture the sentiment of the input text. For instance, given a product review: “The battery life of this phone is very long, and the camera performance is excellent,” you can prompt GPT-2 with: “The battery life of this phone is very long, and the camera performance is excellent. What is the sentiment of this review?” GPT-2 might respond with: “The sentiment of this review is positive.” This shows that GPT-2 can effectively perform sentiment analysis without specific task training.

GPT-2 has shown outstanding performance across various zero-shot learning tasks. For example, in reading comprehension, GPT-2 achieved top results in the LAMBADA language modeling task without any supervised fine-tuning. In semantic similarity tasks, the STS-B (Semantic Textual Similarity Benchmark) task highlighted GPT-2’s strong zero-shot capabilities. In translation tasks, GPT-2 demonstrated a notable degree of translation ability in English-French and English-German translations, despite not being fine-tuned for these tasks. The paper also discusses GPT-2’s zero-shot learning performance in other NLP tasks like question answering and summarization. Although specific experimental setups for these tasks are not detailed in the paper, they were used to validate GPT-2’s zero-shot learning abilities.

4.5 The autoregressive generation process of GPT-2

During its unsupervised pretraining phase, GPT-2 absorbed a vast amount of knowledge from extensive natural language texts. This process optimized the model’s weight matrix, enabling it to excel in language modeling tasks. In zero-shot learning applications, this pretrained model is used as is, without any task-specific fine-tuning. This means that when tackling NLP tasks, the weight matrix remains unchanged and is not further modified.

Furthermore, a vocabulary was employed during pretraining. Initially, each word’s embedding vector was randomly generated, but these vectors were refined during training to maximize performance in language modeling. After pretraining, the updated embeddings for each word reflect the language patterns the model learned from the large corpus.

For specific NLP tasks, the pretrained GPT-2 model is typically fine-tuned. This fine-tuning process can alter the model’s weight matrix to better suit the task. However, in a zero-shot learning scenario, the model’s weights stay the same.

4.5.1 Subword unit embeddings

The GPT-2 model employs BBPE for preprocessing input text, breaking it into “subword units” rather than traditional words. BBPE treats each byte as the basic processing unit, so the embedding vector for each subword unit (token) is 1 byte long. Notably, because BBPE splits text into byte-level subword units, a word can be divided into multiple subword units, each with its corresponding embedding vector. Thus the GPT-2 model’s vocabulary is generated from the statistical information in the pretraining corpus, accounting for common vocabulary and word structures.

The length of the input token sequence in the GPT-2 model varies with its version. As shown in Fig. 4.9, the input token sequence length is 768 in the smaller GPT-2 model (i.e., GPT-2 small), while in larger models, it can extend to 1024 or 2048.

The model vocabulary size is 50,257. The token sequence length is shown as 768 (Small), 1024 (Medium), 1280 (Large) and 1600 (Extra Large). The embeddings are represented in a matrix with values such as 3.5, 2.1, 6.2, negative 1.3, negative 1.1, 3.5, negative 1.2, 4.8, 1.7, 5.4, 2.2, 1.6, negataive 1.6, 1.9, 5.5, negative 1.4, 5.2, 3.3, 3.2, 6.3, 5.1, 6.5, 2.3, 3.1, negative 1.9, 5.0, 1.4, 4.6. The subwords listed are un, believ, able, lean, learn, dot dot dot, ing.

During GPT-2’s pretraining, the embedding representations of the vocabulary are trained through autoregressive prediction on the input sequence. This means that the model adjusts the embedding representations of tokens in the vocabulary based on the context and prediction targets to optimize prediction performance. This training process updates the embedding parameters, allowing them to better capture semantic and syntactic information.

It is crucial to note that after pretraining is complete, the vocabulary’s embedding representations are fixed when the model is applied and are not updated further. The aim of pretraining is to provide the model with initial parameters that have strong semantic representation capabilities, not to dynamically update the embeddings during actual use. Since the model is pretrained on a large corpus, these embedding vectors typically adapt well to various NLP tasks.

4.5.2 Autoregressive process

GPT-2 is a trained model that includes the decoder weights of the Transformer, the token embedding matrix, and the positional encoding matrix. As a typical autoregressive generative model, GPT-2 can process inputs up to 1024 tokens long and generate sequences in an autoregressive manner starting from the input token. However, the sequences generated in this way often lack meaningful themes. Therefore in practical applications, GPT-2 often uses zero-shot learning, where it receives an instructional prompt (without examples) and generates text around a specific theme.

For instance, the input prompt might be “Large datasets are crucial for training these models.” As shown in Fig. 4.10, before the input sequence enters the Transformer model, it needs to be converted into corresponding token representation vectors by adding the word embedding vector to the positional encoding. The Transformer model comprises multiple decoder components, with each layer of the token sequence first processed by a self-attention layer and then by a neural network layer before being passed to the next decoder layer. Although the processing flow of each decoder component is identical, the self-attention and neural network layers of each component have independent weights.

At the top, there is a representation of Decoder number 12, Position number 1, Output Vector being multiplied by Subword Unit Embedding Token Embedding to produce Output Subword Probabilities (Logits). The probabilities are shown for different subwords like I, you, he and others, with the highest probability subword being selected. Below this, a larger box labeled Decoder times N contains a Feedforward Neural Network and a Masked Self-Attention layer. The Masked Self-Attention layer shows percentages for different words in a sentence: Large datasets are crucial for training these models. The percentages indicate the attention weights for each word. At the bottom, there is a sequence of words with corresponding positions from 1 to 1024, showing the input sentence Large datasets are crucial for training these models.

When the model’s final decoder produces an output vector, this vector is multiplied by the token embedding matrix. The result, after normalization, can be considered the cosine similarity between the two vectors. This implies that the closer the output vector is to a vector in the token embedding matrix, the larger their dot product result will be. The normalized result can be interpreted as the probability or score of selecting a vector from the token embedding matrix. Typically, the word with the highest score is selected (top_k=1). However, to achieve better results, other high-scoring words can also be considered. Thus a common strategy is to perform random sampling based on these scores, increasing the likelihood of selecting high-scoring words. Another common strategy is to set top_k to N and choose the top N highest-scoring words. This way, the model completes one iteration and outputs a word. The model continues to iterate until it generates the entire context (up to 1024 tokens for GPT-2) or until it generates the token indicating the end of the sequence <e>.

4.6 Summary

The introduction of GPT-2 did not resolve the ongoing debate over encoder versus decoder architectures, but its success in MTL has offered the research community a fresh perspective. There is now a growing emphasis on effectively utilizing vast amounts of unstructured text data in unsupervised learning environments. Researchers are also looking into optimizing model architectures and refining training strategies to boost performance in MTL and zero-shot learning.

Shortly after GPT-2, OpenAI launched the more advanced GPT-3 model, which has shown exceptional performance across various NLP tasks. Astonishingly, GPT-3 contains 175 billion parameters, an order of magnitude greater than the largest version of GPT-2. Given the impracticality of supervised multitask fine-tuning on such a massive scale, the zero-shot learning approach introduced by GPT-2 has proven crucial for GPT-3, offering new insights and perspectives in the realm of NLP.

Chapter 5 Sparse attention and content-based learning

Abstract

This chapter delves into the architecture and learning strategies of GPT-3, emphasizing its Sparse Transformer design and content-based learning. GPT-3, with 175 billion parameters, introduces a hybrid attention mechanism alternating between dense and locally banded sparse attention patterns, optimizing its efficiency in handling long sequences. The chapter explores the Sparse Transformer’s ability to reduce computational complexity, enhancing GPT-3 scalability and performance across various natural language processing tasks. Additionally, it discusses metalearning and in-context learning as key strategies for task adaptation without extensive retraining. The chapter also covers Bayesian inference as a framework for understanding contextual learning and introduces the chain-of-thought approach, which enhances reasoning capabilities through step-by-step problem-solving techniques. These innovations enable GPT-3 to perform complex reasoning tasks effectively, demonstrating its advanced capabilities in natural language understanding and generation.

Keywords

GPT-3; Sparse transformer; content-based learning; in-context learning; Bayesian inference; chain of thought; meta-learning; sparse attention; few-shot learning

In May 2020, OpenAI unveiled GPT-3, a groundbreaking language model. GPT-3, like its predecessors GPT and GPT-2, is built on a Transformer-based decoder architecture. However, it features an innovative Sparse Transformer pattern that alternates between dense and locally banded sparse attention mechanisms across its layers. This innovation significantly boosts GPT-3’s efficiency in processing long sequences.

Boasting an impressive 175 billion parameters, GPT-3 stands as one of the largest Transformer models ever created. Its expanded capacity has notably enhanced its performance across a variety of natural language processing (NLP) tasks. Yet, this scale comes with high training costs—estimated at $1.4 million by Guosheng Securities—which makes it economically challenging to fine-tune such large models using supervised data for specific tasks. Instead, GPT-3 focuses on in-context learning. In this approach, the model adapts to new tasks using just a few examples, without extensive retraining of its parameters. This allows GPT-3 to quickly assimilate task-specific knowledge even from minimal data, showcasing remarkable performance on new challenges. This chapter will explore GPT-3 advanced Sparse Transformer pattern and the principles of in-context learning in greater detail.

5.1 GPT-3

OpenAI’s GPT-3 builds on the foundational architecture of the GPT series, incorporating techniques such as orthogonal initialization, layer normalization, and the byte-level byte-pair encoding (BBPE) tokenization method. These features collectively enhance the training efficiency and generative power of the model. A notable upgrade in GPT-3 lies in its attention mechanism: as depicted in Fig. 5.1, it employs a hybrid of dense and locally banded sparse attention within the Transformer’s decoder layers. This method, inspired by Sparse Transformers, deviates from the traditional fully connected attention used in GPT-2. Such improvements allow GPT-3 to handle longer sequences more efficiently, reducing both computational demands and memory footprint, thereby boosting the model’s scalability.

The neural network architecture starting with Token Embedding. Next is Relative Position Encoding, followed by Add and Norm. Then, there is a Transformer Layer (Dense Attention) with Multi-head Self-attention (Dense) and Position-wise Feedforward Neural Network. Another Add and Norm follows, leading to a Transformer Layer (Sparse Attention) with Multi-head Self-attention (Sparse) and Position-wise Feedforward Neural Network. The final components are Linear Layer and Softmax.

GPT-3, with its colossal 175 billion parameters, dwarfs GPT-2, which has only 1.5 billion. This vast increase in parameters has enabled GPT-3 to outperform on numerous tasks. Both models share a Transformer-based decoder structure, encompassing input, embedding, Transformer layers, and an output stage. Despite adopting GPT-2’s orthogonal initialization, layer normalization, and reversible tokenization, GPT-3 innovates with alternating dense and locally banded sparse attention across its Transformer layers. Additionally, GPT-3 extensive training dataset further boosts its ability to generalize across diverse NLP tasks. The architecture of GPT-3 includes the following:

  1. 1. Input and BBPE tokenization: Utilizing BBPE, a method that efficiently reduces vocabulary size by merging frequent word pairs into single units.
  2. 2. Embedding layer: This layer maps discrete tokens into continuous vector representations.
  3. 3. Positional encoding: Adds vital positional data to vectors, enabling the model to differentiate word positions and understand contextual relationships.
  4. 4. Transformer layers: Each layer is a stack of encoders comprising a sparse self-attention mechanism, alternating between dense and sparse patterns, and a feedforward network for abstracting higher-level features.
  5. 5. Layer normalization: Integrated at every Transformer layer’s input and output, it normalizes these points to stabilize training and speed up convergence.
  6. 6. Output layer: Transforms Transformer outputs into a probability distribution via a linear layer and a softmax function, predicting the next token by selecting the highest probability.

The primary distinction between GPT-3 and GPT-2 lies in the implementation of sparse attention within the model architecture. Additionally, GPT-3 adopts many of the design elements and techniques used in GPT-2. The significant increase in both the scale of GPT-3 and the size of its training dataset have contributed to its enhanced performance.

5.2 The sparse transformer

The Sparse Transformer represents a specialized adaptation of the standard Transformer model, designed to optimize the processing of long sequences. This model incorporates a sparse attention mechanism that cleverly cuts down on both computational complexity and memory demands, facilitating efficient management of lengthy data streams.

5.2.1 Characteristics of the sparse transformer

Traditional Transformer models struggle with processing long sequences due to the inherent fully connected setup of the self-attention mechanism, which causes both computational complexity and memory usage to scale quadratically with the sequence length, expressed as O(n2). This level of complexity becomes prohibitive for extensive sequences. The Sparse Transformer addresses this issue by not calculating attention scores for every pair of elements, thus enabling a more computationally sparse approach. There are two main strategies for this optimization: first, by limiting the reach of the attention mechanism with a defined attention window; second, by employing specific sparse connection patterns that can reduce the computational load and memory requirements to O(n).

5.2.1.1 Sparse attention patterns

Illustrated in Fig. 5.2, the Sparse Transformer capitalizes on sparse attention patterns. Under this framework, each input token selectively focuses on a subset of contextual information instead of every possible token, dramatically cutting the computational complexity from O(n2) to O(n). Sparse attention can be implemented in several ways, such as local banded attention, which concentrates on closely positioned tokens, and strided (sparse fully connected) attention, which samples the sequence at fixed steps throughout its entire length.

The Sparse Transformer capitalizes on sparse attention patterns. The image shows six grid patterns with different highlighted areas, representing various sparse attention patterns. The top row has four smaller grids and the bottom row has two larger grids. Each grid has a unique pattern of highlighted squares.

5.2.1.2 Alternating dense and sparse attention patterns

GPT-3 enhances its performance and generalizability by alternating between dense and sparse attention patterns in different Transformer layers, leveraging principles from Sparse Transformer designs. This strategic variation enables the model to effectively capture long-range dependencies without sacrificing computational efficiency. Practically, the balance between dense and sparse patterns can be tailored to fit the specific requirements of a task and the available computational resources.

5.2.1.3 Learnable relative positional encodings

GPT-3 employs learnable relative positional encodings that account for both the absolute and relative distances between tokens. This innovative approach significantly bolsters the model’s capability to understand and process long-range dependencies.

5.2.2 Local banded attention

Local banded attention in GPT-3 is designed to reduce the computational load and memory usage typical of traditional Transformer models. This sparse attention mechanism focuses each input token on just a portion of its neighbors instead of every token in the sequence. The implementation involves several key steps:

  1. 1. Determine window size: First, establish a window size that dictates the range of context each token considers. This parameter can be adapted to fit the needs of the task and the available computational power. Larger window sizes provide richer contextual details but require more computational resources.
  2. 2. Build the attention matrix: Construct a one-dimensional attention vector for each token that contains the weights for the adjacent tokens within its window. This process mirrors the conventional self-attention approach but is confined to the specified window. These vectors are then stacked to form a two-dimensional attention matrix.
  3. 3. Execute local banded attention: Multiply the attention matrix by the input sequence’s hidden states to generate outputs that include contextual information for each token, focusing only on immediate neighbors.

Local banded attention notably reduces the computational complexity from O(n2) to O(n×window size), efficiently balancing performance and contextual awareness. However, it might struggle with long-range dependencies. To overcome this limitation, it can be integrated with other sparse attention mechanisms, such as sparse fully connected attention, or used alternately with dense attention patterns across different Transformer layers.

5.2.3 Cross-layer sparse connections

Cross-layer sparse connections in GPT-3 are designed to forge longer-range dependencies between layers, optimizing the model’s handling of extended sequences. This approach improves the attention mechanism’s efficiency, enabling processing of larger inputs. GPT-3 implements cross-layer sparse connections through a blend of dense and locally banded sparse attention patterns, detailed as follows:

  1. 1. Local banded sparse connections: These connections focus each token on a nearby subset of tokens, facilitating the capture of immediate contextual relationships within a constrained scope.
  2. 2. Dense connections: Contrasting with the local approach, dense connections allow each token to engage with every other token in the sequence, broadening the scope to global dependencies.

Alternating these connection styles throughout the model layers, GPT-3 adeptly manages both near and far dependencies. The local banded approach targets adjacent layer interactions for nearer relationships, while dense connections bridge longer distances. This structured versatility not only boosts GPT-3 efficiency in processing lengthy sequences but also extends its capacity to recognize extensive relational patterns.

Furthermore, GPT-3 employs these cross-layer sparse connections to enhance its overall effectiveness in NLP tasks, simultaneously conserving computational resources. It achieves this through a strategic alternation of dense and sparse Transformer layers, where dense attention and local banded sparse attention layers interchange, optimizing both performance and computational efficiency.

By integrating sparse attention mechanisms, alternating attention patterns, and adaptive positional encodings, GPT-3 significantly alleviates the challenges faced by traditional Transformers with long sequences, positioning Sparse Transformer as a robust framework for extensive data handling. The model’s specific attention strategies, akin to those in Sparse Transformer, significantly elevate its capability in processing long texts.

5.3 Meta-learning and in-context learning

Due to the vast number of parameters in GPT-3 (175 billion), fine-tuning every single one for different downstream tasks has become unfeasible. Consequently, metalearning and in-context learning have emerged as the key strategies for leveraging this model. Meta-learning involves pretraining the model on an extensive text corpus to enhance its natural language generation skills and overall linguistic knowledge and comprehension. Throughout this phase, GPT-3 absorbs the intricacies of grammar across different language elements—words to paragraphs—and garners a foundational understanding of common knowledge.

In-context learning, alternatively, focuses on making slight adjustments to the model using a small subset of labeled data to tailor it to specific tasks. This method of fine-tuning doesn’t retrain the model extensively; instead, it leverages the broad capabilities developed during pretraining to meet the needs of particular applications. Examples of such data include question–answer pairs or sentiment-labeled sentences. This approach allows GPT-3 to achieve high performance in a variety of NLP tasks with just minimal labeled data.

5.3.1 Meta-learning

In the GPT-3 paper, the authors reinterpreted the concept of metalearning, differentiating it from its traditional definition. Traditionally, metalearning refers to training a model to quickly learn new tasks with a small amount of labeled data. However, the GPT-3 paper expands the definition of metalearning to include the process of unsupervised pretraining.

In this context, large pretrained language models like GPT-3 itself are considered metalearners because they have already acquired a vast amount of knowledge and language understanding during the pretraining phase. By pretraining on a large corpus of unlabeled text data, the model learns the structure of language and common-sense knowledge, thus gaining some generalization ability.

This new interpretation of metalearning is viable because GPT-3 has demonstrated strong generalization capabilities during its pretraining phase. This enables GPT-3 to perform excellently on new tasks with minimal labeled data (or even no data), similar to traditional metalearning methods. However, in GPT-3’s case, this capability primarily stems from the unsupervised pretraining phase, rather than from a specialized training process tailored for metalearning tasks.

5.3.2 In-context learning

To minimize adjustments to its parameters during the fine-tuning phase, GPT-3 focuses more on in-context learning strategies. In the task adaptation stage, the model adopts zero-shot, one-shot, and few-shot learning approaches, circumventing the constraints associated with traditional fine-tuning techniques.

As depicted on the right side of Fig. 5.3, the fine-tuning process is initiated after the model’s pretraining phase is complete. For each specific task, a subset of training samples is provided, such as the single-sample batch used in this instance. By computing the loss and updating its weights, the model is able to achieve commendable performance on new tasks. Fine-tuning generally demands fewer data compared to starting from scratch and permits lower learning rates. This efficiency is possible because fine-tuning starts from a pretrained model’s parameters, which are already close to the optimal solution for the tasks at hand.

The model predicts answers solely based on a natural language description of the given task, without performing gradient updates. Task Description: Translate English into French colon Task Content colon cheese becomes cheese. One-shot Learning colon In addition to the task description, a single example is provided, without performing gradient updates. Task Description colon Translate English into French colon Example colon sea otter becomes loutre de mer Task Content colon cheese becomes cheese. Few-shot Learning colon In addition to the task description, the model is exposed to some task examples, without performing gradient updates. Task Description colon Translate English into French colon Example colon sea otter becomes loutre de mer Example colon Peppermint becomes menthe poivrée Example colon plush giraffe becomes girafe en peluche Task Content colon cheese becomes cheese. Conventional Fine-tuning colon Fine-tuning colon The model is trained through repeated gradient updates using a large number of example tasks. Example number one colon sea otter becomes loutre de mer Gradient Update Example number two colon Peppermint becomes menthe poivrée Gradient Update Example number three colon plush giraffe becomes girafe en peluche Gradient Update Prompt colon cheese becomes cheese.

Fig. 5.3 on the left illustrates how GPT-3 employs varying degrees of example-based in-context learning techniques—zero-shot, one-shot, and few-shot—to boost its performance on new tasks without modifying its parameters. This strategy greatly simplifies the use of large language models, making it easier for users to apply the model across diverse tasks with minimal setup.

Zero-shot learning in GPT-3 involves tackling tasks without prior exposure to any related labeled data, relying entirely on knowledge gleaned from its extensive pretraining. In this mode, the model must interpret the task’s description and generate appropriate responses without example prompts. The main hurdle here is enabling the model to grasp the task requirements thoroughly and produce suitable responses solely based on its pretrained intelligence.

GPT-3 prowess in zero-shot learning stems from its colossal architecture, which incorporates up to 175 billion parameters, and its pretraining on vast quantities of unlabeled text. This extensive background allows it to understand intricate language patterns and common-sense contexts, enabling it to generate pertinent outputs without specific training on the task at hand.

One-shot learning, by contrast, involves the model making inferences from a single related example. Here, the challenge lies in empowering the model to quickly decode the task’s demands from just one example and respond accurately, demonstrating the model’s ability to adapt swiftly based on minimal data.

In the one-shot learning scenario illustrated in Fig. 5.3, GPT-3 uses a single example to bridge the gap between the task description and execution. This requires the model to process longer sequences than in zero-shot scenarios, extracting valuable information to improve its performance on translation tasks. Importantly, no gradients are calculated or weights updated during this process.

GPT-3 achieves high performance on one-shot learning tasks by leveraging the knowledge acquired during its pretraining phase, combined with the given single example. This demonstrates GPT-3 ability to rapidly adapt to new tasks with minimal labeled data, thus reducing the training costs and time required for supervised learning on specific tasks.

Few-shot learning involves training the model to make inferences after being provided with a small number of labeled samples related to the target task. In this case, the model needs to understand the specific requirements of the task based on several provided examples and generate the correct answer accordingly. The challenge of few-shot learning is how to enable the model to effectively learn and adapt to new tasks from limited information while improving prediction accuracy. Few-shot learning extends one-shot learning by providing several examples after the task description. However, the processing capacity of the GPT-3 model is limited, so submitting overly long text sequences for few-shot learning may not facilitate the extraction of useful information. This explains why context learning with too many examples is not widespread in GPT-3 models.

GPT-3 also excels in few-shot learning tasks. By taking multiple task-related examples as input, GPT-3 can better adapt to new tasks, achieving high performance across various NLP tasks. This indicates GPT-3 strong generalization ability, capable of producing high-quality outputs with a small amount of labeled data.

Compared to zero-shot, one-shot, and few-shot learning, traditional fine-tuning methods focus on supervised learning of the pretrained model with a large amount of labeled data. While this approach has achieved significant results in many NLP tasks, its limitations include the need for extensive labeled data and computational resources (to adjust model parameters). Moreover, fine-tuning on specific tasks may lead to overfitting, limiting the model’s generalization capability.

In contrast, zero-shot, one-shot, and few-shot learning strategies offer clear advantages. First, since GPT-3 has already acquired a vast amount of knowledge and language understanding during its pretraining phase, it does not require costly supervised learning on specific tasks. This significantly reduces training costs and time. Secondly, GPT-3 can perform well with only a small amount of labeled data, making it easier to adapt to a variety of NLP tasks, especially those where large amounts of labeled data are difficult to obtain.

5.4 Bayesian inference of concept distributions

Contextual learning is a key emergent capability of large language models like GPT, and grasping its principles is essential for crafting effective prompts. Fig. 5.2 illustrates this point: prompts for zero-shot learning include only a task description and content, while those for one-shot and few-shot learning also incorporate examples.

5.4.1 Implicit fine-tuning

Language models are interpreted as metaoptimizers, treating contextual learning as a form of implicit fine-tuning—an approach that establishes a link between GPT’s context-dependent learning and traditional explicit fine-tuning methods. Illustrated in Fig. 5.4, GPT produces metagradients from forward computations related to the target task labels and utilizes these gradients within the given task through its attention mechanism. This metaoptimization mirrors the fine-tuning process, where model parameters are explicitly updated using backward propagation gradients.

At the top, there are two GPT models labeled with Sentence 1, Answer 1 and Sentence 2, Answer 2. These are connected to a Backpropagation process, which generates Gradient delta W sub FT end sub. This gradient is used in the Contextual Learning process within a larger GPT model. The larger GPT model includes components such as Perspective, Feedforward Neural Network and Self-attention. The Forward Computation process generates Meta-gradient delta W sub ICL end sub. Demonstration Examples (Sentence 1 prime, Answer 1) and (Sentence 2, Answer 2) are fed into the Forward Computation process. A Query Example (Sentence, question mark) is also fed into the model to generate an Answer.

At various levels—predictive accuracy, representational depth, and attention dynamics—contextual learning and explicit fine-tuning are akin, both rooted in gradient descent optimization. Yet, the key distinction lies in how these gradients are produced: contextual learning relies on forward computations for metagradients, whereas fine-tuning uses backpropagation.

In few-shot learning, do large language models like GPT implicitly learn a function y=f(x) when presented with a small set of task-relevant examples? If this is true, these sample examples <xi,yi> have a profound influence on context-based learning performance. As illustrated in Fig. 5.5 from the original GPT-3 paper, without any examples, the model struggles to learn or adjust, but performance significantly improves in scenarios with one or a few examples.

The x-axis represents the number of examples (K) on a logarithmic scale from 0 to 10 superscript 1 end superscript. The y-axis shows accuracy percentage from 0 to 70 in increments of 10. Three model sizes are compared: 175B, 13B and 1.3B. Each model size has two lines: one solid line for With Prompt and one dashed line for Without Prompt. The 175B model (top lines) shows the highest accuracy, starting around 10 percent for zero-shot and reaching about 70 percent for few-shot learning. The With Prompt line consistently outperforms Without Prompt. The 13B model (middle lines) shows moderate performance, starting near 0 percent for zero-shot and reaching about 25 percent for few-shot learning. Again, With Prompt performs better than Without Prompt. The 1.3B model (bottom lines) shows the lowest accuracy, barely rising above 0 percent even with many examples. The difference between With Prompt and Without Prompt is minimal for this model size. The graph is labeled with Zero-shot, One-shot and Few-shot at appropriate points along the x-axis, indicating different learning scenarios. Overall, the graph demonstrates that larger models perform better and that using prompts generally improves accuracy across all model sizes and learning scenarios.

However, recent research offers a fresh view on the significance of these examples in contextual learning. The study reveals that the input-label mapping within these examples affects model performance less than previously thought. Surprisingly, when actual labels are swapped with random ones or when examples are replaced with random values, the loss in performance is relatively minor. This finding challenges the intuitive belief in few-shot learning’s reliance on accurate input-label mappings in examples.

Fig. 5.6 showcases how large language models like GPT perform on downstream tasks with no examples, correct labels, and random labels. This figure highlights two distinct inference methods used for performance evaluation: direct and channel. In direct inference, the model assesses the probabilities of different categories based on the given context. In contrast, channel inference calculates the probabilities of the context occurring under specified categories. For instance, in sentiment analysis, for the context x = “This movie is great,” Direct inference evaluates the likelihood of “This movie is great positive” versus “This movie is great negative.” Channel inference, however, assesses “positive This movie is great” against “negative This movie is great.” The data illustrates that, both in classification (upper part of the figure) and multiple-choice tasks (lower part), models with random labels (backslash) perform comparably to those with correct labels (net-like) and distinctly outperform the no-example zero-shot cases (gray).

For the Classification graph: The x-axis lists different models: Direct GPT-2, Channel GPT-2, Direct MetaICL, Channel MetaICL, Direct GPT-J, Channel GPT-J, Direct fairseq 6.7B, Channel fairseq 6.7B, Direct fairseq 13B, Channel fairseq 13B, Direct GPT-3 and Channel GPT-3. The y-axis represents Accuracy (percent) from 25 to 60 in increments of 5. For each model, there are three bars representing: 1. No Demos 2. Demos w/ gold labels 3. Demos w/ random labels The Multi-choice graph has the same x-axis layout as the Classification graph. The y-axis for Multi-choice represents Accuracy (percent) from 35 to 70 in increments of 5. For both graphs, the Demos w/ gold labels generally show the highest accuracy, followed by Demos w/ random labels and No Demos typically showing the lowest accuracy across most models. The Channel GPT-3 model appears to have the highest accuracy in both Classification and Multi-choice tasks across all three demo conditions. The graphs illustrate how different language models perform on classification and multi-choice tasks under various demo conditions, showcasing the impact of providing examples (demos) with correct (gold) or random labels compared to no examples at all.

According to Fig. 5.6, several insights emerge:

  1. 1. The definition of label spaces and the distribution of input texts set by sample examples are pivotal for contextual learning, regardless of the accuracy of individual labels. The distributions of x and y—the input texts and their potential corresponding answers—play a major role in influencing contextual learning outcomes. In scenarios with random labels, such as <xi,yi>, even if yi is incorrect, it remains within the set of possible answers y. Altering these distributions, like substituting y with options outside the expected answer set, would drastically impair the learning process.
  2. 2. The specification of the overall format is equally vital. In contexts where the label space is undefined, using random English words as labels proves far more effective than not using any labels at all.
  3. 3. Employing a contextual learning objective in metatraining enhances these effects, encouraging the model to focus more on the straightforward elements of the sample examples, like their format, rather than on the complexities of input-label mapping.

Large language models might not derive a specific mapping function y=f(x) from examples for tackling new tasks; instead, they often tap into their extensive pretrained knowledge. Yet, if we broaden the definition of task learning to encompass adapting to specific inputs, label distributions, and formats implied by examples—and refining predictions accordingly—then it’s plausible that these models do indeed extract task-relevant information from examples. Pre-trained language models come equipped with an inherent ability to execute tasks, demonstrating that language modeling goals inherently support robust zero-shot capabilities. However, this also implies that contextual learning might struggle with tasks where the necessary input-label relationships are not preencoded.

The influence of sample examples and instructional cues on language models appears to be analogous. This insight applies to instruction-following models too, indicating that such models reactivate existing capabilities rather than learning new semantic nuances of tasks. Experimental findings further reveal that pairing each unlabeled input with a random label and using it as an example can achieve performance nearing that of few-shot scenarios, even without labeled data. This suggests that our current benchmarks for zero-shot capabilities might be set significantly lower than actual potential, marking an important avenue for future enhancements in zero-shot performance.

5.4.2 Bayesian inference

Using a Bayesian framework to interpret contextual learning offers a clearer understanding of few-shot learning principles. This approach posits that during extensive language pretraining, elements like sentences, paragraphs, and tables within a document share underlying semantic themes and structured formats—for example, how questions and answers are alternately presented in Q&A formats. The training data at the document level embody latent concepts of coherence over long distances, which the model taps into during pretraining to deduce these underlying ideas.

Contextual learning, within this Bayesian framework, is seen as a problem of conditional probability, where the task is to predict the likelihood of the next word or phrase based on the pretrained model’s knowledge and a sequence of prompts. The “knowledge of the pretrained model” corresponds to what is learned about cross-document coherence during the extensive text pretraining, which becomes part of the model’s internalized understanding of the world and lays the groundwork for its context comprehension. The “series of prompt information” pertains to the specific task-oriented concept distributions learned through contextual learning with training samples, serving as critical adjustments that refine the model’s task-specific responses.

Together, the pretrained and prompted distributions underpin contextual learning in the Bayesian paradigm, offering a foundational world understanding while specifically honed for particular tasks. Bayesian inference applied to the concepts introduced in few-shot learning enhances the learning process. Successfully deducing these concepts enables the model to accurately predict outcomes in test scenarios.

poutput|prompt=∫conceptpoutput|concept,promptp(concept∣prompt)d(concept) (5.1)

(5.1)

Eq. (5.1) illustrates that prompts serve as evidence, bolstering the certainty of the posterior probability distribution p(concept|prompt). If this distribution is mainly focused on the prompted concepts, the model can effectively “learn” from the prompts, thus enhancing its likelihood of selecting the relevant prompted concepts accurately.

There’s a logical challenge, though: the language model derives prompted concepts based on context examples, yet these prompts are drawn from a prompt distribution that may greatly differ from the model’s training pretraining distribution. Prompts connect independent examples, making the transitions between different examples under the language model and its pretraining distribution unlikely, potentially introducing noise during inference. For example, concatenating independent sentences from different news topics could result in unusual texts due to the lack of sufficient context in any individual sentence. Yet, as demonstrated in GPT-3, despite these distributional discrepancies, the language model can still execute Bayesian inference effectively.

Effective contextual learning by the language model is feasible as long as the signal’s strength surpasses the noise’s disruption. Here, “signal” refers to the KL divergence between the prompted concept and other concepts under the given prompt, while “noise” is the error from transitions between examples. If the prompts make it straightforward for the model to differentiate between the prompted concept and others, the signal is considered strong. This indicates that removing certain information (like eliminating input-output mappings) is permissible if the prompt’s format is constant and the mapping information is already present in the pretrained data, a stark contrast to traditional supervised learning, which fails without intact input-output mappings (e.g., when labels are randomized).

This further clarifies the discussion on implicit fine-tuning earlier, noting that language models are not dependent on the specific <xi,yi> pairings in prompts. The model can engage in contextual learning as long as the output yi is part of the candidate set y, even if it doesn’t directly correspond to xi. This indicates that in contextual learning, every aspect of the prompt—including input distribution, output space, and format—acts as a “signal” that aims to define the task’s conceptual distribution, thus enhancing the model’s ability to accurately infer the concepts learned during pretraining. While randomizing input-output mappings in the prompts introduces “noise” by linking random sequences, the model can still undertake Bayesian inference effectively if there is a sufficient signal, like the correct input distribution, output space, and format. Proper input–output mappings can further facilitate this process by offering more precise evidence and minimizing noise, particularly when such mappings are rare in pretrained data.

The Bayesian framework thus illuminates the underlying mechanics of contextual learning and offers guidance on how to design prompts and their elements—input distribution, output space, and format. These components help define the task’s conceptual distribution through the prompts, enabling the model to more accurately infer the concepts learned during pretraining.

5.5 Thought chains

To further boost large language models’ abilities in mathematical reasoning, members of the Google Brain team, including Jason Wei and Xuezhi Wang, introduced the chain-of-thought (CoT) approach in January 2022. This method employs a divide-and-conquer strategy tailored to various contexts of learning—zero-shot, one-shot, and few-shot—training the models to mimic human-like progressive thinking and reasoning. This technique grants models the capacity to tackle complex reasoning challenges effectively.

The CoT’s most straightforward implementation involves incorporating auxiliary step-by-step instructions into prompts, a method known as zero-shot learning CoT. Despite its simplicity, it has shown effectiveness across many fields. This approach involves two phases (refer to Fig. 5.7): initially, the prompt “Let’s think about this step-by-step” is added to a zero-shot prompt, prompting the model to output its reasoning process. In the second phase, this reasoned output is followed by another prompt, “Therefore, the answer (in Arabic numerals) is,” leading the model to deliver the final answer.

The flowchart shows two prompts: [First Prompt] Inference Extraction and [Second Prompt] Answer Extraction. The first prompt reads: Question: Joe averages 25 punches per minute. A match lasts for 5 rounds of 3 minutes each. How many punches does he throw? Answer: Let's think about this step by step. This connects to a box labeled LLM. Below it, the steps are: In one minute, Joe throws 25 punches. In three minutes, Joe throws 3 times 25 equals 75 punches. In five rounds, Joe throws 5 times 75 equals 375 punches. The second prompt reads: Question: Joe averages 25 punches per minute. A match has 5 rounds, each lasting 3 minutes. How many punches does he throw in total? Answer: Let's think step by step. In one minute, Joe throws 25 punches. In five rounds, Joe throws 5 times 75 equals 375 punches. Therefore, the answer (in Arabic numerals) is. This connects to a box labeled LLM, which then connects to a box with the number 375.

This straightforward technique significantly enhances the performance of large language models on various reasoning tasks. For instance, on the GSM8K mathematical reasoning dataset, adding this specific prompt improved the reasoning accuracy from an initial 10.4% to 40.4%, as detailed in Table 5.1.

Table 5.1

Comparison between zero-shot reasoning chains and zero-shot learning.
 Mathematical ability

Zero-shot learning74.6/78.772.2/77.017.7/22.710.4/12.522.4/22.458.8/58.7

Why does adding “Let’s think about this step-by-step” to prompts enable large language models to elaborate detailed reasoning steps and compute answers? Based on the contextual learning’s Bayesian inference of conceptual distribution discussed earlier, it’s likely because the pretraining data contain an abundance of examples that begin with “Let’s think step by step,” followed by detailed reasoning and culminating in an answer. During pretraining, large language models internalize these patterns. Thus, when prompted with “Let’s think step by step,” these models can more effectively infer the step-by-step reasoning concepts they learned during pretraining, allowing them to methodically reason through tasks and derive answers.

The second application of the CoT approach targets one-shot learning prompts by incorporating manually crafted reasoning examples into the sample scenarios. These examples extend beyond simple question–answer pairs to include detailed, step-by-step reasoning processes tailored to each question. For a detailed illustration of how this is applied, refer to the CoT response depicted in Fig. 5.8.

In the standard prompt, the input question is: Roger initially has 5 tennis balls. He purchases an additional two cans of tennis balls, with each can containing 3 tennis balls. How many tennis balls does he have now? The answer is: The total is 11. The second question is: A cafeteria has 23 apples. If they use 20 apples for lunch and subsequently purchase 6 more apples, how many apples do they have now? The model output is: Answer: The answer is 27. In the chain of thought prompt, the input question is: Roger initially has 5 tennis balls. He then purchases two cans of tennis balls, with each can containing 3 tennis balls. How many tennis balls does he have now? The answer is: Roger starts with 5 tennis balls. Each of the two cans contains 3 tennis balls, totaling 6 tennis balls. Therefore, 5 plus 6 equals 11. The answer is 11. The second question is: A cafeteria has 23 apples. If they use 20 apples for lunch and subsequently purchase an additional 6 apples, how many apples do they have now? The model output is: Answer: The cafeteria originally had 23 apples. They used 20 for lunch, leaving them with 23 minus 20 equals 3 apples. They then bought 6 more apples, so they now have 3 plus 6 equals 9 apples. The answer is 9.

The CoT for one-shot learning prompts is demonstrated by enriching sample examples with detailed reasoning processes, showing how to break down large problems effectively. The paper assesses the performance of standard and CoT-based prompts on three major language models—LaMDA, GPT, and PaLM. While GPT is developed by OpenAI, Google has released the other two. Testing revealed that Google’s PaLM model, boasting 540 billion parameters, reached approximately 60.1% accuracy on the elementary-level math reasoning dataset GSM8K, initially introduced by OpenAI in October 2021.

Following CoT’s introduction, the self-consistency technique, unveiled in March 2022, enhanced GSM8K’s accuracy to 74.4%. Illustrated in Fig. 5.9, self-consistency diverges from previous CoT methods by not just outputting a single task’s reasoning process and answer. Instead, it mandates the large language model to generate multiple reasoning processes and answers for each prompt, selecting the optimal solution through a voting mechanism. This approach integrates three advancements: extending from a single to multiple prompt questions, validating the accuracy of intermediate reasoning steps, and applying weighted voting to the responses, boosting GSM8K’s accuracy to approximately 83%.

It shows a prompt being processed by the LLM, leading to different reasoning paths. The example question is: Janet's ducks lay 16 eggs each day. She eats 3 for breakfast, uses 4 to bake muffins for her friends and sells the remaining eggs for dollar2 each. How much does she earn each day? The greedy decoder path concludes: This means she eats 3 plus 4 equals 7 eggs each day. She sells the remaining eggs for dollar2 each, so she sells 7 times dollar2 equals dollar14 each day. The answer is dollar14. The self-consistency approach samples various reasoning paths: 1. She has 16 minus 3 minus 4 equals 9 eggs left, so she earns dollar2 times 9 equals dollar18 each day. The answer is dollar18. 2. This means she sells the remaining eggs for dollar2 times (16 minus 4 minus 3) equals dollar26 each day. The answer is dollar26. 3. She eats 3 eggs for breakfast, so she has 16 minus 3 equals 13 eggs left. After using 4 eggs for baking muffins, she has 13 minus 4 equals 9 eggs left. Therefore, she sells 9 eggs at dollar2 each, earning dollar18. The answer is dollar18. The final answer is aggregated as dollar18.

The CoT applications all reflect the divide-and-conquer approach, where complex reasoning challenges are segmented into simpler subtasks. By resolving these subtasks, the overall problem solution is derived from the individual answers. This strategy reaffirms the concept of “Bayesian inference of conceptual distribution,” using prompts to define task-specific conceptual distributions and enabling the model to more effectively infer the concepts learned during pretraining. With inherent reasoning abilities, large language models can enhance their step-by-step problem-solving accuracy by employing properly crafted prompts and methodical guidance.

For corpora in various languages, appropriate step-by-step prompts can differ significantly. For instance, in Chinese corpora, a phrase equivalent to “Let’s think step by step” could be better expressed as “The detailed problem-solving approach is as follows,” rather than a literal translation. Consequently, when employing different CoT approaches, it is crucial to tailor prompts based on the specific linguistic context. By doing so, prompts can effectively enhance the posterior conceptual distribution for task decomposition, enabling the large language model to dissect a complex reasoning issue into a series of simpler subtasks, thus improving its inferential prowess.

5.6 Summary

GPT-3 is an advanced model built on an optimized sparse Transformer decoder architecture, boasting up to 175 billion parameters. As the model has expanded, it has exhibited substantial improvements across a variety of NLP tasks; however, this enhancement comes at the cost of increased training expenses. To mitigate these costs, GPT-3 employs a content-based learning strategy, especially leveraging few-shot learning. This approach does not require adjusting model parameters but instead relies on acquiring task-specific knowledge from a limited number of examples, thus delivering strong performance on new tasks. This chapter delves into the workings of GPT-3’s content-based learning through the lenses of Bayesian inference and conceptual distributions, and it discusses the divide-and-conquer philosophy underpinning the CoT approach. Lastly, Section 5.5 investigates the CoT’s capacity for coherent reasoning and innovation. By incorporating this advanced reasoning into its workflow, the model enhances its ability to comprehend and produce complex outputs.

Chapter 6 Pretraining strategies for large language models

Abstract

This chapter provides an in-depth exploration of pretraining strategies for large language models (LLMs), focusing on the critical aspects of dataset selection, data processing, and distributed training techniques. It begins by discussing the importance of diverse, high-quality datasets and methods like deduplication and augmentation to enhance model performance and generalization. The chapter then delves into distributed training strategies, including data parallelism, model parallelism, and pipeline parallelism, which are essential for efficiently handling the massive computational demands of LLMs. Tools such as Pathways, Megatron-LM, and DeepSpeed Zero are highlighted for their roles in optimizing distributed training processes. The chapter concludes with a comparison of various training strategies employed by models like GPT-3, OPT, PaLM, and BLOOM, emphasizing the importance of stable parameters, optimized training settings, and hardware considerations such as BF16 precision. These insights are crucial for improving the efficiency and effectiveness of pretraining large-scale language models.

Keywords

Large language models; pre-training strategies; dataset selection; distributed training; data parallelism; model parallelism; pipeline parallelism; Megatron-LM; DeepSpeed ZeRO; BF16 precisiong

In the initial stages of developing large language models (LLMs), key challenges such as the amount of data, the size of parameters, the design of the training framework, and how the training is managed play crucial roles. These issues might lead to problems like sudden spikes in loss or the model struggling to properly converge. To overcome these, integrating various training approaches, including handling of pretraining data, selection of training frameworks, and adjustments in training parameters, is essential to improve both the stability and efficiency of the pretraining phase.

From the release of GPT-3 onwards, OpenAI has implemented a partly proprietary approach, keeping certain essential technical details confidential. Details such as the methodology for creating the training dataset, the computational resources required, and specifics about hyperparameters like the learning rate, number of iterations, or the type of optimizer remain undisclosed. This chapter aims to delve deeper into the pretraining strategies of ChatGPT, drawing comparisons and insights from the disclosed information of other major natural language processing models.

6.1 Pre-training datasets

The ChatGPT model has been developed and refined based on GPT-3.5, whereas InstructGPT builds upon GPT-3.0. Both have been fine-tuned with the aid of human feedback to optimize performance. As outlined in Table 6.1, during its pretraining, GPT-3.0 extensively used sources like Wikipedia, a variety of books, Internet-sourced web pages such as Common Crawl, and datasets like WebText2. GPT-3.5, on the other hand, expanded its sources to include code-related datasets from GitHub, conversational forums such as StackExchange, and datasets of video subtitles.

  1. 1. Wikipedia: Serving as a free, multilingual online encyclopedia, Wikipedia brings together the knowledge and efforts of over 300,000 volunteers. By April 2022, the English Wikipedia featured more than 6.4 million articles, totaling over 4 billion words. Wikipedia’s text is immensely valuable, noted for its meticulous content, precise references, clear descriptions, and extensive scope, which is why many research laboratories prefer to use its English version as their primary dataset.
  2. 2. Books: This category includes datasets from both fiction and nonfiction genres, aimed at enhancing the model’s capabilities in storytelling and responsiveness. These datasets are primarily drawn from sources like Project Gutenberg and Smashwords (Toronto BookCorpus/BookCorpus). Notably, OpenAI’s GPT-3 paper did not reveal the exact origins of the Books1 (12 billion tokens) and Books2 (55 billion tokens) datasets. Researchers speculate that these might come from collections similar to those on LibGen18 and Sci-Hub, though their vast sizes might not perfectly match the expected dataset characteristics of GPT-3.
  3. 3. Scholarly Journals: This dataset comprises articles from both preprints and journals that have been formally published, providing a robust and precise academic grounding. Academic texts are typically characterized by strong logical structuring, rationality, and detailed exposition. Key sources for these datasets include ArXiv and the National Institutes of Health.
  4. 4. WebText: WebText is a substantial dataset sourced from all outbound links on Reddit that have gathered at least three upvotes. This approach captures the trends of popular content online, which helps in curating quality links and generating related textual content.
  5. 5. Common Crawl: Operational since 2008, Common Crawl offers an extensive collection of web pages, metadata, and text extractions. It covers a diverse array of text in various languages and domains. Researchers often use the English-only, filtered version (C4) of this dataset for their projects.
  6. 6. Other Datasets: This includes datasets like GitHub for code, StackExchange for dialogues, and collections of video subtitles. These are vital in developing the model’s proficiency in understanding code, managing dialogues, and processing multimodal information.

Table 6.1

Dataset used for training GPT-3.
DatasetOrder of magnitude (tokens)Weights in the training mix
Common Crawl (filtered)410B60%
WebText219B22%
Books112B8%
Books255B8%
Wikipedia3B3%

To summarize, the GPT-3.0 and GPT-3.5 models have harnessed an extensive variety of datasets during their training phases, encompassing a broad range of fields, languages, and content types. This diversity in training resources has equipped the models with a more comprehensive understanding and improved efficiency, resulting in enhanced performance in real-world applications. The variety and depth of these datasets enable the models to effectively comprehend and produce a wide array of textual outputs, thereby increasing their usability and adaptability across various applications.

6.2 Processing of pretraining data

GPT-3 underwent its pretraining on a massive dataset of about 300 billion tokens, with around 60% derived from a meticulously selected portion of Common Crawl, and the rest from sources including WebText2, Books1 and Books2, and Wikipedia. The GPT-3.5 update further integrated code-related datasets such as GitHub Code. The way these datasets are sampled isn’t strictly based on size but is optimized for quality. Although the open-source community has made several attempts to replicate ChatGPT, truly successful efforts are quite rare. Models like OPT-175B and BLOOM-176B, while comparable or even larger in parameter size than GPT-3, have not reached the performance level of the original GPT-3. Still, these models serve as valuable case studies for addressing the critical aspects of handling pretraining data discussed in this chapter.

  1. 1. Quality and quantity of pretraining data. The amount of data used for training is crucial for the effectiveness of pretrained models. For example, the volume of data used for PaLM and GPT-3’s pretraining far exceeds that of OPT and BLOOM (refer to Table 6.2), indicating that large, high-quality datasets are pivotal to their success. Moreover, the quality of data significantly affects model performance. GPT-3, for instance, utilizes an efficient classifier to sift through its pretraining dataset, a step not taken by models like OPT and BLOOM during their training.
  2. 2. De-duplication of Pre-training Data. Removing duplicates from the dataset is essential to prevent models from overly adapting to the same data, which improves their ability to generalize to new data. GPT-3 implements de-duplication at the document level to combat overfitting, while the Pile corpus used in OPT’s pretraining still harbors a considerable amount of repeated data.
  3. 3. Diversity of Pre-training Datasets. For large-scale language models, it is vital to train on diverse datasets that cover different domains, formats (like text, code, and tables), and languages. OPT-175B incorporates sources such as Pushshift.io Reddit and Common Crawl, which are predominantly English. However, through a combination of various sources including Common Crawl, the corpus integrates a small portion of nonEnglish data. In contrast, BLOOM utilizes the ROOTS corpus, which encompasses data in 46 natural languages and 13 programming languages from hundreds of sources, providing it with a much richer diversity than OPT-175B.

Table 6.2

Training dataset for large language models.
 Number of tokens in the pre-training corpusNumber of tokens seen during pre-training
PaLM780B770B
GPT-3500B300B
OPT180B300B
BLOOM341B366B

It is crucial to recognize that while having a diverse range of data is essential for training broadly capable LLMs, the specific make-up of the pretraining data has a profound effect on how well these models perform in certain tasks. For example, BLOOM, with its higher proportion of data in multiple languages, excels in tasks like multilingual processing and translation. OPT’s extensive use of conversational data, such as from Reddit, likely contributes to its effectiveness in dialogue-based tasks. GPT-3.5’s emphasis on coding datasets not only enhances its performance in coding-related tasks but may also improve its ability in chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning. However, despite BLOOM’s inclusion of code data in pretraining, it struggles with coding and CoT tasks, indicating that code data alone isn’t enough to excel in these areas.

To push the boundaries of pretrained language models even further, researchers should consider the following:

  1. 1. Finer filtering of training data to ensure high quality.
  2. 2. More efficient de-duplication of training data to minimize redundancy and prevent overfitting.
  3. 3. Enhancing the diversity of training data to boost the models’ ability to generalize across various tasks and fields.
  4. 4. Investigating how data distribution affects model performance in specific applications to optimize the selection and organization of pretraining data.

Effective data de-duplication prevents overfitting, careful data selection ensures quality, and diverse datasets enhance the general applicability of large-scale pretrained language models. However, the detailed processes by which GPT-3 handles these data, or the specifics of the pretraining data itself, remain undisclosed.

6.3 Distributed training patterns

One distinct characteristic of LLMs is their ever-increasing size. Take GPT-3, for instance, with its staggering 175 billion parameters. Training this model requires a month, even with 1024 high-capacity (80 GB) A100 GPUs. The Meta LLaMA model, with 65 billion parameters, pushes the envelope further by using 2048 Nvidia A100 GPUs over a span of 21 days. With such growth in model size, significant challenges inevitably follow.

A primary hurdle is the limitations imposed by current hardware technology, notably the memory wall issue. Due to physical constraints, the computing capabilities and memory capacities of single devices are insufficient to support the expanding scale of large models, leading to challenges as follows:

  1. 1. VRAM limitations: No existing graphics processing unit (GPU) can fully store the parameters of these massive models. For instance, GPT-3 needs about 700 GB just for the model parameters, another 700 GB for gradients, and 1400 GB for optimizer states, adding up to roughly 2.8 TB.
  2. 2. Computational challenges: Even if it were possible to cram an entire model into one GPU (say, by shuttling parameters between the host and device memory), the sheer volume of computations required would make the training process impractically long. For perspective, training GPT-3 on a single V100 NVIDIA GPU would take an estimated 288 years (Fig. 6.1).
