The y-axis represents training compute in PFLOPS, ranging from 1eplus02 to 1eplus09 on a logarithmic scale. The x-axis shows years from 2012 to 2021. Three trend lines are shown: 1. Transformer: 750x slash 2 yrs 2. CV slash NLP slash Speech: 15x slash 2 yrs 3. Moore's Law: 2x slash 2 yrs Various AI models are plotted: AlexNet (2012) VGG, Seq2Seq (2014) ResNet, InceptionV3 (2015) Xception (2016) ResNext, DenseNet (2017) ELMo, Transformer (2018) BERT, GPT-1 (2019) MoCo, ResNet50, XLNet, Megatron LM, Microsoft T-NLG (2020) GPT-3, Wav2Vec 2.0 (2021) GPT-3 is the highest point on the graph, showing significantly higher compute requirements than other models. The graph illustrates the rapid increase in compute requirements for AI models, particularly for transformer models, which show a 750x increase every 2 years, far outpacing Moore's Law (2x every 2 years).

As a result, despite the growing pains of advancing chip technology, distributed training across multiple nodes has emerged as the primary strategy for managing these gargantuan training tasks. Modern approaches include data parallelism, model parallelism, pipeline parallelism, and hybrid methods that combine data and model parallelism.

6.3.1 Data parallelism

Data parallelism is a technique where the training data batch is split across multiple computational devices. Each device processes a portion of the data independently—performing tasks like forward and backward propagation and updating parameters. After each cycle of training, these devices synchronize their parameters to ensure the model remains consistent across all platforms. This method is particularly useful for processing large datasets quickly (Fig. 6.2).

Below the parameter server, there are three model replicas, each connected to a data sharding component. The parameter server is labeled with Delta W prime equals W minus H times Delta W. Each model replica is connected to the parameter server with arrows labeled W prime and Delta W. The data sharding components are depicted as cylindrical shapes below each model replica.

In practical terms, data parallelism is achieved by dividing the overall batch into smaller subbatches, each handled by a different device. A common framework for this is the parameter server model. Here, every device holds a copy of the model. Each node processes different subsets of data and calculates gradients. These gradients are then sent to a central node, or parameter server, which updates the model and redistributes the updated version back to each node. Despite its efficacy in scaling, data parallelism faces certain challenges:

  1. 1. If the batch size per GPU drops below a certain point, it can lead to underutilization of GPU capacities and higher communication overhead.
  2. 2. There’s a limit to how many devices can be effectively used, dictated by the manageable batch size, which can cap the number of accelerators employed in training.

6.3.2 Model parallelism

Model parallelism is designed to bypass the memory restrictions of individual computing units, like GPUs, by distributing a massive model across several devices during training. In this setup, each component of the model is handled by different devices, and every device is involved in all steps of the training process, as illustrated in Fig. 6.3. Model parallelism includes two primary types: tensor parallelism and pipeline parallelism.

  1. 1. Tensor parallelism: This approach splits model parameters—such as weights, gradients, and optimizer states—into smaller subtensors, which are then allocated to various computing devices. Each device handles its portion of the computation independently and synchronizes with others to update the entire model. This method effectively reduces the burden on individual device memory, facilitating the training of larger models within memory constraints.
    Tensor parallelism specifically involves dividing the model’s parameters and distributing them across different devices. This process uses different methods of sharding, with one-dimensional sharding being the most common, where a tensor is split along a single dimension, either horizontally or vertically. Future techniques may include more complex, multidimensional sharding methods. For example, consider matrix multiplication in the format XA=Y, where X is the input, A the weights, and Y the output. The process involves breaking down the matrix into segments for calculation and then aggregating the results. The matrix A could be divided by rows or columns:

    Y=XA1,A2=XA1,XA2Y=X1,X2A1A2=X1A1+X2A2 (6.1)

    (6.1)

    This setup allows, for example, two different GPUs to handle separate segments of a matrix multiplication task and then communicate to combine the final output. This method can be expanded to include more GPUs and can be applied to other operations that support sharding.
  2. 2. Pipeline parallelism. This advanced approach divides a model into several sequential stages, each containing one or more layers, with each stage executed on different computational devices. Similar to a production line, training data flows seamlessly from one stage to the next. Each device processes its assigned stage and then passes the outcome forward, boosting both the efficiency and speed of training (Fig. 6.4).

The initial matrices are X and A. Matrix X is a 2 by 4 matrix with elements 0, 1, 2, 3 in the first row and 4, 5, 6, 7 in the second row. Matrix A is a 4 by 2 matrix with elements 10, 14 in the first row, 11, 15 in the second row, 12, 16 in the third row and 13, 17 in the fourth row. The resulting matrix Y is a 2 by 2 matrix with elements 74, 98 in the first row and 258, 346 in the second row. Column-wise parallelism is shown with matrix X multiplied by sub-matrices A1 and A2. Matrix A1 is a 4 by 1 matrix with elements 10, 11, 12, 13. Matrix A2 is a 4 by 1 matrix with elements 14, 15, 16, 17. The resulting matrices Y1 and Y2 are 2 by 1 matrices with elements 74, 258 and 98, 346 respectively. These are combined to form matrix Y. Row-wise parallelism is shown with matrix X divided into sub-matrices X1 and X2. Matrix X1 is a 2 by 2 matrix with elements 0, 1 in the first row and 4, 5 in the second row. Matrix X2 is a 2 by 2 matrix with elements 2, 3 in the first row and 6, 7 in the second row. Matrix X1 is multiplied by matrix A1 to form matrix Y1, a 2 by 1 matrix with elements 11, 15 in the first row and 95, 131 in the second row. Matrix X2 is multiplied by matrix A2 to form matrix Y2, a 2 by 1 matrix with elements 63, 83 in the first row and 163, 215 in the second row. The resulting matrices Y1 and Y2 are combined to form matrix Y.

Neural networks can benefit from tailored parallel strategies based on their structure. For example, components with numerous parameters might best be managed by splitting the model among devices, whereas segments with fewer parameters could be better served by splitting the data. Mixing parallel strategies in specific network parts can minimize data transfer. In parts of the network with extensive hidden layers, both data and model matrices might be split simultaneously. Unlike data and model parallelism, where devices perform parallel computations, pipeline parallelism organizes tasks into a clear sequence, delegating different stages to various devices. This allows each device to focus on a specific segment of the network, supporting the training of larger models or handling larger batches more effectively.

In practice, different network layers are distributed across various GPUs, each sequentially processing its designated stage with fresh data inputs at every step, optimizing throughput. As depicted in Fig. 6.5, each computing unit is responsible for a specific layer or set of layers, executing in an orderly fashion. For instance, when the initial data input is received, device 0 calculates the first layer, F0 by the next step, device 0 processes another dataset’s first layer, while device 1 moves on to the second layer, F1, of the initial dataset. Though pipeline and tensor parallelism typically exist alongside each other and complement one another, pipeline setups can lead to underutilization, as downstream units must wait for their predecessors to finish. Additionally, this method can lead to “pipeline bubbles” where devices may idle between completing forward propagation and starting backward propagation, as illustrated in Fig. 6.5.

The diagram illustrates the computation process across four devices labeled Device 0, Device 1, Device 2 and Device 3 over time. The time axis is represented by a horizontal arrow pointing to the right. The computation is divided into forward computation and backward computation stages. Forward computation includes F sub 0 end sub on Device 0, F sub 1 end sub on Device 1, F sub 2 end sub on Device 2 and F sub 3 end sub on Device 3. Backward computation includes B sub 0 end sub on Device 0, B sub 1 end sub on Device 1, B sub 2 end sub on Device 2 and B sub 3 end sub on Device 3. There is a pipeline bubble between the forward and backward computation stages. After backward computation, each device undergoes an update stage labeled as update.

Table 6.3 provides a comparative analysis of three parallelism techniques—data parallelism, tensor parallelism, and pipeline parallelism—focusing on aspects like generality, computational utilization, VRAM consumption, and communication overhead. Data parallelism stands out for its versatility and high efficiency in both computation and communication, although it comes with a considerable VRAM overhead. Tensor parallelism, on the other hand, is more VRAM-efficient but incurs additional communication costs and is less flexible. Pipeline parallelism is efficient in terms of VRAM usage and has lower communication overheads but may suffer from “pipeline bubbles,” which can disrupt the smooth processing flow.

Table 6.3

Comparison of three types of parallelism.
 Data parallelismTensor parallelismPipeline parallelism
GeneralityFully generalIt can only be used on some models; for example, tensor parallelism cannot currently be applied to CNNsGenerally applicable
Computation utilizationWhen data distribution is relatively even, the utilization loss of computational devices is lowFrequent transmission of intermediate results incurs some utilization loss of computational devicesPipeline bubbles result in some utilization loss of computational devices
Memory overheadEach card retains the same model and optimizer parameters, resulting in relatively high overheadIt can evenly distribute memory overhead across different serversIf the parameter sizes of different layers of the model vary significantly, adjustments are required to achieve optimal results
Communication volumeOnly the gradients on different GPUs need to be transmitted, resulting in low overheadFrequent transmission of intermediate results incurs significant communication overheadOnly the hidden states and backpropagation gradients need to be transmitted between different layers, resulting in low communication overhead

6.4 Technical approaches to distributed training

Distributed training often outperforms training on a single machine with a single GPU because it enables training on a much larger scale. However, each method of distributed training comes with its own set of pros and cons. Simply adding more computing devices doesn’t always lead to increased processing power, due to the slower growth rate of memory bandwidth compared to processing power, and even slower growth in interdevice network bandwidth. Limited by the so-called “memory wall” and “network wall,” the amount of data transferred becomes a critical factor in determining the speed and effectiveness of distributed training.

When comparing data parallelism with model parallelism, it is clear that if a batch of data results in many intermediate outputs but has relatively few parameters, data parallelism is likely the better option. On the other hand, if processing a batch involves managing a large number of parameters with fewer intermediate outputs, model parallelism may prove more beneficial. It is important to note that data parallelism has a distinct advantage because it allows communication tasks to overlap more seamlessly with computations, effectively hiding some of the communication delay. This is more challenging to achieve with model parallelism.

For training massive models, pipeline parallelism is advantageous due to its minimal data transfer requirements, which involve only the sum of data transferred between stages. This is in contrast to data and tensor parallelism, which involve data transfers across the entire computation graph. Therefore, pipeline parallelism may be preferred for devices with limited bandwidth. However, a hybrid approach combining pipeline and model parallelism might offer superior performance compared to using just one of these methods. Additionally, there is potential for optimization in both data and model parallelism, as the time spent computing can obscure the time needed for data transfers.

As LLMs gain prominence, the focus on developing and implementing parallel processing strategies like model parallelism and pipeline parallelism for the foundational Transformer model has become a critical task in both academic and industrial circles. Examples include the GPipe neural network training library built on Lingvo, Microsoft’s PipeDream, NVIDIA’s Megatron-LM, Facebook’s FairScale, Microsoft’s DeepSpeed ZeRO, and Google’s Pathways.

There are primarily two technological paths for training massive language models. First, the approach led by Google utilizes a combination of tensor processing units (TPUs), accelerated linear algebra (XLA), and TensorFlow/JAX, deeply integrated with Google’s cloud infrastructure. The second path, supported by industry giants like NVIDIA, Meta, and Microsoft, relies on GPUs combined with PyTorch, Megatron-LM, and DeepSpeed. This latter path represents the most developed open-source strategy and facilitates large-scale model training through parallelism. Both methods are tailored for training substantial models across numerous GPUs and nodes, adeptly overcoming the challenges of memory and computation that typically accompany LLM training, enabling the scaling of models despite hardware limitations.

6.4.1 Pathways

Pathways is an innovative asynchronous distributed data flow architecture developed by Google, tailored for machine learning (ML) applications. Illustrated in Fig. 6.6, the architecture organizes the model’s computation into a directed acyclic graph (DAG), where nodes signify computation tasks and edges denote data dependencies. Pathways aims to enhance distributed training efficiency, showing particular strength when handling expansive models and datasets. The architecture boasts four main features:

  1. 1. Flexibility. Pathways supports various forms of parallel computing, including data parallelism, model parallelism, and pipeline parallelism, making it adaptable to different ML challenges and hardware setups.
  2. 2. Asynchronous execution. Pathways enables tasks to start processing as soon as their input data dependencies are satisfied, without the need for the entire batch to be ready. This approach minimizes idle time for computing resources, boosting training efficiency.
  3. 3. Distributed processing. The architecture allows tasks to be spread across multiple devices, maximizing the use of available hardware and enhancing both the speed and scalability of training.
  4. 4. Fault tolerance. Pathways is designed with fault-tolerance capabilities, ensuring continuous operation even in the face of device failures. This feature is crucial for long-term training tasks within large-scale distributed systems, where such failures are more common.

The diagram shows a network of interconnected nodes representing the flow from tasks to outputs. At the bottom, there are labels for Task 1, Task 2, Task 3 and Task N. These tasks are connected through a series of nodes and pathways to the outputs at the top, labeled as Output, Output, Output and Output.

Fig. 6.7 provides a comprehensive view of the Pathways architecture, encompassing three primary components:

The image consists of three primary components of the Pathways architecture. The first component, labeled Sharded Dataflow Program (Plaque), shows a diagram with nodes A, B and C connected by transfer subgraphs. It includes symbols for Host (many per island), Resource Manager (global), Scheduler (per island), Executor (per device) and Collective operations. The second component, labeled Datacenter Network (RDMA), depicts a network with accelerator slices with private interconnects and model components labeled A, B and C. Hosts, resource managers, schedulers and executors are represented within the network. The third component, labeled Gang scheduling, shows a client running a program over time with various tasks scheduled and executed in parallel. The diagram includes arrows indicating the flow of tasks and operations.

Distributed computing represented as DAG (left diagram): This diagram depicts each node as an individual compiled function, with edges between nodes illustrating the flow of data between these functions. This setup forms the core of the Pathways system, where all computations are structured as a DAG.

Resource manager distributes island accelerator subsets (middle diagram): The resource manager assigns subsets of island accelerators, termed “virtual slices,” to each compiled function. This allocation method ensures that each function operates on designated subsets of accelerators, significantly improving the efficiency of computations.

Centralized scheduling by each island's scheduler (right diagram): Each island features a central scheduler that orchestrates gang scheduling for all computations within the island. These computations are subsequently assigned to the executors on each slice for execution. Arrows emanating from the client module in the diagram denote control messages, whereas the other arrows depict the transmission of data paths.

The Pathways architecture enables components of a model to be processed in parallel across multiple devices, facilitating model parallelism. Within Pathways’ DAG framework, each node symbolizes a segment of the model, allowing the entire computation to occur concurrently on various devices, thereby accelerating model training. Additionally, Pathways supports data parallelism by processing different data subsets simultaneously across devices. In this configuration, each node processes a data subset and integrates the results into a unified model. Pathways is versatile in its parallel strategies and can fine-tune the level of parallelism as needed, offering the flexibility to employ model parallelism, data parallelism, or their combinations based on specific requirements.

Pathways aims to leverage existing hardware assets like GPUs and TPUs to boost ML tasks’ performance. While it doesn’t require specialized hardware, its effectiveness is tightly linked to the distributed computing power of the underlying hardware. Pathways can assign tasks across multiple devices and capitalize on interdevice communications for distributed computing, making it suitable for a variety of hardware setups, including clusters of multiple GPUs, TPUs, or a mix of both.

However, to fully harness the capabilities of the Pathways architecture, it is crucial to use hardware with robust communication facilities. High-speed networking options such as InfiniBand or high-speed Ethernet can dramatically enhance data transfer rates between devices, improving distributed computing performance.

Pathways is designed to operate on existing hardware, but for best results, choosing hardware with superior communication capabilities is essential. It is noteworthy that Google develops the specific implementation of Pathways as an internal project, and the details and source code are not entirely public.

6.4.2 Megatron-LM

Megatron-LM is a deep learning tool developed by NVIDIA and is available as an open-source project on GitHub, along with its documentation. It is compatible with other deep learning frameworks, such as PyTorch and TensorFlow, enabling the training of large-scale models across different hardware and software setups. The tool is designed to train massive models efficiently on multiple GPUs and nodes by utilizing model parallelism strategies, primarily tensor and pipeline parallelism.

By integrating tensor and pipeline parallelism, Megatron-LM optimizes the training process for extensive Transformer models. It achieves this by distributing the weights and layers of the model across several GPUs, allowing the training of larger, more complex models even though it necessitates transferring data between GPUs and synchronizing gradients. Therefore, having an effective communication strategy is essential to reduce communication overhead significantly.

Megatron-LM doesn’t establish a new parallel modeling framework or compiler but instead enhances the existing implementation of PyTorch Transformers with precise modifications. This straightforward method avoids the need for new compilers or extensive code rewriting, using simple primitives to implement model parallelism. Specifically, Megatron-LM divides operations within the Transformer's masked multihead attention and feedforward sections, facilitating their parallel processing.

Fig. 6.8 illustrates this process using masked multihead attention as an example. Megatron-LM leverages the inherent parallelism of multihead attention operations, dividing the associated small matrix multiplications (GEMM) for keys (K), queries (Q), and values (V) in a column-parallel fashion. This division allows each GPU to handle the matrix multiplications for corresponding attention heads locally, splitting the parameters and workload across GPUs, with each one producing parts of the final output.

On the left, the self-attention mechanism is shown with input X passing through a function f, resulting in separate attention heads. Each head has components Q sub 1 end sub, K sub 1 end sub, V sub 1 end sub, Q sub 2 end sub, K sub 2 end sub and V sub 2 end sub. The heads are combined as Q equals left parenthesis Q sub 1 end sub, Q sub 2 end sub right parenthesis, K equals left parenthesis K sub 1 end sub, K sub 2 end sub right parenthesis and V equals left parenthesis V sub 1 end sub, V sub 2 end sub right parenthesis. The outputs Y sub 1 end sub and Y sub 2 end sub are obtained through softmax and dropout operations. On the right, the dropout process is shown with Y equals Dropout left parenthesis YB right parenthesis. The outputs Y sub 1 end sub B sub 1 end sub and Y sub 2 end sub B sub 2 end sub are combined as B equals left parenthesis B sub 1 end sub over B sub 2 end sub right parenthesis. The final output Z is obtained after another dropout operation.

In the subsequent fully connected layers of the model, each GPU handles a portion of the output, allowing the weight matrix B to be row partitioned for direct computations with inputs Y1 and Y2. The final output, Z, is then derived through an all-reduce operation combined with Dropout. In real-world setups, it is unnecessary to dedicate a GPU to each attention head; instead, multiple heads can efficiently share a single GPU without breaching the principle of independent operations on individual GPUs. Hence, the total number of attention heads is ideally set to be divisible by the number of GPUs available.

Megatron-LM employs a comparable approach to split operations in the feedforward section, with decisions on which operators to divide being based on workload characteristics such as the amount of computation, parameters, and required communications. In the process of both forward and backward propagation within a single model parallel Transformer layer, Megatron-LM involves four instances of communication. Notably, the subsequent GEMMs of the linear layer outputs post-self-attention are parallelized along rows and seamlessly gather outputs from the parallel attention layer, circumventing the need for inter-GPU communication. This integration strategy, applied to both multi-layer perceptron (MLP) and self-attention layers, merges two sets of GEMM operations and removes intermediate synchronization points, thus boosting the model’s scalability. This configuration enables all GEMM operations within a straightforward Transformer layer, utilizing two all-reduce operations each in both the forward and reverse paths (as depicted in Fig. 6.9).

The diagram illustrates a neural network model with two stages of model parallelism. The first stage starts with input X, followed by layer normalization, self-attention, a linear layer and dropout. The second stage includes layer normalization, a linear layer, GeLU, another linear layer and dropout, ending with output Y. Both stages involve 2 All-Reduce operations for feedforward and feedback.

Megatron-LM’s strategy for splitting operations focuses on minimizing communication overhead and maintaining manageable computation loads across GPUs. It avoids isolating operations such as dropout, layer normalization, or residual connections to a single GPU with results then broadcasted across the network. Instead, it promotes simultaneous replicated computations across multiple GPUs.

In its architectural design, Megatron-LM treats model parallelism and data parallelism as independent dimensions. Illustrated in Fig. 6.10, the architecture integrates pipeline, tensor, and data parallelism, making the majority of operations compute-bound rather than limited by memory constraints. Megatron-LM adeptly divides the computation graph across devices, minimizing both the data transmitted over networks and the idle time of devices. This method, termed pipeline, tensor, and data parallelism (PTD-P), leverages pipeline parallelism across several GPU servers and both tensor and data parallelism within these servers. It operates within a well-optimized cluster featuring high-bandwidth connections for both intra- and interserver communications. This setup enables the training of models with up to a trillion parameters on 1000 GPUs, showcasing robust scalability and efficient computational performance, achieving up to 52% of the peak throughput of the devices.

The diagram illustrates two transformer layers labeled as Transformer Layer number 1 and Transformer Layer number 2. Each transformer layer is enclosed within a dashed line and further divided into two sections labeled as Tensor MP Part itioning number 1 and Tensor MP Part itioning number 2. The entire transformer layer is also labeled as Pipeline MP Part itioning number 1 for the first layer and Pipeline MP Part itioning number 2 for the second layer. The diagram shows the internal structure and connections within each partitioning.

6.4.3 ZeRO

DeepSpeed features the Zero Redundancy Optimizer (ZeRO) as its primary component, a strategy of data parallelism specifically aimed at decreasing both the memory demands and the communication load during model training. ZeRO’s primary objective is to reduce the amount of redundant data, such as model parameters, optimizer states, and gradients. It achieves this by distributing these elements among various training processes, facilitating more effective training of larger models within constrained hardware setups.

For training language models, the combination of mixed precision training and the Adam optimizer has become the norm. The Adam optimizer dynamically adjusts learning rates based on the first and second moments of the gradients for each parameter. According to Fig. 6.11, during the training phase, ZeRO divides storage into model states and residual states. The model state encompasses model parameters (fp16), model gradients (fp16), and the Adam states, which include fp32 backups of model parameters, and fp32 first and second moments. If the total amount of model parameters is Φ, then the storage requirement is 2Φ+2Φ+ (4Φ+4Φ+4Φ)=4Φ+12Φ=16Φ bytes, with Adam states making up 75% of this total. The residual state comprises activation values, a variety of temporary buffers, and fragments of VRAM that cannot be utilized effectively.

The diagram illustrates a neural network training process. It starts with Core Weights (F32) which are converted to float2half. The Weights in FP16 are used in Forward Propagation to produce Activations in FP16. These Activations are then used in Backpropagation - Activation to generate Activation Gradient in FP16. The Weights and Activation Gradient are used in Backpropagation - Weights to produce Weight Gradient in FP16. The Weight Gradient is then used in Weight Update along with Core Weights (F32) to Update Core Weights in FP32.

ZeRO reduces memory redundancy in data parallel processes by distributing model states—parameters, gradients, and optimizer states—across processes rather than duplicating them. It employs a dynamic communication strategy during training to distribute essential states across devices effectively, which helps balance the demands of computational granularity with the communication needs of data parallelism. With ZeRO, each device’s memory usage increases linearly with the level of data parallelism, and the communication volume mirrors this scalability. ZeRO can handle any size model, provided the devices in the cluster have sufficient memory to share the model state.

Fig. 6.12 depicts ZeRO’s three-tiered system, where each level represents a progressively greater degree of model state partitioning:

  • • ZeRO-1: This level partitions the optimizer state.
  • • ZeRO-2: This level extends partitioning to both the optimizer state and model gradients.
  • • ZeRO-3: This most extensive level partitions the optimizer state, model gradients, and model parameters.

The table consists of five columns: unnamed column, gpu0, gpu1, gpuN-1, Memory Usage and Comm Volume. It reads as follows: Row 1. Baseline: (2 plus 2 plus K) times psi, 120GB, 1 times. Row 2. POS: 2 times psi plus 2 times gamma plus K times psi divided by Nd, 31.4GB, 1 times. Row 3. POSplusg: 2 times psi plus (2 plus K) times psi divided by Nd, 16.6GB, 1 times. Row 4. POSplusgplusp: (2 plus 2 plus K) times psi divided by Nd, 1.9GB, 1.5 times.

The ZeRO paper not only quantifies the potential memory savings at each level but also notes that the first two levels do not increase the communication overhead, whereas the third level boosts communication costs by about 50%. Additionally, ZeRO optimizes residual states, which include activation values, temporary buffers, and VRAM fragments, though the specific methods for this optimization are not detailed here.

ZeRO uses a combination of partitioning and checkpointing techniques for handling activation values. In the forward phase of model propagation, checkpointing preserves certain intermediate variables to assist in gradient calculations during the backward phase. This approach essentially opts for increased computational tasks in lieu of higher storage requirements. For instance, in matrix multiplication C=A · B, the gradients for A and B are calculated as follows:

∇A=∇C⋅BT∇B=AT⋅∇C. (6.2)

(6.2)

Storing variables A and B for backward propagation occupies a considerable amount of memory. The key to checkpointing is maintaining only the gradients at specific checkpoints, with gradients between these points recalculated during the backward pass by re-executing forward operations. This method effectively replaces storage demands with additional computations.

Regarding temporary buffers, the model training process necessitates the creation of buffers of various sizes, such as those used for the All-Reduce operation on gradients. A practical solution is to establish a fixed-size buffer at the outset, which prevents the need for dynamic buffer creation during training. Additionally, for smaller data transfers, efficiently grouping multiple data sets into buckets for simultaneous transmission can improve performance.

VRAM fragmentation often arises from the frequent creation and deletion of gradients that are not stored, a consequence of employing gradient checkpointing. A practical approach to mitigate this issue is to allocate a continuous segment of VRAM in advance. This segment is used to store the model states that need to remain in memory consistently, along with the values from checkpoints, while the rest of the VRAM is available for the dynamic handling of obsolete activation values.

ZeRO-Offload is a technique that shifts part of the computational workload and model parameters from the GPU’s VRAM to the CPU’s memory. This involvement of the CPU in processing tasks substantially reduces the demand on GPU VRAM, making it feasible to train larger models with limited hardware.

To counteract the potential communication overhead between the GPU and CPU and the generally lower computational speed of the CPU, ZeRO-Offload strategically allocates computation tasks. By analyzing the performance of the Adam optimizer in fp16 mode, it selectively places only those parameters that require computation on the CPU, while keeping the rest on the GPU. Typically, it delegates the task of updating the model to the CPU, effectively turning the CPU into a parameter server. To boost efficiency further, as depicted in Fig. 6.13, ZeRO-Offload runs communication and computation tasks in parallel, thereby reducing the impact of communication delays on the overall computational workflow.

The diagram illustrates the process of forward propagation, backpropagation and parameter update in a neural network. On the left side, the process is shown without specifying the hardware. It starts with an activation value of 16, which goes through forward propagation, resulting in another activation value of 16. This value is then used in backpropagation, producing a gradient value of 16. The gradient value is used in the parameter update process, which involves parameter values of 32, momentum values of 32 and variance values of 32. The data flow includes 2M for forward propagation, 2M for backpropagation and 2M for gradient value. The parameter update process involves 12M for parameter value, 12M for momentum value and 12M for variance value. The right side of the diagram specifies the hardware, with the GPU handling forward propagation and backpropagation and the CPU handling the parameter update. The GPU section shows forward propagation and backpropagation with an activation value of 16, resulting in a gradient value of 16. The data flow includes 4M for parameter value and 2M for gradient value. The CPU section shows the update supernode with parameter values of 32, momentum values of 32 and variance values of 32. The parameter update process involves 12M for parameter value, 12M for momentum value and 12M for variance value. The float2half process is also shown, converting parameter values of 16 to 2M.

In the context of backward propagation, GPUs are designed to fill up a storage “bucket” with gradients. As it computes new gradients, it concurrently sends these buckets to the CPU. By the time backward propagation concludes, the CPU typically has updated gradients. Likewise, during parameter updates, the CPU efficiently transfers the updated parameters back to the GPU, ensuring continuous synchronization, as depicted in Fig. 6.14.

The diagram illustrates the computation and exchange flow between GPU and CPU during steps i and steps i plus 1. In steps i, forward propagation and backpropagation occur on the GPU, followed by an update on the CPU. The exchange flow shows data being unloaded from GPU to CPU (g Unload) and exchanged from GPU to CPU (p Exchange). In steps i plus 1, forward propagation and backpropagation occur again on the GPU.

ZeRO-Infinity is essentially an enhanced version of ZeRO stage-3, which utilizes non-volatile memory express (NVMe) technology. This system enables the offloading of all model parameters not just to the CPU, but also to NVMe storage. Leveraging the capabilities of the NVMe protocol allows ZeRO to make additional use of solid-state drives, which helps substantially cut down on memory demands and boosts the speed of data transfers.

6.5 Examples of training strategies

Since the release of GPT-3, OpenAI has chosen to keep its technology private. However, other models like OPT, PaLM, and BLOOM, which are similar in size to GPT-3, openly share their training strategies in their academic papers. These documents offer valuable insights into how they prepare their data before training, choose their architecture, set their training parameters, and manage other elements that are crucial for ensuring the training runs smoothly and reaches its goals effectively.

6.5.1 Training framework

Training LLMs usually involves complex techniques like model parallelism, which includes methods such as tensor and pipeline parallelism, and advanced distributed optimization methods like ZeRO. For instance, OPT utilizes ZeRO’s FSDP and Megatron-LM’s approach for model parallelism. BLOOM uses ZeRO's Deepspeed and also Megatron-LM’s model parallelism strategies. PaLM, on the other hand, employs a system called Pathways which combines model and data parallelism on TPU technology. While the specifics of GPT-3 training system are still not fully disclosed, it is known to incorporate some form of model parallelism.

6.5.2 Parameter stability

Maintaining stable parameters during the training of models like OPT and PaLM is key to their success. For OPT, frequent adjustments such as changing gradient norms, learning rates, and optimizers, and restarting training from the latest checkpoint were made. These continual changes may have hindered OPT from reaching its full potential. On the other hand, PaLM followed a more consistent strategy. It only restarted training from a checkpoint about 100 steps prior to any sudden increase in loss and skipped around 200–500 batches of data. This approach of minimal intervention helped PaLM achieve remarkable results.

6.5.3 Optimizing training settings

Optimizing training settings is essential for stable model training. PaLM, for example, improved its stability through various enhancements, including using a better version of the Adafactor optimizer, adjusting the logits before the softmax function, adding auxiliary losses that help keep normalization close to zero, using different initialization methods for word vectors and layer weights, omitting bias in certain layers, and avoiding dropout during pretraining. Techniques like applying postlayer normalization with DeepNorm and restricting gradient flow in the word vector layer further stabilize large model training. On the other hand, OPT and BLOOM have not employed these advanced methods, which might explain why they lag behind PaLM in terms of training stability and overall performance.

6.5.4 BF16

In model training, the stability can often be affected by the choices made around training systems and hardware. An important decision in this realm is using bfloat16 (BF16) for storing model weights and activations, which is key because it handles large numbers better than FP16, particularly during spikes in loss, due to having the same exponent size as FP32. Despite these benefits, FP16 limited capacity can still lead to issues, often requiring models to keep weights smaller to avoid frequent overflows, a challenge especially in large models (Fig. 6.15).

Floating Point Format BF16: Brain Floating Point Format Exponent: 8bits Mantissa (Significand): 7bits Range: approximately 1e superscript -38 end superscript to approximately 3e superscript 38 end superscript FP32: Single-Precision IEEE Floating Point Format Exponent: 8bits Mantissa (Significand): 23bits Range: approximately 1e superscript -38 end superscript to approximately 3e superscript 38 end superscript FP16: Half-Precision IEEE Floating Point Format Exponent: 5bits Mantissa (Significand): 10bits Range: approximately 5.96e superscript -8 end superscript to 65504.

Both BF16 and FP16 occupy the same amount of space (2 bytes), but BF16 can be less precise. In practice, whether using BF16 or FP16, a version of the weights is always kept in FP32 to aid in optimization updates. The 16-bit formats are mainly used for calculations, with updates done in full precision FP32 before being converted back for the next processing cycle.

However, GPUs typically prefer FP16, as seen with NVIDIA’s V100, which uses it by default for mixed precision training. OPT’s choice of FP16 might be contributing to its instability in training. Meanwhile, BLOOM has addressed the instability seen with FP16 by switching to BF16, which has improved its training process.

6.5.5 Other factors

While not always the main factor, certain choices can significantly influence the overall performance of language models. For instance, both PaLM and GPT-3 gradually increased their batch sizes during training—a strategy that’s known to boost performance for big language models. In contrast, OPT and BLOOM kept their batch sizes constant. The type of activation function used also plays a role; OPT uses ReLU, while PaLM goes with SwiGLU, and both GPT-3 and BLOOM prefer GeLU. Typically, GeLU and SwiGLU are seen to perform better in such models.

In terms of handling longer sequences, these models also differ in how they encode position information. PaLM uses Rotary Position Embeddings, BLOOM applies ALiBi which biases attention scores based on distance instead of embedding position directly, and GPT-3 and OPT use learnable relative position encodings. These choices can affect how well models manage long sequences. These examples highlight how various factors come together to influence the overall effectiveness of models during their pretraining phase.

6.6 Summary

This chapter dives deep into the pretraining strategies for LLMs. It starts with a critical look at how pretraining datasets are built and chosen, highlighting how the diversity and quality of data play a crucial role in the performance of these models. It also discusses methods like data cleaning, deduplication, and augmentation, which are key to boosting a model’s ability to generalize and maintain stability.

In discussing distributed training, the chapter focuses on strategies for data and model parallelism that can dramatically cut down on training times and save on computational resources. It details tools like Pathways, Megatron-LM, and DeepSpeed Zero, which are essential for efficient distributed training setups.

The chapter concludes with examples of training strategies for large NLP models, discussing choices around training frameworks, model architecture tweaks, training settings, and optimizations like using BF16. These strategies are vital for managing challenges like sudden spikes in loss and ensuring smooth model convergence.

Though the analysis is primarily based on information from other large NLP models of similar size, the strategies and methods discussed are widely applicable and offer valuable insights for optimizing pretraining processes in models like ChatGPT. As computational power grows and new innovative methods emerge, we can expect more breakthroughs in the pretraining strategies for LLMs.

Chapter 7 Proximal policy optimization

Abstract

This chapter provides a comprehensive overview of proximal policy optimization (PPO), a policy gradient method in reinforcement learning that focuses on online learning. The chapter begins by introducing traditional policy gradient methods, emphasizing their direct approach to optimizing policies using real-time data. It then explores the Actor-Critic framework, which combines policy gradients with value function approximation to stabilize learning. Trust Region Policy Optimization (TRPO) is discussed as an enhancement, introducing constraints on policy updates to prevent instability. PPO builds on TRPO by incorporating a clipping mechanism in its objective function, ensuring stable and efficient policy updates without the need for complex Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence constraints. The chapter concludes by highlighting PPO’s effectiveness across various applications while acknowledging its limitations, which present opportunities for further research and innovation.

Keywords

Proximal policy optimization; policy gradient; Actor-Critic; trust region policy optimization; reinforcement learning; online learning; clipping mechanism; stability; convergence

Proximal policy optimization (PPO) stands out as a policy gradient method, distinct from popular reinforcement learning techniques like Deep Q Network due to its focus on online learning. Policy gradient methods, unlike others that depend on replaying past experiences, learn directly from the real-time data that agents gather in their environments. The Actor-Critic framework refines this approach by using a value function, the Critic, to steer the policy, the Actor, towards more stable and efficient learning outcomes. A specific enhancement within this framework is the Trust Region Policy Optimization (TRPO), which stabilizes learning by setting a “trust region” in the policy space where updates can safely occur without causing instability due to excessive changes.

The PPO algorithm builds upon TRPO by limiting the magnitude of updates in what’s known as PPO, ensuring that the new policy remains similar to the previous one. This approach has paved new avenues for tackling complex tasks in reinforcement learning. It has shown particular efficacy in natural language processing, where it is used to fine-tune behemoths like ChatGPT and InstructGPT. This chapter explores the journey and merits of PPO, starting from the basics of policy gradients and Actor-Critic methods to the more advanced TRPO and PPO.

7.1 Traditional policy gradient methods

7.1.1 The principles of policy gradient methods

Policy gradient methods form a key group of reinforcement learning algorithms that focus directly on improving policies. These methods help agents figure out the best strategies by interacting with their surroundings. The main concept here is to tweak the policy parameters in a way that aligns with the policy gradient—essentially a direction that suggests how to change the policy to get the most reward over time. In practical terms, a policy maps different situations (states) to actions. To manage this mapping, policy gradients use neural networks, like deep neural networks, to simulate and enhance the policy by tracking and adjusting to these gradients.

Consider a policy πθ(a|s) determined by parameters θ, where s indicates the state, a the action, and θ the policy parameters. Policy gradient methods strive to find the best set of parameters θ* that maximize expected rewards. These methods typically use a loss function that aims to minimize negative expected rewards, illustrated by the formula:

L(θ)=−E∑t=0Tγt×rst,at|π (7.1)

(7.1)

where θ refers to neural network parameters, T is the maximum number of time steps, γ is the discount factor reducing future rewards’ value, and rst,at is the reward received for choosing action at in state st at time t. The goal is to find the values of θ that make this loss as small as possible (thereby maximizing the expected rewards). This is done by computing the policy gradient, or how the expected rewards change with small changes in θ. The method used for this is gradient ascent, where parameters are updated in the direction that increases expected rewards:

∇θE[Σtrt]=E[Σt∇θlogπθ(at|st)×rt]. (7.2)

(7.2)

The equation shows how to compute the gradient ∇θ, which tells us how to adjust the parameters θ of our policy to maximize rewards. Here, at and st represent the action taken and the state observed at each timestep t, respectively. To find this gradient, the agent interacts with its environment, gathering data on various “trajectories,” each a sequence of states, actions, and rewards. Analyzing these trajectories provides an accurate measure of the policy gradient. This insight is then used to tweak the policy parameters θ, effectively teaching the agent to make better decisions over time. For example, a trajectory, denoted by τ, captures how an agent’s states, actions, and rewards evolve over time as it interacts with its environment. It’s expressed as

τ=(s1,a1,r1,s2,a2,r2,…,st,at,rt). (7.3)

(7.3)

As illustrated in Fig. 7.1, by knowing an agent’s (or actor’s) policy parameters θ, we can calculate the likelihood of any given trajectory τ. This probability reflects how the agent’s decisions, based on its specific actions in certain states, play out over time. Each state and action within the trajectory is determined by sampling from the agent’s action probability distribution pθ(at∣st) and from the probabilities of transitioning to new states, pst+1∣st,at. This model helps us understand and predict how the agent will behave in various scenarios.

pθ(τ)=p(s1)∏t=1Tpθ(at∣st)p(st+1|st,at). (7.4)

(7.4)

The process starts with the environment providing state S sub 1 end sub to the agent. The agent then takes action a sub 1 end sub, which leads to a reward r sub 1 end sub. The agent updates and the environment provides the next state S sub 2 end sub. The agent takes action a sub 2 end sub, leading to reward r sub 2 end sub. This process continues with the environment providing state S sub 3 end sub. The equation at the bottom shows the calculation of R sub theta end sub as the sum over tau of R left parenthesis tau right parenthesis p sub theta end sub left parenthesis tau right parenthesis, which equals E sub tau tilde p sub theta end sub left parenthesis tau right parenthesis left bracket R left parenthesis tau right parenthesis right bracket. R left parenthesis tau right parenthesis is defined as the summation from t equals 1 to T of r sub t end sub.

In scenarios where an agent operates under the same policy parameters θ, it can produce multiple trajectories. Each trajectory τ has a specific probability of occurring. By calculating the weighted sum of all these probabilities, along with their associated rewards, we derive the expected value:

∇R¯θ=∑τR(τ)∇pθ(τ)=Eτ~pθ(τ)[R(τ)∇logpθ(τ)]. (7.5)

(7.5)

To approximate this calculation, we use the Monte Carlo method:

∇R¯θ=1N∑n=1N∑t=1TnRτn∇logpθatn|stn. (7.6)

(7.6)

The parameters are then updated using gradient ascent, where θ is adjusted by adding a learning rate η multiplied by the gradient ∇R¯θ. The learning rate itself can be tuned using methods like Adam or RMSProp:

θ←θ+η∇R¯θ. (7.7)

(7.7)

Policy gradient methods stand out because they directly optimize the policy without the need to estimate action or state-value functions, which is particularly beneficial in environments with high-dimensional continuous action spaces and complex dynamics.

7.1.2 Importance sampling

In policy gradient methods, importance sampling plays a crucial role, especially when it comes to effectively using data previously gathered before updating the policy parameters. The main purpose of importance sampling is to estimate the expected value of one distribution (the target policy) from another (the reference policy). Illustrated in Fig. 7.2, during policy updates, we can use importance sampling to draw on trajectories from the old policy to estimate what the expected returns might look like under the new policy. This approach saves the time and computational resources normally spent on gathering new samples.

On the left side, it shows the policy pi sub theta end sub with tau superscript 1 end superscript and tau superscript 2 end superscript, each containing pairs of s sub 1 end sub comma a sub 1 end sub and s sub 2 end sub comma a sub 2 end sub, followed by R left parenthesis tau superscript 1 end superscript right parenthesis and R left parenthesis tau superscript 2 end superscript right parenthesis. This data is used only once. On the right side, it shows the update model process with theta left arrow theta plus eta nabla R bar sub theta end sub. The gradient nabla R bar sub theta end sub is calculated as 1 over N summation from n equals 1 to N summation from t equals 1 to T sub n end sub R left parenthesis tau superscript n end superscript right parenthesis nabla log p sub theta end sub left parenthesis a sub t superscript n end superscript vertical bar s sub t superscript n end superscript right parenthesis. The diagram indicates the flow between data sampling and model updating.

Specifically, in these methods, we’re interested in the expected returns of state-action pairs under the new target policy πθ. When policy parameters change from θ to θ’, the probability associated with an action a in the same state s will shift from pθ(s,a) to pθ′(s,a). To continue using the same trajectory τ, we need to recalculate its expected return Rτ using the formula:

∇R¯θ′=1N∑n=1N∑t=1TnRτn∇logpθ′atn|stn. (7.8)

(7.8)

This strategy allows for the reuse of trajectories from the reference policy π_θ, employing importance sampling to estimate the expected returns under the new target policy π_(θ'). In real-world applications, this means we can update policies using existing trajectories without needing to collect new data each time there’s a change, enhancing efficiency.

However, when the target and reference policies are quite different, importance sampling estimates can exhibit high variance. This happens because the trajectories must be weighted, and these weights can vary significantly. Additionally, the unpredictability of the environment and changes in policies can make the estimated returns highly variable. This variability can cause instability in gradient calculations, which can hinder the learning process. Moreover, since policy gradient methods depend on trajectories sampled from the policy, these trajectories can often be strongly correlated, which might also contribute to high variance in gradient estimations, impacting the algorithm’s stability and convergence.

To combat these issues, advantage functions are commonly used in practice to help reduce variance. An advantage function evaluates the benefit of choosing a specific action in a given state compared to the average return. By deducting the average return, we get a more stable estimate of the advantage, which helps reduce the variance from importance sampling and enhances the stability of gradient calculations. However, accurately estimating advantage functions still poses a challenge in real-world applications.

7.1.3 Advantage function

In policy gradient methods, researchers use something called an advantage function to boost learning efficiency and stabilize the algorithm. The advantage function evaluates how much better a specific action is in a given state compared to the average outcome of the current policy. Essentially, it helps identify the extra benefits of choosing a particular action in a certain situation. The advantage function, denoted as Aπ(s,a), is defined as

Aπ(s,a)=Qπ(s,a)−Vπ(s) (7.9)

(7.9)

where Qπ(s,a) is the action-value function, representing the expected total reward for taking action a in state s under policy π. Vπ(s), on the other hand, is the state-value function, which estimates the expected total reward in state s under policy π. Therefore, the advantage function quantifies how much more beneficial a specific action a is compared to what’s typically expected under policy π in that state.

Once we include the advantage function, we can update the way we calculate the policy gradient:

∇θEΣtrt=EΣt∇θlogπθat∣st×Aπst,at. (7.10)

(7.10)

Introducing the advantage function helps reduce the variance that comes from relying solely on the original reward signals, enhancing both the stability and accuracy of our gradient estimates. This improvement occurs because the advantage function strips out the components related to the state value function, allowing us to focus more on the relative benefits of different actions.

With the advantage function, policy gradient methods can identify and optimize effective actions more swiftly, speeding up the overall learning process.

However, while the advantage function offers clear benefits, calculating it requires the estimation of both the action-value function, Qπ(s,a), and the state-value function, Vπ(s), adding to the computational complexity.

7.2 Actor-Critic

The Actor-Critic algorithm, as shown in Fig. 7.3, is a type of reinforcement learning that combines elements of policy gradient methods and value function approximation. What sets this algorithm apart is how it splits responsibilities: the Actor formulates policies, and the Critic assesses how effective these policies are.

The components include Action Trajectory, Environment, Actor, Critic and Policy Gradient. The Action Trajectory leads to Practice, which interacts with the Environment. The Environment sends information to the Actor, which updates a given action based on state (a|s). The Actor is depicted with a neural network structure. The Environment also sends information to the Critic, which updates Q(s,a). The Critic is also depicted with a neural network structure. The Critic sends Q(s,a) to the Policy Gradient, which is then updated. The flow of information is indicated by arrows connecting these components.

Policy gradient methods by themselves often suffer from high variance in their gradient estimates, which can make learning unstable. The Actor-Critic method tackles this by bringing in a Critic to learn and provide a value function, which acts as a reliable baseline to help calculate more stable policy gradients.

While traditional policy gradient methods typically use the Monte Carlo method to calculate returns, which is accurate but variable, the Actor-Critic approach estimates returns using a value function, reducing variance at the cost of a slight bias. This compromise allows the Actor-Critic method to be more stable during training.

In this setup, both the Actor and the Critic are modeled using function approximators like neural networks. The Actor uses a parameterized policy πθ(a∣s) to decide actions, with θ as the policy parameters. The Critic, on the other hand, evaluates the expected total rewards for different states using a parameterized value function Vψ(s), with ψ as its parameters. This collaboration between the Actor and Critic helps the agent better manage the trade-offs between exploring new actions and exploiting known strategies, enhancing overall learning efficiency.

7.2.1 Algorithm steps

The Actor-Critic algorithm follows these basic steps:

First, the parameters for both the policy (θ) and the value function (ψ) are initialized.

During each training episode, these actions are taken:

  1. a. Begin at the starting state and use the policy πθ(a|s) to decide an action.
  2. b. The chosen action is then performed, which leads to receiving the next state s’ and a reward r.
  3. c. The Critic computes the temporal difference (TD) error for the value function as follows:

    δ=r+γVψs′−Vψ(s).


    Here, γ represents the discount factor, which adjusts the importance of future rewards.
  4. d. The TD error δ is used to refine the Critic’s parameters ψ.
  5. e. This same TD error δ is also used as a proxy for the advantage function to update the Actor’s policy parameters θ:

    θ=θ+α∇θlogπθ(a|s)×δ

    where α is the learning rate.
  6. f. If the episode concludes, it’s time for the next one; otherwise, update the state to s′ and repeat the steps from a to f.

7.2.2 Value function and policy update

In the Actor-Critic algorithm, the value function is intricately linked with how policies are updated. The Critic’s role is to learn the value function to assess the expected total rewards for the current policy across various states. Meanwhile, the Actor uses insights from the value function to refine the policy. This dynamic interaction unfolds primarily in these ways:

  1. 1. Advantage function estimation: The Actor uses this function, which relies on the Critic’s value function, to guide updates, making policy learning more stable and efficient.
  2. 2. TD error as learning signal: The Actor uses the TD error computed by the Critic to gauge the difference between actual experiences and the expected outcomes predicted by the value function, which helps in fine-tuning the policy.
  3. 3. Adaptive exploration and exploitation: The Critic’s insights allow the Actor to smartly adjust its strategy for exploring new actions and exploiting known rewarding actions, based on the current value assessments.
  4. 4. Dual learning process: Both the Actor and the Critic learn simultaneously—policy from the Actor and value functions from the Critic. This dual approach not only speeds up learning but also enhances the stability by reducing variability in policy updates.

7.2.3 Issues and challenges

While the Actor-Critic algorithm addresses many limitations of traditional policy gradient methods, it still faces several challenges:

  1. 1. Stability of dual-network training: The Actor and Critic, each trained with their own neural network models, must be managed simultaneously, which can destabilize training. Inaccurate value assessments from the Critic could potentially misguide the Actor in policy updates.
  2. 2. Exploration strategy issues: Though the algorithm can adapt its exploration strategies, such as adding noise, these may not be effective in more complex scenarios.
  3. 3. Hyperparameter tuning challenges: The algorithm requires careful adjustment of several parameters, like learning rates and discount factors, to optimize performance. Finding the right set of parameters can be challenging and time-consuming in real-world settings.
  4. 4. Sample complexity issues: Improved over simple policy gradient methods, the Actor-Critic still demands a significant amount of training samples, which can be costly and impractical to gather in practical environments.

7.3 Trust region policy optimization

TRPO expands on the Actor-Critic algorithm to tackle the instability in policy updates seen in traditional policy gradient methods and the basic Actor-Critic approach. TRPO’s key feature is its restriction on the extent of policy updates, ensuring that changes remain within a defined “trust region.” This approach significantly boosts the stability and convergence of policy learning, making it more reliable and effective.

7.3.1 Optimization objectives

TRPO not only aims to enhance policy improvements but also controls the changes by limiting the KL divergence (a measure of similarity between probability distributions) between new and old policies. As depicted in Fig. 7.4, by keeping the KL divergence within certain limits, TRPO avoids drastic changes to the policies, which helps maintain stability during updates.

The left graph shows two probability distribution curves labeled p(x) and q(x). The x-axis ranges from negative 4 to positive 4, while the y-axis ranges from 0 to 0.4. The p(x) curve (blue) peaks around negative 1, while the q(x) curve (purple) peaks around positive 1. The right graph illustrates the Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence D_KL(P||Q) between these distributions. Its x-axis also ranges from negative 4 to positive 4, with the y-axis ranging from negative 0.1 to 0.4. The KL divergence is represented by a filled area under a curve, which is mostly positive but has a small negative region. Three arrows point from the left graph to the right, indicating that the KL divergence is derived from the comparison of the two probability distributions. The image relates to the concept of Trust Region Policy Optimization (TRPO), which uses KL divergence to limit policy changes during updates, maintaining stability in the learning process.

During each update, TRPO addresses a constrained optimization problem:

Maximize:

E[πθnew(a|s)/πθold(a|s)×Aθold(s,a)]. (7.11)

(7.11)

Under the constraint:

EKL(πθold(a|s)‖πθnew(a|s)<δ (7.12)

(7.12)

where “E[·]” denotes expectation, “πθold(a|s)” is the value under the old policy, “KL(·)” is the KL divergence, and “δ” is a predefined threshold. By solving this optimization challenge, TRPO ensures that policy updates are both effective and stable.

The incorporation of KL divergence into the updating process prevents unstable updates and enhances learning stability. Moreover, by managing the magnitude of updates, TRPO avoids overlooking optimal solutions, thus improving convergence. This strategy results in robust updates that are reliable even in complex scenarios and noisy environments.

7.3.2 Limitations

While TRPO excels in many areas, it is not without its challenges:

  1. 1. Computational complexity: TRPO controls policy updates through constrained optimization, each requiring the resolution of complex problems. This demand for high computational power can be a limiting factor in large-scale or real-time scenarios.
  2. 2. Need for second-order derivatives: The algorithm requires calculating second-order derivatives, like the Hessian matrix, especially using methods such as conjugate gradient. This computation can be particularly intensive for large neural networks.
  3. 3. Hyperparameter tuning: Despite its relative stability, TRPO still necessitates fine-tuning hyperparameters, such as the KL divergence threshold δ, which can be a cumbersome and time-consuming task in practice.
  4. 4. Handling discrete actions: While effective in continuous action spaces, TRPO’s performance dips in discrete action settings. This is primarily due to its conservative updates, mandated by the limitation on KL divergence, which might not be optimal in discrete scenarios.

Overall, TRPO’s introduction of KL divergence constraints boosts the stability, convergence, and robustness of policy updates, leading to significant enhancements in many reinforcement learning tasks. However, its computational demands among other challenges present notable limitations.

7.4 Principles of the proximal policy optimization algorithm

PPO is designed to improve agent performance by updating policies in a way that avoids the pitfalls of earlier approaches. Unlike traditional policy gradient methods, PPO introduces an objective function with a clipping mechanism, ensuring that policy updates don’t stray too far from the current policy, thus simplifying the process by sidestepping the complex KL divergence constraints typical of TRPO.

In practice, PPO operates with two threads: one interacts with the environment to generate data, while the other uses this data to train the policy network using PPO’s specific objective function. This method has proven to be competitive, often outperforming TRPO in a variety of tasks.

The core of PPO’s effectiveness lies in its loss function, which comprises three parts:

  • 1. Clipping objective function: This part aims to refine the policy to maximize expected returns. It works as follows:

LCLIPθ=Etmin(rt(θ)Aˆt,cliprt(θ),1−ϵ,1+ϵAˆt) (7.13)

(7.13)

where “Et[⋅]” denotes the expected value at time step t, “rt(θ)” is the ratio of probabilities between the new and the current policy, and “Aˆt” represents the estimated advantage at that state. The function uses both the standard policy gradient target, which promotes actions with high advantages, and a clipped version of the probability ratio, constrained between 1−ε and 1+ε, with ε typically set around 0.2. The clipping ensures the policy updates do not stray too far from the original, maintaining a balance between exploration and exploitation.

2. Value estimation loss: This component focuses on refining the value function to enhance its accuracy in estimating the values of states. It is mathematically represented as

LVFθ=EtVst−Vtarget2 (7.14)

(7.14)

where “V(s_t)” is the value function’s prediction for the state “s_t,” and ‘V_ “target” represents the actual observed return.

3. Entropy loss: This component aims to boost the entropy of the policy, fostering greater exploration. It is defined mathematically as

LEntropyθ=−Et[∑aπa|st;θlogπa|st;θ] (7.15)

(7.15)

where “πa∣st;θ” indicates the probability of choosing action “a” in state “st,” with “θ” representing the parameters of the policy network.

The overall loss function for PPO integrates these three components into a weighted sum:

Lθ=LCLIPθ+c1LVFθ+c2LEntropyθ. (7.16)

(7.16)

In this context, c1 and c2 serve as hyperparameters, which are essentially tuning knobs to balance the importance of various components within the model.

At the heart of the PPO algorithm is a strategic component known as the clipped PPO objective function. Central to this function is the advantage function Aˆt, which gauges how advantageous an action at is at a given state st compared to typical outcomes. Illustrated in Fig. 7.5, the advantage function can either be positive, suggesting an action is better than average, or negative, indicating it is worse. The function’s graph varies accordingly: on the left side where the advantage is positive, the model promotes taking such beneficial actions; on the right side where the advantage is negative, it discourages them.

The left graph shows the advantage function greater than zero, with the x-axis ranging from 1 minus epsilon to 1 plus epsilon and the y-axis ranging from 1 minus epsilon to 1 plus epsilon. The right graph shows the advantage function less than zero, with the same x-axis and y-axis ranges. Both graphs have three data points on the x-axis at 1 minus epsilon, 1 and 1 plus epsilon and three data points on the y-axis at 1 minus epsilon, 1 and 1 plus epsilon.

In practical terms, if the action ratio rt(θ) veers too high, the model flattens its loss curve to signify a higher likelihood of these actions under the new policy compared to the old. This area is deliberately truncated to prevent the model from overly adjusting based on this data, thus moderating the influence of such updates. When actions yield negative outcomes, the model flattens this function as the action ratio approaches zero, reflecting their improbability under the current guidelines and preventing drastic changes that might otherwise zero out these actions.

A crucial aspect to remember is that the advantage function can be noisy; thus, sweeping changes to the policy based solely on this data are discouraged. The most extreme scenario, where a previous update substantially increased an action’s likelihood and degraded the overall strategy, calls for a potential rollback facilitated by the PPO’s function. In such cases, negative gradients help recalibrate the policy by decreasing the likelihood of those actions, aligning the adjustment with the scale of the initial misjudgment. This delicate balancing act ensures that the policy refines itself, focusing on minimizing loss where it counts.

The PPO algorithm was crafted by experts with a nuanced grasp of its objective function’s behavior, which is crucial for devising specific reward functions. This deep understanding was likely conveyed through a series of detailed diagrams before they finalized the precise objective function to ensure reliable system operations. If the minutiae of this process seem daunting, don’t fret—understanding the essence is sufficient. Both PPO and its cousin TRPO share a common goal: they employ conservative updates to prevent significant deviations from the current policy. However, PPO stands out by employing a simplified objective function that eschews the need for calculating additional constraints or the KL divergence. This simplicity has proven to give PPO an edge over TRPO’s more elaborate mechanisms.

Now, let’s explore how PPO functions. It operates in a cycle of two alternating phases. Initially, the policy interacts with the environment, generating data trails which are immediately analyzed using a tailored baseline model to estimate state values and compute advantage functions. Following this, the second phase updates the policy using these trails with a policy gradient method that integrates the advantage function into PPO’s streamlined objective function. The baseline estimator, often a neural network, is also updated to refine its accuracy. This iterative process—switching between collecting data and updating the policy—continues until certain goals, like a specific number of iterations or performance benchmarks, are met.

PPO’s merits, particularly its stability, adaptability, and straightforward design, have made it a preferred choice for complex applications like robotics and gaming AI. The algorithm cleverly balances conservative updates with efficient use of data to optimize policy adjustments.

Despite its successes, PPO faces hurdles, notably in scenarios with sparse rewards or intricate settings. Addressing these issues has led researchers to integrate sophisticated strategies, including hierarchical learning frameworks and predictive controls, into the PPO architecture. Ongoing innovations such as adaptive clipping and variable learning rates are also being investigated to push PPO’s performance further.

7.5 Summary

This chapter delves deeply into several policy optimization algorithms, including policy gradient methods, Actor-Critic, TRPO, and PPO, enriching the reader’s understanding with a mix of theoretical underpinnings and practical insights. Among these, PPO stands out as a particularly revered tool in the field of reinforcement learning due to its robust effectiveness and broad applicability. Known for its straightforwardness and stability, PPO has demonstrated exceptional performance across a range of scenarios. Nevertheless, it is its very limitations on certain fronts that open up generous opportunities for future research, with the goal of pushing the boundaries of the algorithm’s capabilities even further.

Chapter 8 Human feedback reinforcement learning

Abstract

This chapter delves into the critical role of human feedback reinforcement learning (HFRL) in refining and optimizing GPT models, particularly ChatGPT and InstructGPT. The chapter begins by explaining how reinforcement learning, driven by human feedback, enhances model alignment with human expectations. It then outlines the evolution of GPT models, from GPT-3 to GPT-3.5, highlighting key developments like Codex and InstructGPT. The chapter details the three training stages of HFRL—supervised fine-tuning, reward modeling, and reinforcement learning—emphasizing their importance in improving model performance. The discussion extends to the datasets used for fine-tuning, the selection and tasks of annotators, and the challenges associated with multiturn dialogue capabilities. Lastly, the necessity of HFRL is underscored as a means to equip models with robust adaptability and learning capabilities essential for achieving true artificial intelligence.

Keywords

Human feedback reinforcement learning (HFRL); Codex; InstructGPT; supervised fine-tuning; reward modeling; reinforcement learning; proximal policy optimization (PPO); multi-turn dialogue; dataset annotation

Generative pre-trained transformer (GPT) models undergo a crucial pretraining phase where they learn to mimic the distribution of their training datasets. This phase is vital as the quality of the training data significantly influences the content these models generate. To enhance the quality of this content, it is essential to align the model’s outputs with human expectations in a controlled manner. “Alignment” here refers to the match between what the models produce and what humans anticipate. These expectations include not only the fluency and grammatical accuracy of the content but also its relevance, authenticity, and safety.

Reinforcement learning operates through a reward mechanism that directs model training. This mechanism serves as the training’s optimization goal. Rewards are versatile and can vary widely, such as in AlphaGO, where rewards are determined by the game’s outcome. In reinforcement learning, strategies are developed by extensively sampling rewards to estimate the optimization goal, thereby steering the training process. Thus, human feedback can be treated as a form of reward, introducing the concept of reinforcement learning driven by human feedback.

8.1 Reinforcement learning in ChatGPT

In May 2020, OpenAI introduced the Davinci API, boasting an impressive 175 billion parameters. Following this, OpenAI embarked on two significant explorations: Codex, which focuses on coding and reasoning, and InstructGPT, which aimed at better understanding human intentions. The technological developments from GPT-3 to GPT-3.5 are illustrated in Fig. 8.1.

It starts with the initial version of GPT-3, which branches into two: Code and Reasoning leading to the Initial Version of Codex (Code-davinci-001, Code-cushman-001) and Human Intent Understanding leading to the Initial Version of InstructGPT (Instruct-davinci-beta, Text-davinci-001). Both branches converge into LMplusCode Training then instruction tuning, resulting in Code-davinci-002. This further leads to Supervised instruction tuning, branching into Text-davinci-002 and RLHF, which then leads to Text-davinci-003 and ChatGPT. The phases are labeled as GPT-3 Phase and GPT-3.5 Phase.

The first pathway concentrates on boosting abilities in coding and reasoning. Here, OpenAI merged GPT-3 with coding training to create Codex. In July 2021, the initial Codex model was launched, fine-tuning the original GPT-3 using 159GB of Python code. This model eventually evolved into what is now known in the OpenAI API as code-cushman-001, showcasing superior coding and reasoning skills. Despite its comparatively modest text-processing capabilities, the model improved significantly by learning from extensive code designed to tackle mathematical reasoning challenges.

The second pathway delves deeper into understanding human nuances. Google pioneered the use of reinforcement learning from human feedback (RLHF) in 2017. They introduced a method that enhances the performance of reinforcement learning in simulations involving robots and Atari games through feedback derived from human annotations.

To enhance alignment with human intents, OpenAI employed RLHF to refine GPT-3, resulting in the creation of InstructGPT. Initial explorations with RLHF began in 2020 with GPT-3, although these early models sometimes produced inappropriate or hazardous content. It was not until March 2022, detailed in the InstructGPT paper, that OpenAI effectively harnessed RLHF, introducing key APIs such as instruct-davinci-beta and text-davinci-001.

OpenAI’s endeavors in these areas culminated in the development of code-davinci-002 (refer to OpenAI’s model index documentation for more details). While Codex may seem focused solely on coding, code-davinci-002 could be considered the most potent GPT-3.5 variant for natural language processing (NLP)—even surpassing text-davinci-002 and -003. This is likely due to code-davinci-002 being trained on both text and code, and being finely tuned for instruction alignment.

Released in May 2022, the text-davinci-002 model was fine-tuned under supervised instructions based on code-davinci-002. This targeted fine-tuning may have reduced its contextual learning prowess but significantly improved its zero-shot learning capabilities. Later, in November 2022, the text-davinci-003 and ChatGPT (gpt-3.5-turbo) were introduced as different variants fine-tuned through reinforcement learning based on human feedback. Text-davinci-003 reclaimed some contextual learning capabilities that text-davinci-002 had partially lost, likely due to integrating language modeling into the fine-tuning process, and it further enhanced its abilities to understand text intentions, perform logical reasoning, and conduct causal analysis, benefiting from RLHF.
