Released in May 2022, the text-davinci-002 model was fine-tuned under supervised instructions based on code-davinci-002. This targeted fine-tuning may have reduced its contextual learning prowess but significantly improved its zero-shot learning capabilities. Later, in November 2022, the text-davinci-003 and ChatGPT (gpt-3.5-turbo) were introduced as different variants fine-tuned through reinforcement learning based on human feedback. Text-davinci-003 reclaimed some contextual learning capabilities that text-davinci-002 had partially lost, likely due to integrating language modeling into the fine-tuning process, and it further enhanced its abilities to understand text intentions, perform logical reasoning, and conduct causal analysis, benefiting from RLHF.

ChatGPT and InstructGPT share similar training approaches. ChatGPT’s understanding of human intentions, fueled by RLHF and the incorporation of extensive coding examples, particularly those solving mathematical problems and code annotations, has significantly advanced its capabilities in NLP and code generation. Although there are some variations in the training datasets between ChatGPT and InstructGPT, their foundational training methodologies remain largely identical. The subsequent section will use InstructGPT as a case study to detail the training and evaluation strategies employed in ChatGPT|InstructGPT, which leverage reinforcement learning from human feedback.

8.2 InstructGPT training dataset

The training of InstructGPT/ChatGPT is structured around three pivotal stages: supervised fine-tuning (SFT), reward modeling (RM), and reinforcement learning (RL), each associated with its distinct dataset—SFT, RM, and RL datasets, respectively. In the SFT phase, annotators craft manual responses to prompts within the samples. This stage demands considerable human input and requires annotators with a high level of expertise. During the reward modeling phase, annotators rank several (4–9) model-generated outputs. Although the demands on annotators are somewhat less stringent during this phase, they still must be well acquainted with the evaluation criteria to ensure the rankings align with expected standards, thereby preventing inconsistent outcomes.

8.2.1 Sources of fine-tuning datasets

The fine-tuning data for the models mainly derives from two key sources:

  1. 1. Prompts submitted through the OpenAI API, predominantly sourced from the OpenAI Playground. When users opt for the InstructGPT model, they receive a notification alerting them that their submitted prompts may contribute to the training dataset for newer model versions. For privacy and compliance reasons, API data from the actual product are not utilized. Data from the API excludes prompts with repetitive, lengthy prefixes and restricts the number of prompts per user to under 200 to maintain diversity. The datasets are also segmented by user ID to prevent overlaps between training inputs and those in the validation and test sets. Moreover, all prompts that contain personally identifiable information are systematically excluded to safeguard user privacy.
  2. 2. Prompts crafted by annotators, which fall into three categories:
    1. a. Simple tasks: Annotators are asked to create straightforward tasks, ensuring a variety of tasks is covered.
    2. b. Few-sample tasks: Here, annotators develop multiple (query, response) pairs for a given prompt. For example, for a prompt like “Conduct sentiment analysis on this tweet,” the query would be a real tweet, and the response would be classified as either “positive” or “negative.” If K pairs are created, the initial K-1 pairs form the context.
    3. c. User data: This involves taking use cases from the OpenAI API and crafting corresponding prompts, often rewriting them to meet standardization requirements. These types are systematically categorized into 10 distinct classes, as detailed in Table 8.1.

Table 8.1

Distribution and examples of different types of use cases based on user data.
Use case typePercentageExample
Brainstorming11.2%What are the next 10 sci-fi books I should read?
Categorization3.5%Using the text below, rate this person’s level of sarcasm on a scale of 1 to 10 (1 = not at all sarcastic, 10 = extremely sarcastic), and provide an explanation: {text} Rating:
Extraction1.9%Extract all place names from the following article: {news article}
Generation45.6%This is the message I received: {email} Here are some key points of the reply: {message} Write a detailed reply
Rewriting8.4%Rewrite the following text to make it more concise: {very formal text}
Chatting6.6%This is a conversation with the Buddha. Each response is filled with wisdom and love. Me: How can I achieve greater peace?
Specific question2.6%Tell me the differences between hydrogen and helium, using the following facts: {list of facts}
Open question12.4%Who built the Great Wall?
Summarization4.2%Summarize the following text for a second grader: {text}
Other3.5%Search “cowboy” on Google and return the results

Typically, prompts used for brainstorming and open-ended question-and-answer sessions are shorter, whereas prompts for dialogues and summaries tend to be longer.

8.2.2 Annotation standards

The selection criteria for annotators are based on two key aspects:

  1. 1. Consistency in identifying sensitive content. The research team has compiled a dataset that includes sensitive materials, each entry comprising a prompt and its completion. If any section of the prompt or completion features content related to pornography, politics, violence, etc., it is classified as sensitive. Annotators are tasked with determining the sensitivity of the content to gauge the alignment between their judgments and those of the research team.
  2. 2. Consistency in assessing the quality of model outputs. Using API-generated prompts and the varied responses provided by different models, annotators are asked to rank the outputs based on quality. The agreement between the annotators’ and researchers’ rankings is then evaluated to ensure consistency in quality assessment.

Annotators have the following primary duties:

  1. 1. Crafting prompts.
  2. 2. Responding to prompts.
  3. 3. Comparatively ranking outputs from different models for the same prompt.
  4. 4. In the evaluation phase, feeding prompts that contain coarse, sexually explicit, or violent content into an InstructGPT model trained through PPO to assess both the toxicity and fluency of the outputs.

Notably, tasks (3) and (4) require annotators to assess the quality and toxicity of content, providing them with clear operational guidelines.

For task (3), high-quality outputs must satisfy three standards: usefulness, truthfulness, and harmlessness. Typically, truthfulness and harmlessness are more critical than usefulness. However, if an output “a” is significantly more useful than output “b”, and its relative harmfulness and untruthfulness are not overly severe—especially if it doesn’t involve high-risk domains like law or medicine—then output “a” is considered superior to “b.” The fundamental sorting criterion is which output one would expect in response to a machine-posed question.

For task (4), two primary metrics are utilized: toxicity, which determines if the output includes vulgar, disrespectful, or sexually violent content, evaluated both in absolute and relative terms to the prompt’s toxicity; and fluency, which assesses the text’s coherence and natural presence in a digital environment.

In the ranking tasks, the approach involves first allowing annotators to independently rate each output following established standards, followed by a collective display of all outputs for final ranking. This method prevents the bias of simultaneous display and rankings, improving evaluation accuracy.

8.2.3 Data analysis

Table 8.2 outlines the origins and sizes of each dataset:

  • • SFT dataset: Comprising 13,000 prompts jointly supplied by the API and annotators, this dataset is vital for training the SFT model. Annotators provide corresponding answers for these prompts. It is crucial to ensure that each entry in the SFT dataset includes both a prompt and its answer.
  • • RM dataset: Featuring 33,000 prompts, this dataset is contributed by both the API and annotators. Here, annotators are tasked with ranking the model outputs, and this data serves as the training set for the RM model.
  • • PPO dataset: Consisting of 31,000 prompts solely from the API and lacking any annotation, this dataset is utilized specifically for RLHF fine-tuning.

Table 8.2

Data distribution of InstructGPT.

TrainingAnnotator11295TrainingAnnotator6623TrainingUser31144

Appendix A1 of the research paper provides a thorough discussion on the distribution of data used in the training of InstructGPT. The chapter highlights several critical factors potentially influencing the model’s effectiveness:

  1. 1. More than 96% of the data are in English, with the remaining under 4% comprising 20 other languages, such as Chinese, French, and Spanish. This suggests that while InstructGPT is capable of processing inputs in multiple languages, its performance may be less effective with nonEnglish inputs.
  2. 2. There are nine distinct types of prompts, predominantly generative tasks. This indicates that the model might not be fully equipped to handle every type of task.
  3. 3. The 40 outsourced annotators, who are responsible for labeling, are from the USA and Southeast Asia, representing a small and geographically concentrated group. Given that InstructGPT aims to train a model that embodies appropriate values, the relatively narrow diversity of these annotators could introduce biases, particularly on issues of significant concern in specific regions.

This discussion sheds light on InstructGPT’s dataset. Although ChatGPT and InstructGPT employ similar training methodologies, there are notable differences in their dataset compositions. Currently, detailed comparisons of these differences are scarce. Considering ChatGPT’s primary application in conversational settings, it is likely that its dataset differs from InstructGPT in two key aspects: a higher proportion of conversational tasks and a transformation of prompt formats into a question-and-answer style. For a more precise understanding, we await the release of detailed documents, such as ChatGPT’s research papers and source codes.

8.3 Training stages of human feedback reinforcement learning

To more closely align language models with user intentions, supervised learning and reinforcement learning fine-tuning strategies informed by human feedback are utilized. This approach not only expands the capabilities of these models but also enhances their precision in adhering to user directives. As depicted in Fig. 8.2, the human feedback fine-tuning process for InstructGPT involves three principal phases: SFT, training of the reward model, and the reinforcement learning stage. Given that the training methodologies for InstructGPT and ChatGPT share significant similarities, the forthcoming discussion will focus on InstructGPT as a representative example.

Step 1: Collect demonstration data and train a supervised policy. A prompt is sampled from the prompt dataset. A labeler demonstrates the desired output behavior. This data is used to fine-tune GPT-3 with supervised learning. Step 2: Collect comparison data and train a reward model. A prompt and several model outputs are sampled. A labeler ranks the outputs from best to worst. This data is used to train the reward model. Step 3: Optimize a policy against the reward model using reinforcement learning. A new prompt is sampled from the dataset. The policy generates an output. The reward model calculates a reward for the output. The reward is used to update the policy using PPO.

8.3.1 Supervised fine-tuning

SFT is a training technique that utilizes the previously mentioned SFT dataset to fine-tune the pretrained GPT-3 model. Known as the SFT model, it displays the foundational predictive abilities of a baseline model, as depicted in Fig. 8.3, with a parameter count reaching 175 billion. The fine-tuning approach is akin to that used for GPT-1. Specifically, it incorporates a cosine learning rate decay strategy across 16 training epochs, and to mitigate overfitting, a residual dropout rate of 0.2 is established. These measures are taken to ensure that the model effectively learns and adapts to new data during fine-tuning, thereby improving its predictive performance.

The output options are Ornamental Plant, A sour fruit and A banana is a yellow fruit. The correct answer is indicated as A banana is a yellow fruit.

Due to the limited training data volume of only 13K samples, the GPT-3 model is susceptible to overfitting. However, this model serves primarily as an initial baseline for further training and not for direct practical application. Consequently, even if overfitting occurs during the SFT stage, it can still aid in enhancing the performance of later models.

So, is it feasible to align the SFT model with human needs solely through fine-tuning? Clearly, this presents several challenges. For instance, manually crafting answers for all queries can be daunting due to the need for an in-depth understanding of the question content and substantial time, knowledge, and skills to provide diverse, high-quality answers. Thus, while fine-tuning is a potent method of training models, real-world applications might necessitate the integration of additional strategies like reinforcement learning to better meet human requirements.

8.3.2 Reward modeling

Given the impracticality of humans writing answers for every conceivable query, a reward model is trained to discern human preferences. This model processes input prompts and answers to produce a scalar reward value. Illustrated in Fig. 8.4, both API-generated and manually crafted prompts are fed into the SFT model, which then generates answers. These answers are derived through probabilistic sampling and subsequently ranked by annotators. After obtaining the rankings, a reward model is trained by removing the final nonembedding layer of the SFT model and incorporating a linear layer post-Softmax. This configuration maps the output sequence to a scalar value—essentially rating the prompt–response pair.

It starts with a prompt that is fed into an SFT model, which generates multiple outputs labeled A, B and C. Human annotation ranks these outputs as A greater than B greater than C. The next step involves training the reward model using pairs of prompts and outputs (Prompt, A, B), (Prompt, A, C) and (Prompt, B, C). This process is aimed at pairwise ranking loss minimization.

Notably, InstructGPT employs a smaller 6B parameter GPT-3 fine-tuned (SFT) model for training the reward model instead of the maximal 175B parameter version. This choice is due to the larger models’ propensity for instability during training, while smaller models offer computational efficiency.

8.3.3 Reinforcement learning

On the foundation of reward modeling, the PPO algorithm previously discussed is employed to fine-tune the supervised strategy using outputs from the RM as scalar rewards to enhance this reward mechanism. As illustrated in Fig. 8.5, the reinforcement learning phase progresses by further fine-tuning the SFT model. It inputs some unannotated prompts (like “Write a diary entry for a spring outing” from a set of 31k prompts from the API) into the SFT model. At this stage, instead of relying on manual quality evaluations, the reward model developed in the second phase scores the outputs of the SFT model. Initially, the SFT model acts as the policy network within the PPO framework, optimizing parameters to elevate the scores of the generated answers. Responses are then regenerated based on the refined policy, assessed by the reward model, leading to further optimizations of the SFT model parameters in a cycle until the policy achieves optimality.

It involves two main components: GPT3/GPT3.5 and a Reward Model. The connections between these components are as follows: State leads to GPT3/GPT3.5, which then leads to Action. Action connects to the Reward Model, which then provides Reward back to GPT3/GPT3.5.

During the three phases of training with human feedback reinforcement learning (HFRL), the reward model and PPO fine-tuning phase are iteratively refined, gathering more comparative data from the best current strategies for training new reward models and strategies. However, this iterative process introduces the challenge of shifting data distributions. As the model evolves, so does the data distribution—a departure from traditional statistical learning approaches, necessitating continual adaptation to the evolving data environment.

During the process of SFT and reward modeling, one might question why a ranking model is necessary when labeled data already exists—why not proceed directly with training? The answer lies in the nature of the data available; only rankings are available, not explicit outputs y. If outputs y were directly labeled, the approach would fall under supervised learning. However, reinforcement learning typically lacks such explicit labels, necessitating the training of a ranking model to assimilate feedback from the environment. This assists the model in comprehending and adjusting to new data distributions, enabling it to learn effectively within an ever-evolving environment.

In the realm of reinforcement learning, there is a concept known as online learning, prevalent in applications like gaming. Here, the model is in a state of continuous update, engaging with the environment and assimilating instant feedback. Adopting online learning would require a person to constantly provide ranking feedback for the model’s generated responses and update the model accordingly. This approach, however, can be inefficient as aligning the pace of model training with manual annotation rates is challenging.

Thus, training a ranking model Rθ to substitute for human ranking has been adopted to facilitate real-time feedback during the training process. This necessity to train two models arises from the desire to enable the newly trained reinforcement learning model to produce highly ranked responses, enhancing the overall generation quality.

8.4 Reward modeling algorithms

8.4.1 Reward scores

The fundamental principle of reward modeling involves converting manually ranked data into actionable reward scores that are utilized to fine-tune the GPT-3 model’s parameters. To realize this objective, InstructGPT has adopted a cutting-edge technique for reward modeling, depicted in Fig. 8.6. This method assigns a reward score to each input-output combination, representing the preferences of human annotators for that particular pairing.

It starts with a prompt dataset providing sample prompts to the SFT Language Model. The SFT Language Model generates an article, for example, Mars, a barren world of haunting beauty, holds secrets beneath its windswept red sands. This generated article is then scored by humans. The human scoring results are used to train a reward model with preferences. The reward model processes the text and produces ranked outputs based on relevance, ELO, etc.

To handle the inputs of ranking problems—essentially, a set of ranked prompts—the reward model integrates a pairwise ranking loss mechanism. In practice, for each prompt, pairs are chosen from the generated responses Yw and YL, where Yw is ranked higher than YL. The pair comprising X (the prompt) and Yw (the higher-ranked response) is then input into the reward model to compute its reward score. Subsequently, the pair X and YL is processed to determine its reward score. Given that Yw ranks above YL, InstructGPT anticipates a higher reward score for Yw. Consequently, the disparity between the two scores is calculated and converted into a scalar value using logistic loss, effectively translating ranked preferences into a format that guides model optimization.

8.4.2 Loss function

The loss is computed using the following formula:

lossθ=−1K2Ex,yw,yl~Dlogσrθx,yw−rθx,yl. (8.1)

(8.1)

Here, loss(θ) signifies the reward model’s loss function. K represents the count of responses generated for each prompt, and (K2) indicates the number of two-response combinations drawn from K responses. The expectation Ex,yw,yl~D considers the dataset D, comprising tuples of the prompt x, higher preferred response yw, and lower preferred response yl. The sigmoid function σ normalizes inputs to a range between 0 and 1, and rθx,yw denote the reward scores for responses yw and yl, respectively.

This loss function aims to minimize the expected loss across dataset D. Each component of the loss is based on annotators’ preferences for two responses given a specific prompt. The reward model’s role is to predict which response is more favored given a prompt. By minimizing this loss, the reward model distinguishes between different prompt–response pairs, facilitating a reward mechanism for subsequent reinforcement learning stages.

In RLHF, rankings serve as a form of weak supervision. Despite their simplicity, these rankings enable the reward model to effectively learn human preferences. Rankings are preferred over direct scoring because they align more closely with objective realities. For instance, while different annotators may assign varying scores to the same output—some may deem an exceptional text worthy of a 1.0 score, others might rate it at 0.8—such discrepancies could introduce a considerable amount of noise. However, the consistency found in rankings is generally greater, making them a more reliable measure.

To enhance the collection of annotative data, InstructGPT sets the parameter K to 9, thereby generating nine possible answers for each prompt. From these, 36 answer pairs are selected to compute the loss. To maintain consistency in loss values irrespective of changes in K, InstructGPT normalizes the loss by dividing it by 36, adhering to the standard practice of pairwise ranking loss.

Opting for a higher value of K offers dual benefits: it provides a richer set of annotative data, aiding in more effective model training, and it facilitates the reuse of previously calculated values, which enhances computational efficiency. Moreover, to minimize the risk of overfitting, InstructGPT employs a full-order ranking approach. This technique more accurately assesses the quality of each response, improving the model’s ability to generalize across different scenarios. Eventually, the reward model progressively learns to assign scores based on human preferences. This approach, known as deep reinforcement learning guided by human preferences, essentially leverages human preferences as the driving reward mechanism.

8.5 PPO in InstructGPT

In the development of InstructGPT’s reinforcement learning approach, the proximal policy optimization (PPO) algorithm was employed. The strategy network for this PPO setup was the fine-tuned GPT-3 SET model (refer to Fig. 8.7). The training utilized a dataset comprising 31,000 prompts. Each prompt was fed into the active reinforcement learning model, which produced an output y. This output, alongside the prompt (x,y), was then evaluated using a pretrained reward model to assess its effectiveness. The objective was to refine the objective function, enabling the freshly trained model to generate responses deemed superior by human standards.

The prompt dataset is labeled as x: A dog is.... The SFT Model generates base text y: A furry mammal. The SFT Model Fine-Tuned with RLHF adjusts the text to y: Man's best friend with parameter freezing. The text is then processed by the Reward Model (with Preferences) and updated using reinforcement learning algorithms (e.g., PPO) with the formula theta left arrow theta plus gradient sub theta J left parenthesis theta right parenthesis. The KL Prediction Shift Penalty is represented as -lambda sub KL D sub KL left parenthesis pi sub RL left parenthesis y vertical bar x right parenthesis parallel pi sub SFT left parenthesis y vertical bar x right parenthesis right parenthesis.

The objective function can specifically be defined as

Objective (ϕ)=|Ex,y~DπϕRLrθx,y−βlogπϕRLy|xπSFTy|x+|γEx~Dpretrain logπϕRLx. (8.2)

(8.2)

Here, rθ(x,y) denotes the expected reward during the reinforcement learning process, as determined by the trained reward model Rθ. This model quantifies human preferences for outputs y given inputs x. The calculations are based on the data distribution DπϕRL, which is generated from the policy πϕRL(y|x).

The component −βE(x,y)~DπϕRLlogπϕRL(y|x)/πSFT(y|x) in Eq. (8.2) accounts for the Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence. This metric is utilized to moderate the modifications to the model parameters θ, ensuring that the learning policy πϕRL does not stray excessively from the original policy πSFT, thereby maintaining the model’s fidelity to the original training data. Here, β is a hyperparameter that regulates the weight of the KL divergence.

The γEx~Dpretrain logπϕRL(x) term acts as a bias factor, with Dpretrain representing the pretrained data distribution from the GPT-3 SFT model. The model πϕRL(x) reflects the probability distribution postreinforcement learning optimization for given inputs x. The hyperparameter γ, known as the pretraining loss coefficient, moderates the influence of pretraining data on the objective function to prevent ChatGPT from overly optimizing during training, which could significantly impair its performance on general NLP tasks. If γ is set to 0, the configuration is known as the PPO model; otherwise, it’s termed the PPO-ptx model.

8.6 Multiturn dialogue capability

The GPT series, a lineup of large language models, constructs probability distributions for sequences of words. These models take combinations of words within sentences as input conditions to forecast the probability distribution of various words emerging at subsequent moments. In the PPO models utilized by ChatGPT and InstructGPT, the state is defined as the input sentence, while the SFT model, which requires adjustments through RLHF, acts as the agent. This agent generates responses based on the input state, with the policy’s action being the prediction of the next word’s probability distribution. At this juncture, the word with the highest probability is typically selected as the output. A pertinent question arises regarding whether the reward model evaluates each word produced individually or assesses the entire output sequence only after completion.

Illustrated in Fig. 8.8, if the reward function is only activated after the agent has produced a full response, it remains unclear how each action’s output word Xk secures its corresponding reward during the generation phase. The InstructGPT paper does not explicitly address this query. Other studies indicate that for ChatGPT, the sequence is only deemed complete following the end-of-sentence token, and rewards are dispensed solely based on the final response, with no rewards allocated during the intermediate steps.

The environment is represented by a rectangular block labeled Environment, and the agent is represented by another rectangular block labeled Agent. The agent takes an action, denoted as a sub t end sub, which is indicated by an arrow pointing from the agent to the environment. The environment responds with a state, denoted as S sub t end sub and a reward, denoted as R sub t end sub, both indicated by arrows pointing from the environment to the agent. There is also an arrow indicating the probability distribution P of a sub t end sub given the history of states and actions. Additionally, there is a mathematical expression X equals the summation of S sub n end sub and a question mark is shown with an arrow pointing downward from the reward.

Both ChatGPT and InstructGPT are equipped with the capability for multiturn dialogues, yet the foundational documents for InstructGPT do not clarify the training procedures for these extended interactions. Multiturn dialogues demand that the model retain memory of previous interactions, but the reward functions described in the InstructGPT publications only evaluate single input–output pairs. The complexity of multiturn conversations, especially when dealing with references to entities mentioned in earlier exchanges, calls for further exploration.

To effectively manage multiturn dialogues, InstructGPT relies on the ability of the GPT-3 or GPT-3.5 models to process extended input tokens, accommodating segments of past dialogues (up to a limit, such as 8 KB) combined with current inputs. These are fed into the SET model. The data paired with the outputs of the SET model, represented as (x, y), are used to train the PPO model through a pretrained reward system to better align with human conversational patterns.

Moreover, the Transformer’s self-attention mechanism equips the model to address issues of long-distance dependencies, recognizing the relationships between segments of dialogue history and integrating this understanding into response generation. Positional encoding is also employed to differentiate the placement of each historical interaction, ensuring the model accurately interprets the sequence of the conversation. To bolster their multiturn conversational capabilities, both InstructGPT and ChatGPT incorporate substantial multiturn dialogue datasets during their training phases.

8.7 The necessity of human feedback reinforcement learning

As depicted in Fig. 8.9, following the RLHF fine-tuning process, the PPO model equipped with 1.3 billion parameters notably outperforms both the 175 billion parameter SFT model and the GPT-3 model. This superior performance is attributed to the strategy adopted by InstructGPT/ChatGPT of integrating a variety of annotators who are tasked with crafting prompts and ranking outputs, building upon the foundation provided by GPT-3. Moreover, in the reward model’s training phase, greater rewards are assigned to data that more closely mirrors real-world scenarios.

The graph shows the win rate against SFT 175B on the vertical axis, ranging from 0.2 to 0.6 in increments of 0.1. The horizontal axis represents the model size, with data points at 1.3B, 6B and 175B. Five different models are compared: 1. PPO-ptx: Solid line, highest performance 2. PPO: Long dashed line, second-highest performance 3. SFT: Dotted line, middle performance 4. GPT(prompted): Dash-dot line, second-lowest performance 5. GPT: Dash-dot-dot line, lowest performance The win rates for each model at different sizes are approximately: PPO-ptx: 1.3B: 0.52 175B: 0.67 PPO: 1.3B: 0.52 175B: 0.63 SFT: 1.3B: 0.35 175B: 0.5 GPT(prompted): 1.3B: 0.24 175B: 0.41 GPT: 1.3B: 0.14 175B: 0.25 All models show an increasing trend in win rate as the model size increases from 1.3B to 175B. The graph includes error bars at the 1.3B and 175B data points for each model. The detailed data points are as follows: - For a model size of 1.3B, the win rate against SFT 175B for PPO-ptx is 0.52, for PPO is 0.53, for SFT is 0.35, for GPT (prompted) is 0.24 and for GPT is 0.14. - For a model size of 6B, the win rate against SFT 175B for PPO-ptx is 0.58, for PPO is 0.58, for SFT is 0.41, for GPT (prompted) is 0.31 and for GPT is 0.20. - For a model size of 175B, the win rate against SFT 175B for PPO-ptx is 0.67, for PPO is 0.63, for SFT is 0.50, for GPT (prompted) is 0.41 and for GPT is 0.25.

Additionally, InstructGPT conducted comparisons on the TruthfulQA dataset, demonstrating that even the models with fewer parameters, like the 1.3 billion parameter PPO model, exceed the performance of GPT-3 after fine-tuning. Nevertheless, despite enhancements in reducing harmful outputs, these models did not show substantial improvements in tackling issues like discrimination and bias. This may be partly because GPT-3 is already a high-performing model with a low inherent likelihood of generating harmful or biased content. Furthermore, the limited volume of annotated data may constrain InstructGPT’s ability to fully enhance the model’s efficacy in these specific areas.

RLHF fine-tuning might bring about certain drawbacks. For instance, InstructGPT could degrade in performance across general NLP tasks. While modifying the loss function might mitigate this issue, it doesn’t address the root cause. Additionally, InstructGPT could generate outputs that lack logical coherence. This might stem from the limited scope of human feedback, which cannot encompass every possible output scenario, with the most significant influences on model performance originating from supervised language modeling tasks. The likely limitations due to scarce corrective data or misdirection from supervised tasks (focusing solely on model outputs rather than human intent) can lead to unrealistic content generation. This is akin to a student who, despite guidance, cannot be guaranteed to master all concepts.

The models refined through RLHF exhibit heightened sensitivity to prompts, likely due to training with an insufficient range and number of prompts. Furthermore, the model might overanalyze simple concepts because annotators are inclined to reward longer responses more generously during content evaluation. Hence, researchers might need to devise a better approach to leveraging human feedback or develop more sophisticated model architectures.

Significantly, with risky prompts, such as a user requesting an “AI plan to destroy humanity,” InstructGPT could generate detrimental responses. This occurs because InstructGPT presumes user instructions are rational and align with ethical standards, lacking a deeper evaluation of these prompts, thus enabling responses to any input. Although subsequent reward models may assign lower rewards to such outputs, when generating content, the model must consider both its ethical standards and the alignment of its responses with the given instructions. This can sometimes result in outputs that are ethically questionable.

8.8 Summary

This chapter explores in depth the practical application and pivotal role of HFRL within ChatGPT, highlighting its crucial place in the iterative and optimization processes of the model. It begins by examining the contributions of HFRL to the iterations of ChatGPT, detailing its influence on model optimization and decision-making learning processes. The discussion then progresses to an analysis of the composition of the InstructGPT/ChatGPT training datasets, the origins of the fine-tuning datasets, the criteria for selecting annotators, the standards for annotations, and data analysis. This is followed by a comprehensive description of the training phases involved in HFRL, which includes the SFT phase, the reward modeling phase, and the reinforcement learning phase. Each phase is thoroughly described to showcase the ongoing enhancements made to the ChatGPT model for adapting to the dynamic complexities of human language.

In discussing the reward modeling algorithms, the chapter clarifies the underlying concepts and the configuration of loss functions, and discusses how these theories are applied to refine the ChatGPT model. Furthermore, it delves into a detailed analysis of the PPO technique in InstructGPT, emphasizing its crucial role in reinforcement learning. The chapter also underscores the importance of multiturn dialogue capabilities for chatbots and the significant impact of HFRL in bolstering these capabilities.

Finally, the chapter extensively discusses the necessity of HFRL, underscoring that this approach endows the model with robust adaptability and learning capabilities, which are crucial for the realization of true artificial intelligence.

1“Fundamentals of Generative Large Language Models and Perspectives in Cyber-Defense.”

Chapter 9 Low-resource domain transfer of large language models

Abstract

This chapter delves into the challenges and strategies for adapting large language models (LLMs) to specific low-resource domains. It highlights the importance of fine-tuning open-source pretrained models to reduce costs associated with human annotation and computational resources. The chapter introduces several methods like self-instructed annotation, which automates data annotation using LLMs, and constitutional artificial intellignece (AI), which emphasizes ethical alignment in AI outputs. Low-rank adaptation is discussed as a technique to minimize parameter updates during fine-tuning, significantly reducing computational costs. The chapter also covers Quantization and SparseGPT techniques for model compression, ensuring efficient inference without compromising performance. Case studies in healthcare, law, and other sectors illustrate the practical application of these methods, showcasing how tailored LLMs can be effectively deployed in industry-specific contexts.

Keywords

Large language models; domain adaptation; fine-tuning; self-instructed annotation; constitutional AI; low-rank adaptation; Quantization; SparseGPT; industry-specific models

Large language models (LLMs), such as ChatGPT, now possess foundational skills in common sense understanding, planning, and logical reasoning. By incorporating middleware, these models can also integrate external knowledge, thereby boosting their relevance and numerical capabilities. Nonetheless, creating LLMs dedicated to specific industries continues to draw significant interest. This often stems from businesses that rely on industry-specific data to gain a competitive edge and may be reluctant to share their valuable data with a general-purpose LLM.

To lower the costs associated with training and deploying LLMs, whether for adapting to new domains or for creating smaller-scale models within specific sectors, it is common to fine-tune open-source pretrained models—referred to as base models—for specialized tasks. This approach aims to minimize two major expenses: the cost of human-generated annotations and the computational costs of training and deployment.

9.1 Self-instruct

In the training of LLMs, fine-tuning through human feedback plays a pivotal role. While human annotations can boost model accuracy, their high costs and the limited variety of these annotations pose significant challenges in practical scenarios. Most human-generated annotations are typically focused on popular tasks within natural language processing, resulting in a lack of diversity in both the types of tasks and the ways they are described. Thus, finding ways to reduce the costs of manual annotations while preserving their quality, and enhancing the diversity of the data, has become a crucial task for enabling low-cost model transitions in open-source settings.

The self-instructed annotation method offers a cost-effective solution for data annotation. It utilizes a “teacher” LLM that iteratively generates both the tasks and their corresponding inputs and outputs. This process starts with a limited set of manually crafted task seeds and, through iterations, develops new tasks. Each complete task consists of a set of instructions and several input–output pairs (<input, output>).

As depicted in Fig. 9.1, self-instructed annotation includes four stages: generating instructions, identifying classification tasks, creating instances, and filtering.

Step 1: Instruction Generation involves generating instructions from a task pool using a language model (LM). An example task is Instruction: Give me a quote from a famous person on this topic. Step 2: Classification Task Identification uses the LM to identify classification tasks. Step 3: Instance Generation involves creating instances based on the instructions. An example task is Instruction: Determine whether the given text is in favor of AI development or against it. with Output Label: Supports AI Development and Input: I believe AI makes human life more convenient. Another example task is Instruction: Give me a quote from a famous person on this topic. with Input: Courage and Output: The only thing we have to fear is fear itself. — Franklin D. Step 4: Filtering involves filtering the generated instances. The process starts with 175 seed tasks, each with one instruction and one instance.

9.1.1 Instruction generation

By harnessing the contextual learning abilities of LLMs, new instructions are crafted from a handful of existing examples. As illustrated in Fig. 9.1, the process starts with 175 tasks—each comprising instructions and instances—to populate the initial task pool. The method uses iterative rounds to foster a richer variety of instructions. Each round selects eight task instructions for few-shot learning, mixing six human-crafted tasks with two generated by the LLM in a previous round. This blend of human and machine efforts serves to enrich the diversity of instructions. For more details, see Fig. 9.2.

Propose a series of tasks: Task 1: Instruction of existing Task 1. Task 2: Instruction of existing Task 2. Task 3: Instruction of existing Task 3. Task 4: Instruction of existing Task 4. Task 5: Instruction of existing Task 5. Task 6: Instruction of existing Task 6. Task 7: Instruction of existing Task 7. Task 8: Instruction of existing Task 8. Task 9:

9.1.2 Task classification identification

Recognizing the distinct processing needs between classification and nonclassification tasks is crucial. To ascertain whether an LLM-generated instruction pertains to a classification task, a set of 12 classification-specific instructions and 19 nonclassification instructions are prepared as prompts. These are then evaluated by the LLM to identify their nature, as depicted in Fig. 9.3.

The text from top to bottom is as follows: Can the following tasks be considered classification tasks with a limited set of output labels? Task: Given my personality and job, tell me if I am suitable. Is this a classification task? Yes. Task: Give me an example of a time when you had to use your sense of humor. Is this a classification task? No. Task: Replace the placeholders in the given text with appropriate named entities. Is this a classification task? No. Task: Fact-checking — Based on your knowledge and common sense, tell me if this statement is true, false, or unknown. Is this a classification task? Yes. Task: Return this person's SSN number. Is this a classification task? No. Task: Detect if the Reddit thread contains hate speech. Is this a classification task? Yes. Task: Analyze the following sentence to identify bias. Is this a classification task? No.

9.1.3 Instance generation

For each new instruction, appropriate input and output instances are generated based on the type of task. There are two approaches to instance generation: input-first and output-first. With input-first, the model initially crafts the input part based on the instruction, followed by the output. Illustrated in Fig. 9.4, the input-first method in one example generates multiple outputs without needing additional input, while another example includes instruction, input, and output components.

Propose examples for the following tasks. Where possible, try to generate multiple examples. If no additional input is required for the task, you can directly generate the output. Task: Which exercise is best suited for reducing belly fat at home? Output: Lying leg raises, leg adduction and abduction, sit-ups, side planks. Task: Extract all the names of countries in this paragraph and list them separated by commas. Paragraph: Dr. No is the sixth novel by British author Ian Fleming, featuring his British spy James Bond as the protagonist. The novel was written at Fleming's Goldeneye estate in Jamaica and was first published in 1958 by Jonathan Cape in the United Kingdom. In this novel, Bond investigates the disappearance of two MI6 colleagues in Jamaica who were investigating Dr. No. Bond arrives at Dr. No's Caribbean island, where he meets Honeychile Rider, who is collecting shells there. They are captured and taken to a luxurious residence built on a mountain. Dr. No is the son of a German missionary and a Chinese woman and his character is influenced by Sax Rohmer's Manchu stories. Dr. No was Fleming's first novel and received widespread negative reviews in the UK, but was better received in the USA. Output: Jamaica, United Kingdom, Germany, China, USA. Task: New instruction requiring generation of examples.

Input-first methods can lead to biased inputs, particularly noticeable in classification tasks. For instance, in a task aimed at detecting grammar errors, inputs often contain grammatical mistakes. To mitigate this, the output-first method is preferred for classification tasks, where the model first suggests possible category labels, then generates corresponding inputs for each label. For examples of this method, see Fig. 9.5.

Provide the definition of the classification task and category labels and generate an input corresponding to each category label. Correspond to each category label. If the task does not require input, simply generate the correct classification labels. Task: Classify the sentiment of sentences as positive, negative, or neutral. Classification label: Neutral (Output) Sentence: The food at this restaurant is good, but their service is too slow. (Input) Classification label: Positive (Output) Sentence: I had a great day today. The weather was nice and I was with friends. (Input) Task: New instruction requiring generation of examples.

9.1.4 Filtering

To preserve the diversity and quality of instructions, new instructions are added to the task pool only if their overlap with the longest common subsequence of all existing instructions is below a certain threshold. Moreover, instructions containing specific keywords such as “image,” “picture,” or “chart” are excluded since these cannot typically be processed by language models. The process involves filtering out identical instances and those with identical inputs but varying outputs.

As a cost-effective solution for data annotation, self-instructed annotation has become a standard in the realm of low-cost data solutions for open-source models. Yet, its success hinges directly on the capabilities of its “teacher” LLM, potentially leading to a bias towards frequently occurring tasks in the training corpus and a reduced likelihood of generating novel or creative instructions. Moreover, this iterative process might exacerbate certain social biases and provoke intellectual property issues.

9.2 Constitutional artificial intelligence

Constitutional artificial intelligence (CAI) leverages the capabilities of AI systems not just to understand and generate data, but to obtain data annotations in a way that emphasizes safety and responsibility. Unlike traditional methods that primarily seek to minimize costs, CAI focuses on the significant role of AI feedback to enforce content restrictions. This ensures that the AI does not generate harmful content. In CAI, a process known as reinforced learning from AI feedback (RLAIF) replaces the need for human intervention to align AI outputs with safe and harmless content standards. Illustrated in Fig. 9.6, the training of CAI unfolds in two distinct phases: an initial supervised learning (SL) stage followed by a reinforcement learning (RL) stage. This structured approach helps in shaping an AI that is not only effective but also ethically aligned.

The flowchart starts with a Useful RLHF Model leading to generating responses to harmful samples prompted by Red Teaming. This connects to a process involving Response, Evaluation and Modification. The next step is the Fine-tuned Supervised Learning CAI Model (SL-CAI). Another path from generating responses to harmful samples prompted by 'Red Teaming' leads to Self-improving AI Feedback, which then connects to the Fine-tuned Preference Model (PM). The PM and SL-CAI are used to train RLAIF, which finally leads to the RL-CAI Model.

During the SL phase of training, the process involves several critical steps:

  1. 1. The helpful reinforcement learning from human feedback (RLHF) model is prompted to respond to potentially harmful requests. For instance, if asked, “Can you help me hack into my neighbor’s Wifi?” the model might initially respond affirmatively, suggesting an app for the purpose.
  2. 2. The responses are then evaluated against a set of ethical principles. For example, a prompt might ask, “Is the assistant model’s reply harmful, unethical, racist, sexist, toxic, dangerous, or illegal?” The model assesses its previous response, recognizing that hacking into someone’s Wifi is both a privacy violation and potentially illegal.
  3. 3. Based on this evaluation, the assistant model revises its responses to eliminate any harmful content. This iterative process refines the response to advise strongly against such actions due to privacy and legal concerns.

The ultimate goal of this stage is to produce ethically sound, harmless annotated data. For example, from the initial harmful suggestion about hacking Wifi, the model learns to respond: “Hacking into your neighbor’s Wifi is an invasion of their privacy, which I strongly oppose. It could also lead to legal troubles.” This refined response forms the basis for further training the language model, now called the SL-CAI models, which integrates these ethical guidelines into its framework. This approach not only makes the AI safer but also aligns it closely with human values.

The second stage of training involves RL, akin to the RLHF method used by InstructGPT. It starts with the creation of a preference model (PM), designed to capture human preferences. The CAI system then employs the SL-CAI model to produce a range of responses to each harmful prompt. These responses are evaluated and ranked based on their adherence to principles of harmlessness, with the top choices compiled into an AI-generated dataset reflecting harmless preferences.

This dataset, derived from AI evaluations, is then blended with data annotated by humans for helpfulness, which serves to train the PM further. The final step involves refining the SL-CAI model through RL, guided by the PM, culminating in the creation of an RL-CAI model that has been trained through RLAIF.

Both CAI and self-instructed annotation use AI feedback to secure cost-effective annotation data, but CAI specifically focuses on aligning AI outputs with human preferences for nonharmfulness. This focus is especially crucial for LLMs that are intended for public service, positioning it as a key player in offering harmless solutions during the cost-effective adaptation of open-source large models.

9.3 Low-rank adaptation

LLMs undergo essential stages of pretraining and fine-tuning, each necessitating adjustments across all model parameters. However, as these models grow in complexity and size, the cost of retraining becomes prohibitively high. An effective strategy to mitigate this issue involves decomposing the weight matrices and selectively adjusting only a subset of weights. This approach can drastically reduce the number of parameters that need modification during fine-tuning, significantly cutting down the costs associated with updating and refining these powerful tools.

9.3.1 Model training and deployment

Low-rank adaptation (LoRA) is an innovative approach used in neural networks to train certain dense layers by optimizing rank-decomposed matrices, all while leaving the pre-trained weights intact. In simpler terms, “low rank” means the matrix has a smaller rank, allowing it to be represented with fewer vectors, enhancing efficiency. Dense layers in neural networks, which often handle matrix multiplication, typically have fully ranked weight matrices. However, during incremental pretraining, the adjustments to these weights show a low “intrinsic dimensionality,” indicating that these changes can be confined to a smaller subspace, which significantly reduces both computational and storage demands.

For example, in the GPT-3 model with 175 billion parameters, the full rank might reach as high as 12,288. Despite this, the actual adjustments in the weight matrices during fine-tuning can be maintained at a remarkably low rank (such as 1 or 2 as shown in Fig. 9.7), which suffices for maintaining performance. This ability of LoRA to manage weight adjustments with minimal ranks offers significant advantages in terms of both storage and computational efficiency. The technique operates as follows:

The diagram illustrates the structure of a pre-trained weight matrix W in R superscript d times r end superscript. The components include a rectangular block labeled h at the top, a rectangular block labeled x at the bottom and a large rectangular block labeled Pre-trained Weight Matrix W in R superscript d times r end superscript on the left. On the right, there are two trapezoidal shapes, the top one labeled B equals 0 and the bottom one labeled A equals N(0, sigma squared). The diagram shows arrows pointing from h and x to the pre-trained weight matrix and from the pre-trained weight matrix to A and B, indicating the flow of information.

For the initially trained weight matrix W0 which is a d×k matrix, the updates to its weights, ΔW, can be effectively managed by using a technique called low-rank decomposition. This is done by expressing W0+Δ as W0+BA, where B is a d×r matrix and A is a r×k matrix. Importantly, the rank rrr is much smaller than the smaller of d and k (rmin(d,k)), which helps in reducing complexity. During the training phase, W0 remains unchanged and only receives updates through gradients, whereas A and B are adjusted as the training progresses. It is key to note that both W0 and the update ΔW=BA interact with the same input, and their output vectors are added together element by element. For the computation of hhh using h=W0x, the adjusted forward process includes the following:

h=W0x+ΔWx=W0x+BAx. (9.1)

(9.1)

Fig. 9.7 depicts the LoAR method, where the focus is on training only the matrices A and B while keeping the weight matrix unchanged. At the start of the training, A is set up with random values from a Gaussian distribution and B begins at zero. This setup ensures that initially, ΔW=BA is zero. As training progresses, ΔW is scaled by α/r, with α being a fixed constant and r the rank of the matrices.

Models trained with the LoAR algorithm are highly efficient in real-world applications, as they allow for immediate deployment without any delay in inference. This is possible because the model's parameters W are explicitly calculated and stored as W=W0+BA. If there is a need to shift to a different task, you can simply revert back to the base matrix W0 by subtracting BA and then incorporating a new combination B′A′. This capability of swapping out A and B, while keeping the core model parameters unchanged, not only drastically reduces the need for additional storage but also minimizes the overhead associated with task transitions. Such features make the LoAR algorithm particularly valuable for managing multiple tasks efficiently and flexibly.

9.3.2 Choice of rank

LoRA can be utilized across various neural network architectures to lessen the number of parameters that need training. In the context of LLMs, which are typically built on the Transformer architecture, there are several key matrices involved: four in the self-attention module (Wq, Wk, Wv, Wo) and two in the MLP module. When viewing the self-attention weights collectively as a single matrix with dimensions dmodel×dmodel, it becomes evident that LoRA’s application can be highly effective. According to Table 9.1, training these matrices with LoRA using ranks as low as 1 or 2 results in performance on the WikiSQL and MultiNLI datasets that closely matches that achieved with higher ranks of 8 or 64. A rank of 2 proves adequate for capturing essential information in ΔW, making it more advantageous to apply LoRA across several matrices rather than focusing on higher ranks for individual weights.

Table 9.1

Impact of reduced rank on the performance of the Transformer model.
 Weight typer=1r=2r=4r=8r=64
WikiSQL (±0.5%)Wq68.869.670.570.470.0

9.4 Quantization

Quantization is a key strategy used to cut down on the computational demands of large models during inference. This technique works by lowering the precision of weight values, replacing high-precision numbers with lower-precision equivalents. For instance, switching from FP32 to INT8 weights can reduce the data size of weights by four times, boosting the speed at which the model operates. Additionally, on certain hardware, the use of INT8 instructions can enhance throughput.

Nevertheless, in massive language models like OPT-175B and ChatGPT, which have extensive parameter counts and large weight matrices and feature tensors, there are often outlier weights that deviate significantly from the average. These outliers, making up more than 1% of the weights and displaying a clear long-tail distribution, pose a challenge. During quantization, if a uniform precision is applied, these outliers might be pushed to the extreme values (either maximum or minimum), causing a notable shift in the weight distribution from what it was originally. Such discrepancies can drastically affect the model’s accuracy, potentially leading to significant losses in precision.

To tackle the problem of precision loss during quantization, a sophisticated method called hybrid precision quantization, specifically the LLM.int8() approach, has been developed. This method cleverly combines vector-wise quantization with a mixed-precision decomposition strategy to precisely control the quantization precision across different areas of the model, thus neutralizing the adverse effects caused by outliers. This technique not only maintains the model’s performance but also reduces computational and storage costs significantly.

As depicted in Fig. 9.8, vector-wise quantization systematically categorizes the features and weights into different vector regions, either row-wise or column-wise, and assigns appropriate quantization parameters to each. The conversion of these features and weights into INT8 integers allows the quantization process to perform dot products that yield INT32 outputs (from INT8 to INT32). The dequantization step then converts these INT32 results back to FP16 precision (from INT32 to FP16).

It includes two main sections: 8-bit Vector-wise Quantization and 16-bit Mixed Precision Decomposition. The input matrices X and W are shown with regular values and outliers labeled as FP16. In the 8-bit Vector-wise Quantization section, the steps are: (1) Find the vector-wise constants C sub W end sub and C sub X end sub, (2) Quantization X sub F16 end sub times (127 over C sub X end sub) equals X sub 18 end sub and W sub F16 end sub times (127 over C sub W end sub) equals W sub 18 end sub, (3) Int8 Integer Inner Product X sub 18 end sub W sub 18 end sub equals Out sub 132 end sub and (4) Dequantization Out sub 132 end sub times (C sub X end sub circled times C sub W end sub) over 127 times 127 equals Out sub F16 end sub. In the 16-bit Mixed Precision Decomposition section, the steps are: (1) Anomalous Value Decomposition and (2) FP16 Matmul X sub F16 end sub W sub F16 end sub equals Out sub F16 end sub. The final output is labeled as Out sub FP16 end sub.

For those vector regions with outliers, LLM.int8() applies 16-bit matrix multiplication to ensure accurate computation, incorporating these results into the overall quantized outputs, while the remaining 99.9% of the model uses 8-bit multiplication. This method effectively handles outliers and prevents significant precision losses during quantization. Thus, in massive language models containing up to 175 billion parameters, LLM.int8() ensures efficient inference without degrading performance.

9.5 SparseGPT

Pruning is a technique used to compress models by removing parts of the network. This can include anything from individual weights (unstructured pruning) to whole rows or columns of weights in matrices (structured pruning). While this approach works well for smaller models, like those used in visual tasks and smaller language models, it poses challenges for large-scale models such as GPT. The main issue is the significant loss in accuracy, which requires extensive retraining to correct, making it an expensive process. To tackle this, SparseGPT, a sophisticated algorithm for large-scale approximate sparse regression, has been developed to address these drawbacks effectively.

Fig. 9.9 provides a visual depiction of how the SparseGPT reconstruction algorithm works. The process starts with a fixed pruning mask M. Using the inverse Hessian sequence HUj−1, the algorithm progressively updates the remaining weights in each column of the weight matrix W. Specifically, the weights next to the pruned areas (shown in dark) are adjusted to make up for the loss caused by pruning, while the weights that haven’t been pruned (shown in light) remain unchanged.

On the left, there is a matrix labeled M. To the right, there is a sequence of matrices labeled W, showing the process of pruning and updating. The sequence continues with matrices representing the inverse Hessian sequence, labeled as (H sub v sub 1 end sub) superscript -1 end superscript, (H sub v sub 2 end sub) superscript -1 end superscript, (H sub v sub 3 end sub) superscript -1 end superscript and (H sub v sub 4 end sub) superscript -1 end superscript. The process involves pruning, updating and elimination steps, with arrows indicating the flow between matrices.

Most traditional pruning methods, like gradual magnitude pruning, need a lot of retraining to regain accuracy after weights have been removed. But for huge models like GPT, which require extensive computing power and intricate parameter adjustments, relying on retraining to fine-tune the model postpruning becomes unfeasible. Thus, using these gradual pruning approaches for models as large as GPT is not practical.

SparseGPT is specially designed for GPT-scale models as a posttraining method that does not require any fine-tuning. Its main goal is to make the model lighter and less complex by selectively removing parameters, a process known as sparsification. Alongside this, quantization methods like ZeroQuant, LLM.int8(), and nuQmm are popular for compressing models by reducing the precision of parameters. These methods, however, can struggle with anomalies such as outlier features. On the other hand, the GPTQ quantization technique uses a novel approach by employing approximate second-order information to accurately reduce weights to just 2–4 bits, making it ideal for very large models. Combined with powerful GPU kernels, GPTQ can speed up inference by two to five times.

Thus, SparseGPT serves as a complement to quantization methods, focusing on reducing the physical size of the model through sparsification while GPTQ focuses on quantization. In practice, combining these two approaches can lead to the best outcomes in terms of model compression and speeding up inference. Additionally, SparseGPT supports not just unstructured pruning but also semistructured patterns like the n:m sparse formats (2:4 and 4:8), which can be optimized for speed on Ampere NVIDIA GPUs at a 2:4 ratio. This means that SparseGPT can effectively compress the model and enhance inference speed through both sparsification and semistructured pruning, all while maintaining the model’s performance. Importantly, SparseGPT operates locally, updating weights after each pruning step to maintain the integrity of each layer’s input–output relationship. These updates are performed independently of global gradient information, as the primary objective of SparseGPT is to streamline computational demands without compromising performance. Hence, instead of optimizing all parameters globally, SparseGPT strategically updates weights locally after each pruning step.

The GPT model’s extensive parameters enable SparseGPT to derive lighter, sparse versions from heavily parameterized, dense models. These sparse models closely mimic the performance of the original dense models but with much less computational complexity. Moreover, SparseGPT uses perplexity as a sensitivity measure, requiring that the sparse model’s outputs closely match those of the dense model. This is challenging because it’s critical to maintain important parameters that significantly impact model performance during the pruning and weight update processes.

In vast models, there is a high degree of redundancy, especially with unstructured (element-wise) or semistructured (vector-wise) sparsification. This means many parameters can be removed without substantially affecting performance. Compared to structured pruning, these sparsification methods are less likely to lose precision, offering more flexibility in choosing which parameters to prune. SparseGPT enables substantial model compression and its effective sparse regularization can significantly cut training costs. This effectiveness is maintained by locally updating weights after each pruning step, preserving each layer’s input–output relationship while minimizing model size.

However, deploying this type of sparse model for inference requires hardware and software that support sparse computational tasks. To fully benefit from SparseGPT’s compression, there may be a need to develop new sparse computing libraries or enhance existing ones to handle sparse operations better. For optimal compression and acceleration, it’s crucial to extend the use of the SparseGPT algorithm during model training. This involves further research into effectively integrating SparseGPT in the training of large-scale models to reduce computational demands without compromising performance.

9.6 Case studies

9.6.1 Base model

Meta's LLaMA, an open-sourced pretrained model released on February 24, 2023, stands out as a top choice among models designed for platforms with low computational power. It is available in various sizes, including 7B, 13B, 33B, and 65B parameters, as detailed in Table 9.2. The training data for LLaMA included a massive 1.4 trillion tokens, sourced primarily from CommonCrawl (67%), followed by the C4 dataset (15%). Other significant data contributions came from Github, Wikipedia, and Books (each contributing 4.5%), with smaller percentages from ArXiv (2.5%) and StackExchange (2%).

Table 9.2

Versions of LLaMA with parameter scales.
Paramater sizeDimensionn Headsn LayersLearning rateSample sizeToken
6.7B409632323.0e−44M1.0T
13.0B512040403.0e−44M1.0T
32.5B665652601.5e−44M1.4T
65.2B819264801.5e−44M1.4T

Data Source: Academic Pape “Llama: Open and efficient foundation language models.”

LLaMA has demonstrated superior performance over the similarly sized GPT-3 in several benchmarks, including tasks such as commonsense reasoning, question answering, mathematical reasoning, code generation, and language understanding. Notably, the 13B version of LLaMA outperforms GPT-3, which has as many as 175B parameters, in multiple tests. Moreover, the 65B version of LLaMA holds its own against DeepMind’s Chinchilla with 70B parameters and Google’s PaLM with 540B parameters, showing competitive results.

9.6.2 Instruction-following model

The Alpaca model, launched by Stanford University in March 2023, is a low-power transfer model designed to be efficient and effective. It is built on Meta’s LLaMA 7B as the foundational model. Alpaca utilizes a unique instruction bootstrapping method discussed in Section 9.1, leveraging ChatGPT to create a 52,000-entry dataset. It then uses the Lora algorithm to fine-tune the LLaMA model to better follow instructions. Its conversational abilities are nearly on par with those of GPT-3.5 (text-davinci-003), as illustrated in Fig. 9.10.

The diagram illustrates the process of generating instructions using a bootstrapped instruction model and fine-tuning Alpaca 7B. Text-davinci-003 is used to generate instructions from 175 Bootstrap Instruction Seed Tasks. These instructions are then used to create 52K Instruction-Output Data Samples. LLAMA 7B is fine-tuned using these data samples through supervised fine-tuning to create Alpaca 7B. Example Seed Task: Instruction: Brainstorm New Year's resolutions. Output: Learn LLM, Exercise, Get plenty of rest. Example Generated Task: Instruction: Brainstorm creative ideas for a quick move. Output: Use different sizes of cardboard boxes to categorize items.

This approach to instruction fine-tuning is similar to the initial stage of human feedback RL, known as the supervised fine-tuning (SFT) stage, but it does not encompass the entire human feedback process. Despite this, Alpaca offers a highly economical solution for model transfer, requiring only 3 h of training on eight 80GB A100 GPUs, costing approximately $500 in total. The release of Alpaca has inspired the development of several similar models, which enhance the Meta LLaMA model by incorporating more training data and using Lora’s instruction fine-tuning technique. Alpaca sets a model where a larger model serves as a “Teacher,” generating training data that allows a smaller “Student” model to absorb and replicate the larger model’s knowledge and capabilities.

9.6.3 Medical field

ChatDoctor is a project that adapts open-source, large-scale language models for use in the medical field without requiring extensive computational resources. Illustrated in Fig. 9.11, this model uses dialog data from patient–doctor interactions to fine-tune the Meta LLaMA base model and combines it with external knowledge from sources like Wikipedia or medical databases. This integration helps the model provide accurate medical information and generate high-quality responses within the medical domain.

The sources of the dialog data include EBIX, ADAM Suite of Healthcare Products, Healthcare Magic and iClinic The Virtual Hospital. This data is used to fine-tune the Meta AI LLaMA model, which is then used by ChatDoctor. An example conversation is shown where a patient says, Doctor, I have a stomach ache. The response from ChatDoctor is, Thank you for your consultation. If the pain is in the upper part of your abdomen, it could be Peptic Ulcer Disease (PUD), or if it's on the right side... Since I haven't seen your reports, please provide me with more details.

Here’s how the training process unfolds: initially, the model is fine-tuned using 52,000 instructional entries derived from Stanford University’s Alpaca project. Following this, it undergoes a second round of fine-tuning using real patient–doctor dialog data sourced from HealthCareMagic, an online medical consultation platform. This site contributes approximately 100,000 real dialog entries, which are carefully selected and anonymized to remove any personal information, and also corrected for any grammatical inaccuracies.

To improve its accuracy, ChatDoctor has built a knowledge base using Wikipedia and specialized medical databases. This allows the model to pull real-time, authoritative information needed to answer questions in the medically critical context where mistakes can have serious consequences. Additionally, it has developed a comprehensive database that includes information on approximately 700 diseases, detailing symptoms, diagnostic tests, and recommended treatments, establishing a robust standard for medical data.

Illustrated in Fig. 9.12, ChatDoctor enhances its ability to integrate this extensive knowledge by employing a keyword mining approach. This method helps identify essential terms needed to search for relevant information. The system then uses these terms to find and retrieve the most pertinent sections from the knowledge base.

Q: What is the treatment plan for shingles? The illustration shows ChatDoctor using autonomous knowledge retrieval to access relevant medical knowledge from a disease database and Wikipedia. The final diagnosis is provided by ChatDoctor. The treatment plan for shingles typically involves several aspects: 1. Antiviral Treatment: Medications such as acyclovir, valacyclovir and famciclovir are used to reduce viral replication, shorten the course of the disease and alleviate symptoms, particularly when used early in the disease. 2. Pain Management: Nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs) such as ibuprofen, or stronger painkillers like codeine, are used to relieve pain. If you or someone you care about is showing symptoms of shingles, it is advisable to consult a doctor promptly to receive proper diagnosis and treatment.

Recognizing that the model cannot process all data from the disease database simultaneously, ChatDoctor allows the model to access the data in batches. It autonomously selects the most relevant entries that could help address the query. These selected entries are then fed back into the model to help it generate a well-informed and accurate answer. By letting a language model, which has been fine-tuned with medical data, select and utilize information from an external knowledge base, ChatDoctor significantly boosts the reliability and precision of responses to medical inquiries.

9.6.4 Judicial field

LawGPT_zh, LexiLaw, Lawyer LLaMA, and JusticGPT are examples of how LLMs are being adapted for the legal sector in China, using less computational power. Lawyer LLaMA and JusticGPT use the LLaMA 13B model as their foundation, whereas LawGPT_zh and LexiLaw are based on the ChatGLM-6B model.

All these models use a training approach known as LoRA, which typically unfolds in two stages. In the first stage, the base models are pretrained on extensive collections of legal documents and statutes, providing a deep understanding of legal language and context. The second stage focuses on refining these models further through instruction fine-tuning, specifically using datasets designed for legal dialog and question answering. The sources for this data are detailed in Table 9.3.

Table 9.3
