Books and journalsLegal dictionaries, e-books, scholarly articles, etc.

During the second phase of fine-tuning with instructions, not only are Q&A data from legal services websites utilized, but an innovative instruction bootstrapping method is also introduced. This approach uses LLMs like ChatGPT to either generate new Q&A entries or refine and optimize existing ones. For instance, LawGPT_zh, as illustrated in Fig. 9.13, uses a method called reliable-self-instruction, which is grounded in specific knowledge. By feeding ChatGPT detailed texts about legal knowledge, it generates a series of questions that are closely linked to the legal content and its logical implications. ChatGPT then answers these questions using a “text segment-question” format. This process creates legally informative answers and ensures their accuracy.

The diagram illustrates the process of generating questions and answers using ChatGPT based on a legal text. The first section shows a box labeled Law Context approximately 500 Tokens with a note below it saying Please generate five questions based on the above legal text. This is connected to a ChatGPT box, which then leads to another box listing Question 1: XXXXXX, Question 2: XXXXXX, Question 3: XXXXXX, Question 4: XXXXXX and Question 5: XXXXXX. The second section shows a box labeled Question 1: XXXXXXX Answer 1: XXXXXXXXXXXX, which is connected back to the ChatGPT box.

While models like LawGPT_zh, LexiLaw, and Lawyer LLaMA are primarily used for legal consultation, JusticGPT stands out as an experimental judicial decision support model developed by Xiangtan University’s Smart Justice research team. Built upon a LLM, JusticGPT is finely tuned to better understand and generate legal language, handling everything from legal terms to case analysis and generating recommendations for legal decisions. Additionally, it uses an extensive external knowledge base that stores a wealth of cases and judicial documents as vectors, enhancing the model’s decision-making power.

Illustrated in Fig. 9.14, consider a scenario where a user submits a legal query. This query is first converted into a vector, which is then matched against a database using a similarity algorithm to identify the top relevant cases. These selected cases provide context that, along with the initial query, is fed into JusticGPT as a prompt. JusticGPT assesses both the context and the query to produce a ranked list of case similarities.

The process starts with 1. Similar Case Query where a user submits a question to the Web Service, which returns an answer. The question is also sent to the Business Service. 2. Query Vectorization: The Business Service sends the question to the Embeddings Model, which returns a question vector. 3. Query Vector Database: The question vector is used to query the vector database, which returns the top N matching knowledge (facts) from the Judicial Document Vector Database. 4. Construct Prompt: The query case and the top N similar cases are used to construct a prompt. 5. Return answer: The constructed prompt is sent to JusticeGPT LLM, which ranks the top N cases and returns the answer.

JusticGPT is a pioneering project that marries advanced language processing technology with a vast legal database. It aims to dramatically improve the efficiency and accuracy of typical judicial decisions, paving the way for genuine AI-assisted decision-making in the legal field.

9.7 Summary

Fine-tuning or transferring language models for specific industries typically involves adapting open-source pretrained models to perform particular tasks within those sectors. This adaptation faces two main challenges: reducing the cost associated with human annotation and minimizing the computational resources required for both training and deployment.

Most current open-source models that have been transferred are only fine-tuned with instructions, which is similar to the SFT phase found in RLHF. Although there are RLHF versions of the LLaMA model, such as ChatLLaMA in English and ColossalChat, these models don't significantly outperform in terms of size or task effectiveness. Additionally, tailoring models for specific sectors involves creating specialized reward systems and using proximal policy optimization for fine-tuning with data from those sectors, which adds to the cost of data annotation.

There is notable enthusiasm for applying large open-source language models to sectors like healthcare, law, finance, banking, and education, with numerous entities exploring and launching tailored models.

It’s crucial to highlight that Meta’s LLaMA model is not for commercial use. Moreover, datasets used for instruction fine-tuning, such as the Alpaca expansion set, are generated using OpenAI’s language model application programming interface (API) and instruction bootstrapping techniques. Using this data commercially could lead to conflicts with OpenAI and pose risks related to intellectual property.

Chapter 10 Middleware

Abstract

This chapter explores the evolving role of middleware in enhancing large language models (LLMs) like ChatGPT. It addresses the challenges these models face, such as the difficulty of updating knowledge and verifying information accuracy. Middleware solutions, including LangChain and AutoGPT, are introduced to tackle these issues by integrating external knowledge, improving reasoning abilities, and managing complex workflows. LangChain is detailed as a comprehensive framework offering tools for model integration, prompt management, memory handling, and workflow orchestration. AutoGPT is discussed as an advanced agent that autonomously manages tasks with minimal human input. The chapter also examines competitors like Microsoft’s Semantic Kernel and GPT-Index, highlighting the growing competition in middleware frameworks. Despite challenges, middleware is poised to significantly impact small and medium-sized enterprises by boosting productivity through LLMs.

Keywords

Middleware; large language models; LangChain; AutoGPT; task automation; memory management; workflow integration; Semantic Kernel; ChatGPT plugins

Large language models (LLMs) like ChatGPT face two main challenges. Firstly, because these models are so complex, updating them can be expensive and cumbersome. As a result, it’s hard to keep their knowledge up to date. Secondly, they can’t check the correctness of the information they generate. To overcome these issues, it’s critical to incorporate external knowledge and improve their ability to reason, especially in areas like math and logic. Moreover, to effectively use these models across different professional fields, users need specialized tools that can handle long-term memory and manage detailed workflows. This need has spurred the development of a middleware ecosystem around LLMs. Over the next 3–5 years, this middleware market is expected to grow quickly, focusing on enhancing models’ access to external knowledge, boosting their reasoning skills, refining user prompts, and streamlining workflows.

10.1 LangChain

LangChain is an open-source framework that helps developers create applications from start to finish using LLMs. It’s part of a lively open-source community. Normally, these models are accessed through representational state transfer (REST) application programming interfaces (APIs), but LangChain goes beyond just connecting to these APIs. It tackles the integration of LLMs with a company’s internal resources like documents, databases, and platforms such as SharePoint. It also facilitates interaction with various systems like systems, applications, and products (SAP), enterprise resource planning (ERP), customer relationship management (CRM), and human resources (HR), and can manage IT ticketing systems. LangChain keeps track of conversations, seamlessly integrates prompts into the programming, optimizes the use of tokens, and ensures operations stay within set service limits and restrictions. Additionally, LangChain serves as an orchestrator in business workflows, efficiently managing inputs and outputs from different sources like OpenAI, Azure Search, and various databases.

LangChain is a robust collection of tools and components designed to make it easier to develop applications with LLMs. It delivers these seven key capabilities:

Model: LangChain wraps up the interfaces for calling LLMs, supporting three types: text mode, dialog mode, and embedding mode. In text mode, it inputs and outputs plain text strings. In dialog mode, it handles the context of conversations, taking in and outputting chat messages. In embedding mode, it inputs text and outputs a series of text token embeddings. LangChain’s ability to interface with multiple LLMs on the market lets developers switch easily between them, reducing reliance on any single platform.

Prompt: LangChain offers a comprehensive library of prompt templates suitable for different uses. It supports basic text and dialog interactions and allows for customized formatting of outputs, which simplifies tasks like switching between different underlying models, managing, and optimizing prompts (Fig. 10.1).

It consists of an input variable leading to a prompt template, which then connects to an LLM and finally produces an output. The entire process is enclosed within a dashed boundary labeled Application.

Memory: LangChain’s memory module is tailored for complex uses like maintaining ongoing states across different requests and sessions in a chain or agent calls, which helps LLMs remember past interactions. It includes standardized interfaces for managing contexts, multiple implementations of these contexts, and examples of chains or agents that utilize this context management.

Index: Illustrated in Fig. 10.2, the indexing module is crucial for integrating external knowledge into LLMs. It includes interfaces for reading and writing various document types, a text slicing tool to break down large documents, and a token interface for turning documents into vectors, enhancing the model’s ability to digest and utilize large amounts of data.

The process starts with an Input Variable, which connects to a Prompt Template. This Prompt Template is linked to an LLM. The LLM is connected to a Database. The Database is connected to a Search component, which is linked to another Prompt Template. This second Prompt Template is connected to another LLM, which finally connects to the Output.

Chain: For more complex applications, simply building prompts isn’t enough. A “chain” involves sequences of calls, not just to a single LLM, but potentially to other utilities as well. This includes linking LLMs to other sources like search APIs or external databases to expand their capabilities. LangChain facilitates this with standardized interfaces, seamless integration with other tools, and provides practical examples of end-to-end chains for common use cases.

A straightforward example (illustrated in Fig. 10.3) is the “self ask with search” chain. This setup combines the OpenAI API with the SerpApi (a Google search API), enabling the LLM to methodically inquire and ultimately determine the hometown of the winner of the US Open tennis championship.

The code reads as follows: from langchain import SelfAskwithSearchchain, OpenAI, SerpAPIChain llm equals OpenAI left parenthesis temperature equals 0 right parenthesis search equals SerpAPIChain left parenthesis right parenthesis selfaskwithsearch equals SelfAskwithSearchChain left parenthesis llm equals llm, searchchain equals search right parenthesis selfaskwith_search.run left parenthesis double quote Where is the hometown of the defending champion in the men's singles of the US Open? double quote right parenthesis The output shows a series of questions and answers: Where is the hometown of the defending champion in the men's singles of the US Open question mark, Is a follow-up question required: Yes. Follow-up question: Who is the defending champion of the US Open? Intermediate answer: Carlos Alcaraz. Further question: What is the nationality of Carlos Alcaraz question mark, Intermediate answer: Spanish, from Murcia, El Palmar. Therefore, the final answer is: Spain, Murcia, El Palmar. The output below the code is formatted as a string with newline characters indicating line breaks.

Agent: The agent module is even more advanced than the chain module. As shown in Fig. 10.4, agents are not merely about splitting up workflows. They empower LLMs to independently choose their actions and the tools they use. This can include search engines, databases, any form of input or output strings, and could even involve interfacing with another LLM, chain, or agent. LangChain equips these agents with standardized interfaces, offers a selection of different agent types, and provides practical examples of how agents can be implemented from start to finish.

The workflow starts with an input variable, which is processed by an agent. The agent consists of a prompt template and an LLM. The agent interacts with a database, REPL and search components. The final output is generated after processing through these components.

Callback: The callback module boosts how events within chains or agents are observed and examined, simplifying the process of tracking and managing them.

LangChain offers developers a robust suite of tools and components that simplify the task of building applications with LLMs. LangChain not only facilitates the integration of external knowledge sources into models like ChatGPT but also improves these models’ abilities in areas like mathematical reasoning. This enhancement has boosted the performance of LLMs across various tasks and fields. Consequently, LangChain has risen to become the leading middleware framework for LLMs, gaining widespread support from third parties (Table 10.1).

Table 10.1

Features provided by LangChain or supported integrations.
Data preprocessingUnstructuredIO, Airbyte…
Data indexingGPT-Index…

10.2 AutoGPT

AutoGPT capitalizes on the strengths of LLMs for task planning and serving as intelligent agents. Once it is set with one or more long-term objectives, AutoGPT can autonomously create and modify prompts to interact with LLMs, initiate and prioritize tasks, and focus on new tasks first. This cycle continues until all set goals are fulfilled. Moreover, AutoGPT manages short-term and long-term memories using vector databases and files and can execute various Internet-based tasks like searching, form filling, and interacting with APIs.

AutoGPT leverages the task planning and smart agent functions of LLMs, processing task goals and execution feedback in a self-evolving manner. This is distinct from ChatGPT, which relies on manual commands for each task. AutoGPT’s minimal human input requirement is a critical feature, allowing it to not only generate text but also manage entire projects. It has been effectively utilized in creating websites, generating blogs, analyzing markets, formulating trading strategies, writing product reviews, and crafting marketing plans.

Projects similar to AutoGPT include Baby artificial general intelligence (AGI) and AgentGPT. Baby AGI, utilizing OpenAI and Chromea, automates task creation, priority setting, and execution, with new tasks derived from previous outcomes and set objectives. AgentGPT provides a user-friendly web interface, enabling users to create autonomous AI/AGI agents that coordinate tasks to reach goals with minimal user interaction. AgentGPT’s browser-based functionality requires no installation, offering an advantage over AutoGPT and similar tools. Furthermore, VisualGPT and HuggingGPT represent specialized cases of autonomous agents and task planning in multimodal contexts.

As depicted in Fig. 10.5, AutoGPT, powered by GPT-4, enhances its performance over time through learning from experience and feedback. It autonomously generates and executes long-term business strategies and models. Key functionalities include the following.

The components are: ES Quantization Library, Execution Agent (GPT-4), Task Creation Agent (GPT-4), Task Queue, Task Prioritization Agent (GPT-4) and a User. The process is as follows: (1) The User provides a task to the Task Creation Agent (GPT-4). (2) The Task Creation Agent (GPT-4) queries text memory from the ES Quantization Library and adds a new task to the Task Queue. (3) The Execution Agent (GPT-4) sends task results to the Task Creation Agent (GPT-4). (4) The Task Creation Agent (GPT-4) adds new tasks to the Task Queue. (5) The Task Prioritization Agent (GPT-4) determines task priority. (6) The Task Prioritization Agent (GPT-4) cleans up the task list. Additionally, the Execution Agent (GPT-4) completes tasks and stores task/result pairs in the ES Quantization Library.

AutoGPT incorporates memory management via Redis or third-party storage solutions, facilitating both short-term and long-term memory within its conversational system. This setup enables the preservation of past inputs for future reference. The model treats short-term memory as a temporary repository, effective from a few seconds up to a minute, whereas long-term memory serves as a reservoir for substantial knowledge and extended historical data, supporting storage for up to 64,000 words. This dual memory system allows AutoGPT to progressively learn and evolve into a more proficient AI agent.

AutoGPT stores historical data in lists and maximizes the historical context provided to GPT-4 with each interaction, contingent upon token availability. Code analyses indicate that AutoGPT maximizes token usage for each task, incorporating previous instructions if space allows after entering a new command. Moreover, each interaction with GPT-4 includes pertinent contextual information like the current time, aiding the handling of temporally sensitive tasks. AutoGPT maintains a comprehensive historical record, ensuring the most relevant information is relayed to GPT-4 during each interaction.

AutoGPT leverages its Internet access to collect data, efficiently sourcing and compiling information from a wide array of online platforms. This includes data from social media, financial records, consumer behaviors, and market trends, enabling AutoGPT to adapt and respond to new information and evolving market conditions effectively. This capability positions AutoGPT as a valuable tool for both data gathering and analytical research.

Despite being a groundbreaking open-source experiment, AutoGPT is not yet a fully developed application or product and may fall short in complex, real-world business environments. Its limitations in understanding context can lead to irrelevant outputs, potentially wasting time and resources since such outputs might need further editing to align with the desired context. AutoGPT’s effectiveness in task completion largely depends on feedback from large-scale models, and without a reliable method to measure the accuracy of these models’ outputs, it cannot guarantee the precision or authenticity of its results. Moreover, AutoGPT may not fully recognize the constraints of its created agents, sometimes resulting in misperceptions. Nonetheless, AutoGPT is still a robust tool, especially effective in managing simpler tasks that can be segmented into smaller components.

10.3 Competitors in middleware frameworks

LLMs are adept at selecting and utilizing the right tools and can even plan tasks to meet long-term objectives. Middleware frameworks are becoming crucial portals for next-generation human–computer interactions, inviting fierce competition from the market. Among the competitors facing LangChain, key players include the following:

Microsoft’s Semantic Kernel project. As illustrated in Fig. 10.6, Semantic Kernel shares some objectives with LangChain but emphasizes the use of “large language models” in its framework rather than specifically “GPT-4.” This approach is due to its strategy of employing multiple LLMs concurrently to manage business processes, which encompasses session management, a prompt engine, memory management, and connectivity needs for services like dialog histories and external APIs. However, Semantic Kernel targets the engineering community, aiming to integrate LLM functionalities into existing applications, a strategic focus that sets it apart from LangChain’s emphasis on independent developers. While Microsoft poses a formidable challenge to LangChain, its close ties with OpenAI may limit its flexibility in supporting a diverse range of LLMs in the future, unlike LangChain.

The components are divided into several layers: Agent, Secure Frontend Layer, Prompt Orchestration Engine, Session & Token Data Layer and various Azure services. The Agent layer includes Mobile App, Browser-based App, Desktop App and Teams App. The Secure Frontend Layer includes Web App, API Management and App Gateway. The Prompt Orchestration Engine includes LLM AI Application Development Toolkit Semantic Kernel, Azure Serverless Solutions, App Services and App Container Azure Kubernetes Service. The Session & Token Data Layer includes Azure Cache and Azure Analytics. The various Azure services include Unstructured Data, Azure Cognitive Services, Azure Storage, Structured Knowledge Base, Embedding and Azure Database. The diagram also features the OpenAI logo.

Dust and Scale AI Spellbook represent the trend towards no-code and low-code approaches in developing applications with LLMs. While these platforms provide intuitive UI/UX designs, many developers might seek platforms that offer a broader range of functionalities and allow for more experimental flexibility beyond the constraints of low-code environments.

GPT-Index, which leverages LangChain, is particularly geared towards enhancing memory capabilities and facilitating data integration with LLMs. In comparison, LangChain provides a more extensive and abstract set of functionalities, enabling users to tailor and assemble components according to their specific needs and use cases.

For end users, one of LangChain’s most formidable competitors is the ChatGPT plugin. LangChain, as middleware, leverages ChatGPT’s linguistic understanding to transform natural language into a format compatible with Wolfram tools, a scientific search engine rich in knowledge and facts, to access Wolfram’s robust mathematical reasoning capabilities (as seen in Fig. 10.7). However, shortly after securing a $10 million investment, LangChain found itself facing a new challenge as OpenAI launched official ChatGPT plugins, including one for Wolfram. These plugins overlap significantly with LangChain’s functionality by enabling Internet data access, integration with external knowledge bases, and the utilization of third-party tools. This development suggests that the plugins could compensate for some of ChatGPT’s initial limitations, such as enhancing real-time updates and incorporating external knowledge, potentially diminishing the role of middleware like LangChain within the broader ChatGPT ecosystem.

On the left, ChatGPT starts with a prompt, which undergoes text tokenization to create a token vector representation. This representation is processed by a language model neural net, which is trained using textual training data and reinforcement training. The neural net generates iterative tokens and probabilistic choices, resulting in generated text. On the right, WolframAlpha begins with a question or computation, which is processed through generalized grammar and linguistic understanding. This leads to symbolic representation using Wolfram Language. The symbolic representation accesses curated structured data, computational algorithms and real-time data to produce a computed answer and a structured report.

The release of ChatGPT plugins is widely viewed as a strategic move by OpenAI towards commercialization, aimed at creating an app store for the era of LLMs. Another viewpoint is that the initiative to release plugins was also motivated by OpenAI’s interest in gathering insights on how users apply apps and APIs to address specific challenges.

The market for new-generation human–computer interactions, powered by LLMs capable of understanding user intents and adeptly selecting and employing tools to fulfill tasks, is heating up. Besides OpenAI, entities such as Adept AI, Inflection AI, and Meta’s Toolformer are competing for leadership in this space. Importantly, for LLMs to serve as the backbone of future human–computer interactions, they must be both accurate and reliable.

ChatGPT plugins have encroached on the market space traditionally occupied by LangChain. These plugins rely solely on OpenAI’s LLMs, which could pose unforeseen risks to their providers, including businesses. LangChain, by comparison, can work with various open-source and proprietary LLMs, offering more flexibility in integrating third-party resources and potentially garnering more trust from niche market developers. Consequently, LangChain may be the superior choice for developers looking to create complex systems and manage their backend applications. This is largely thanks to LangChain’s robust open-source community, which supports an extensive ecosystem of third-party tools—a distinct competitive edge.

10.4 Summary

Middleware tools based on LLMs need to call these models and blend them with business logic. However, the sensitivity of business data limits their deployment in many corporations. Currently, there are no established quantitative methods to effectively gauge the output of these models, and despite their potential importance in production environments, concerns about latency and security are significant. It might be more practical to allow users to configure models and generate their own prompts for model invocation.

On the other hand, using middleware to integrate LLMs can considerably boost the productivity of small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs). Over the next 3–5 years, it’s anticipated that SMEs will increasingly adopt tools like LangChain and other third-party middleware to streamline their operations through the use of LLMs, helping them stay competitive in a fierce market. This indicates a promising growth trajectory for the middleware ecosystem.

Chapter 11 The future path of large language models

Abstract

This chapter explores the future trajectory of large language models (LLMs) like GPT-4, emphasizing their potential to evolve into strong artificial intelligence (AI). The chapter discusses the challenges these models face, such as data resource limitations and the inherent constraints of autoregressive architectures. While GPT-4 has demonstrated remarkable capabilities, questions remain about whether scaling up data and parameters alone will lead to true general AI. The chapter also delves into concepts like embodied intelligence, highlighting projects like Google’s PaLM-E and Microsoft’s ChatGPT for Robotics, which integrate LLMs with robotics. These advancements suggest a future where AI could perform complex tasks across multiple domains. However, significant hurdles remain in achieving robust, adaptable AI systems that can autonomously learn and make decisions in real-world environments.

Keywords

Artificial intelligence; large language models; GPT-4; strong AI; autoregressive models; data resource depletion; embodied intelligence; PaLM-E; robotics; task planning

GPT-4 has garnered worldwide attention due to its impressive capabilities. As the field of artificial intelligence (AI) advances, there is increasing concern about its potential to pose threats to human civilization. During the creation of this book, more than a thousand leaders in technology and business, spearheaded by Elon Musk, have urged tech companies to pause their research. They have initiated a thorough debate on whether GPT-5 and future iterations could match or even exceed human intelligence. Nonetheless, some argue that the AI journey led by GPT might be limited by the availability of data resources and the inherent constraints of its autoregressive models, which could prevent it from achieving true strong AI. This chapter delves into the myriad challenges that large language models (LLMs) face.

11.1 The path to strong artificial intelligence

GPT-4 seamlessly blends reasoning abilities with a vast repository of human knowledge. Its formidable logic and reasoning are not only the bedrock of GPT-4 exceptional user experience but also potentially chart a course for future advancements. This naturally raises the question: Have the GPT series models laid the groundwork for the development of strong AI?

When delving into the workings of the GPT series, one cannot help but ponder the origins of these impressive capabilities. From a mathematical or machine learning standpoint, autoregressive language models construct a model of the probabilistic relationships between sequences of words. They use combinations of words in sentences—treated as mathematical vectors—as inputs to predict the probability distribution of various words in subsequent positions. Theoretically, the larger the sequence generated, the greater the accumulated errors.

The technological path that OpenAI has taken with its Transformer’s Decode architecture raises a question: Is it grounded in rational mathematical deduction, or is it akin to mystical “alchemy”? A clear pattern emerges from examining the evolution of LLMs: As these models, like GPT-3, GLaM, LaMDA, and Megatron-Turing NLG, are trained on increasingly vast datasets and expand in size, they reach a critical point. Beyond this point, they begin to display complex abilities and features that were unforeseen by their developers, known as the models’ emergent capabilities.

These emergent abilities defy prediction by traditional scaling laws, indicating that their development is not linear in relation to the increase in parameter size. As illustrated in Fig. 11.1, these specific capabilities might seem random or insignificant until they cross a certain threshold—likely between 1022 FLOPs and 1023 FLOPs—after which there is a significant leap in their performance. The exact threshold for these emergent capabilities varies, depending on factors like the quality of the training data, with higher quality data possibly requiring fewer parameters and less training.

The image contains 8 graphs labeled A through H, each showing the performance of different AI models on specific tasks as the model scale increases. (A) Mod. arithmetic: The y-axis shows Accuracy (percent) from 0 to 50. The x-axis shows Model scale (training FLOPs) from 10 raised to the power of 18 to 10 raised to the power of 24. LaMDA, GPT-3 and PaLM models are plotted. GPT-3 shows a sharp increase at 10 raised to the power of 23 FLOPs, reaching about 33 percent accuracy. PaLM shows an increase at 10 raised to the power of 24 FLOPs, reaching about 15 percent accuracy. (B) IPA transliterate: The y-axis shows BLEU (percent) from 0 to 50. The x-axis is the same as (A). Only PaLM is plotted, showing an increase at 10 raised to the power of 23 FLOPs, reaching about 12 percent exact match. (D) Persian QA: The y-axis shows Exact match (percent) from 0 to 50. The x-axis is the same as (A). Only PaLM is plotted, showing a sharp increase at 10 raised to the power of 23 FLOPs, reaching about 45 percent exact match at 10 raised to the power of 24 FLOPs. (E) TruthfulQA: The y-axis shows Accuracy (percent) from 0 to 70. The x-axis shows Model scale from 10 raised to the power of 20 to 10 raised to the power of 24. GPT-3 and Gopher models are plotted. Gopher shows an increase at 10 raised to the power of 23 FLOPs, reaching about 43 percent accuracy. (F) Grounded mappings: The y-axis shows Accuracy (percent) from 0 to 70. The x-axis is the same as (E). Only GPT-3 is plotted, showing a sharp increase at 10 raised to the power of 23 FLOPs, reaching about 45 percent accuracy. (G) Multi-task NLU: The y-axis shows Accuracy (percent) from 0 to 70. The x-axis is the same as (E). GPT-3, Gopher and Chinchilla models are plotted. All three models show sharp increases at 10^23 FLOPs, with Chinchilla reaching the highest accuracy of about 68 percent. (H) Word in context: The y-axis shows Accuracy (percent) from 0 to 70. The x-axis is the same as (E). GPT-3 and PaLM models are plotted. PaLM shows a sharp increase at 10 raised to the power of 23 FLOPs, reaching about 63 percent accuracy. The detailed data points are as follows: - For model scale (FLOPs) of 10 superscript 18 end superscript, the performance of LaMDA is 0, GPT-3 is 0, Gopher is not available, Chinchilla is not available, PaLM is 0 and Random is 0. - For model scale (FLOPs) of 10 superscript 20 end superscript, the performance of LaMDA is 0, GPT-3 is 0, Gopher is not available, Chinchilla is not available, PaLM is 0 and Random is 0. - For model scale (FLOPs) of 10 superscript 22 end superscript, the performance of LaMDA is 0, GPT-3 is 8, Gopher is not available, Chinchilla is not available, PaLM is 7 and Random is 0. - For model scale (FLOPs) of 10 superscript 24 end superscript, the performance of LaMDA is 15, GPT-3 is 33, Gopher is not available, Chinchilla is not available, PaLM is 16 and Random is 0.

How do LLMs like Transformers develop their emergent capabilities? These models are structured hierarchically, consisting of multiple processing layers, typically with 6–8 encoders and decoders that represent varying degrees of abstraction. Each layer captures a different level of complexity, ranging from simple patterns to intricate relationships. As the model trains, the output of each layer feeds into the next, with every weight adjusted based on data through a predefined loss function. This structured approach allows these models to autonomously identify underlying features and patterns in data and adaptively fine-tune their parameters according to statistical trends. As the model’s parameters expand and cross a certain threshold, new and unexpected capabilities begin to manifest.

Interestingly, the human brain exhibits a similar hierarchical structure. It contains about 100 billion neurons spread across various layers of the cerebral cortex, each layer interconnected in a hierarchical fashion. The lower layers process basic features like edges and shapes, while the upper layers manage more complex information like objects, faces, scenes, and abstract concepts. Remarkably, the Transformer architecture shares some parallels with the cerebral cortex, which handles functions such as language, thought, perception, emotion, and planning. Both systems are layered, processing inputs at different levels of abstraction and integrating diverse information sources. Furthermore, the Transformer’s “memory” function mirrors how the cerebral cortex stores and retrieves information over time to comprehend language and produce coherent thoughts.

The Transformer model boasts a sophisticated attention mechanism, allowing various parts of the network to hone in on the most pertinent information. At each layer, this mechanism calculates a weighted sum of the encoder output vectors, pinpointing the elements most relevant for subsequent layers. Attention heads are capable of processing segments of the sequence in parallel, much like how humans can attend to multiple information streams at once. Crucially, it can zero in on key inputs and forge connections between disparate concepts. For instance, at a bustling party, while conversing with a friend, your brain can isolate their voice from a cacophony of background noise. It also links the ongoing conversation with related past discussions, demonstrating attention-driven connections between memories.

Moreover, the brain is adept at managing sequential information, such as language, motor skills, tasks, and temporal reasoning. The Transformer, designed specifically for processing sequential data like natural language, approaches a level of proficiency akin to human capability. In the brain, complex recurrent connections between neurons facilitate an “ongoing internal dialog” that sustains contextual awareness. Conversely, the Transformer employs its self-attention mechanism to capture dependencies among elements within the input sequence. Thus, both the brain and the Transformer are equipped with tailored architectures and network components for learning and processing sequential information, though they do so in distinct ways and with different efficacy levels.

While Transformers and the human brain process information in similar ways, Transformers remain quite restricted in their application scope, lacking the robust functions derived from the brain’s biological complexity. The parallels between deep neural networks and the brain offer insights into the fundamentals of natural language processing, yet pinpointing further distinctions between these systems is essential for creating algorithms that mimic human learning and reasoning.

Both Transformers and the human brain exhibit similarities in their hierarchical structure, attention mechanisms, and handling of sequential information, hinting at a shared foundation in their approaches. GPT-4, a landmark model, is theorized to have reached a parameter scale comparable to the number of neurons in the brain, incorporating multimodal training data. This integration heralds the emergence of new multimodal abilities, such as capabilities that combine audio, visual, and text semantics to form a more advanced intelligence, not just an additive combination of these elements. It is speculated that as more modal data is incorporated and the parameter size increases, an even broader range of multimodal capabilities will emerge. Whether the GPT-N series could lead to strong AI and whether humanity’s destiny aligns with Elon Musk’s view that “carbon-based civilization is merely a precursor to a silicon-based one” are provocative questions for our future. These developments remind us that the future has already begun.

11.2 Data resource depletion

In assessing the potential and future trajectories of GPT-5 and subsequent GPT-N models, it’s crucial to focus on three key aspects: the volume of data, the quality of data, and the sources from which the data are drawn. These factors are likely to play pivotal roles in determining whether GPT-5 and its future iterations can match or even exceed human intelligence.

Recent media speculations have begun forecasting the release date for GPT-5, with expectations set for groundbreaking advancements in multimodal processing. Reports suggest that GPT-5 could be trained using approximately 25,000 GPUs. TechRadar has noted that Chachi BT has been trained on 10,000 NVIDIA GPUs, each surpassing the performance of the A100 model. Regarding the timeline for GPT-5 debut, predictions from Geordie Rybass are often referenced; he anticipates the model or its equivalents might launch in late spring or early summer of 2024.

Research from DeepMind has highlighted a critical balance between the size of model parameters and the volume of training data required. Notably, models like GPT-3 and Palm possess far more parameters than necessary, pointing towards a greater need for extensive, high-quality data. Consequently, the claim that GPT-4 requires one trillion parameters may not hold true. In fact, GPT-5 could potentially maintain or even reduce the parameter count compared to GPT-4. A LessWrong blog post from July 2022 suggests that the performance limitations in current language models are more due to data constraints than the sheer scale of the models. With sufficient data, there’s no necessity for models to escalate to 500 billion, a trillion, or more parameters.

The quality of data plays a pivotal role in refining GPT models, yet sourcing high-quality data remains challenging. Currently, GPT-3 and other models train on approximately 300 billion tokens. Considering that DeepMind’s Chinchilla model was trained on around 1.4 trillion tokens, it’s likely that GPT-5 will significantly expand its data volume. Known sources for high-quality data include scientific articles, books, web-scraped content, news articles, code, and Wikipedia, ranging between about 4.6 trillion to 17 trillion words in total. This indicates we are nearing the limits of available high-quality data, a threshold that may be reached between 2023 and 2027, profoundly impacting the immediate future of AI.

The uncertainty surrounding data sources remains a significant issue. Companies such as Google and OpenAI have kept their data sources under wraps, likely to sidestep disputes related to ownership and compensation. Moreover, as legal complexities escalate in areas like AI-generated imagery, pinpointing the origins of data is becoming increasingly important. Despite these challenges, GPT-5 aims to leverage past experiences to amass a greater quantity of high-quality data. Since the transition of GPT-4 to Microsoft, the repository of high-quality data has consistently grown by approximately 10% per year, even without enhancements in data utilization or extraction techniques.

Looking beyond just data-related challenges, GPT-5 is poised for breakthroughs across several domains. Researchers might unearth techniques for deriving high-quality data from lesser-quality sources. Furthermore, implementing automated “chain-of-thought prompting” strategies could notably boost the model’s effectiveness. Although constraints related to performance and costs may impede training efforts, recycling data across multiple training sessions has proven to be an effective tactic. Additionally, the strategic creation and curation of datasets are vital for enhancing the model’s capabilities in solving complex mathematical problems and beyond.

If GPT-5 effectively leverages its 9 trillion high-quality tokens, it’s poised for a transformative leap in performance, with potential significant impacts on the job market. GPT-5 could outperform humans in reading comprehension, logical reasoning, critical thinking, and even in subjects like high school physics and mathematics. Moreover, as technologies advance in text-to-speech, image-to-text, text-to-image, and creating virtual avatars from text-to-video, AI teachers could soon become a reality. However, the release of GPT-5 remains uncertain, partly because its launch may hinge on the progress of OpenAI’s internal safety research. Sam Altman, CEO of OpenAI, has indicated that the model will only be released after thorough alignment, safety checks, and collaboration with external auditors.

In this setting, aligning AI safety research with model performance enhancements is essential. While the exact release date of GPT-5 remains unknown, it is poised to introduce disruptive innovations in the future, driven by an influx of high-quality data and further optimization strategies. The evolution of GPT-5 and future models will be shaped by the quality, sources, and strategies employed in data use. Despite prevailing uncertainties, with robust high-quality data and enhanced model training and data utilization strategies, GPT-5 is expected to significantly elevate its performance soon. This advancement could see it excel in fields like reading comprehension, logical and critical thinking, high school physics, and mathematics, potentially reshaping the job market. Nonetheless, it remains crucial to balance technological progression with safety and ethical considerations, ensuring that advances in AI come with necessary safeguards.

11.3 Limitations of autoregressive models

Many experts argue that the inherent limitations of their architecture might prevent autoregressive language models like the GPT series from reaching strong AI simply by scaling up multimodal data and parameters. Scholars, including Yi Ma, have pointed out that autonomous agents need to efficiently learn from past experiences and adapt to current contexts to thrive in complex environments. These capabilities are essential for collecting data, making decisions, and executing actions. Yet, the AI advancements of the last decade have predominantly been driven by deep learning methods that rely on “brute force” training. While these models gain certain abilities in perception and decision-making, the features they learn are often not easily interpretable.

Furthermore, the overreliance on increasing computational power for training has led to a surge in the size and cost of deep learning AI models, causing various practical issues. These include a lack of diversity in learned representations, poor training stability, and a diminished sensitivity to adaptiveness and catastrophic forgetting, complicating their deployment in real-world settings.

Yi Ma identifies the separation in training discriminative models for classification and generative models for sampling or replay within current deep networks as a fundamental issue. These models are usually open-loop systems that depend on supervised or self-supervised training from start to finish. Yet, it has long been recognized, with early insights from Wiener, that these systems lack the capability to self-correct prediction errors and adapt to environmental changes.

As depicted in Fig. 11.2, Yi Ma argues that true general AI must embrace principles of simplicity and self-consistency. The simplicity principle emphasizes extracting and representing information from data in a way that enhances learning efficiency. This approach is deeply intertwined with information encoding theory, which looks at precise quantification and efficient representation of data information. This principle has robust support in neuroscience, evident in brain functionality such as sparse coding and subspace encoding.

It shows the processing of low-dimensional structures of external multimodal high-dimensional sensory data, including visual, tactile, auditory and olfactory inputs. The sensory data is processed through a sensor/encoder function f sub z working end sub equals f of x, resulting in z hat equals f of x hat. This is followed by a closed-loop self-consistency process where z approximately equals z hat equals f of g of z. The controller/decoder function g of z comma eta is also shown. The internal compact and structured representations for parsimony include sparse coding, subspace, grid/positional cell and sparse graph. The diagram also highlights the roles of the multimodal sensory cortex and entorhinal cortex in the primate brain.

The simplicity principle in deep learning offers solutions to several pervasive issues, such as neural collapse, mode collapse, and susceptibility to deformations and adversarial attacks. By emphasizing simplicity in both model design and the training process, models can be developed to be more efficient, stable, and adaptable.

The self-consistency principle is about achieving learning goals effectively and efficiently through computation. It is intimately linked to control/game theory, which provides a robust computational framework through closed-loop feedback systems for reaching any quantifiable objective. In deep learning, self-consistency addresses critical challenges like catastrophic forgetting and poor adaptability to changes in the environment. By creating a unified closed-loop system that incorporates both discriminative and generative models, it fosters autonomous learning, which in turn boosts the efficiency, stability, and adaptability of the learning process.

However, autoregressive LLMs like the GPT series, which rely on an extensive array of neurons to learn vast amounts of knowledge and language, consequently see a substantial increase in both size and computational costs. This growth clearly conflicts with the simplicity principle. Additionally, being open-loop systems, GPT models require supervised or self-supervised end-to-end training and lack the ability to autonomously correct errors in predictions.

Turing Award laureate LeCun has expressed skepticism about the future of such models. From a mathematical or machine learning viewpoint, LLMs essentially map the probabilistic relationships between sequences of words, treating combinations of words in sentences as input vectors to predict the probability distribution of words in subsequent positions. Theoretically, the larger the output sequence, the higher the accumulation of errors, making it challenging for these models to overcome inherent issues such as factual inaccuracies, logical flaws, contradictions, and limitations in reasoning.

LeCun asserts that the cornerstone of future AI research should be the development of “world models.” Unlike existing LLMs, humans and animals exhibit far more efficient learning capabilities. For example, a novice teenage driver can master driving in merely 20 h, while current autonomous driving systems require extensive training on millions or even billions of labeled data points, or millions of reinforcement learning trials in simulated settings. Despite this extensive training, their capabilities still don’t match up to human standards. The key challenges in machine learning today involve mastering world representations and predictive models, reasoning, and planning complex action sequences.

LeCun points out that the real challenge for AI in the coming decades will revolve around crafting architectures for world models and developing effective training paradigms. World model training, a prime example of self-supervised learning, draws its core idea from pattern completion. Predicting future or momentarily unobserved inputs extends this concept. However, as the world is only partially predictable, a significant issue is representing uncertainty in these predictions. Probabilistic models struggle in continuous spaces, and generative models must account for every world detail. To address this, LeCun has introduced the Joint-Embedding Predictive Architecture, enabling a predictive model to encompass multiple predictions.

In conclusion, despite the successes of LLMs like the GPT series, they confront substantial challenges. For AI to reach a stage of true generality, future research should adhere to the principles of simplicity and self-consistency and delve into developing world models and innovative training paradigms.

11.4 Embodied intelligence

Back in 1950, Alan Turing pioneered the notion of “embodied intelligence.” This concept revolves around the idea that an intelligent entity, like a robot, can dynamically engage with its surroundings. Through sensing, acting, and learning, such agents gather knowledge and bolster their ability to adapt to environmental changes.

11.4.1 Challenges of embodied intelligence

Over the last decade, robotics has seen remarkable strides in perception, representation, and action. Deep learning, for example, now outperforms humans in image classification, while humanoid robots from Boston Dynamics showcase exceptional limb control and coordination. Despite these breakthroughs, AI continues to grapple with challenges in extracting environmental insights from data, decision-making, and universal learning. The concept of “embodied intelligence” underscores essential skills robots must develop:

Perception: Robots need the ability to sense their environment, gathering multimodal data like images, sounds, and tactile information through cameras and sensors.

Representation: Robots must be capable of extracting and portraying information from their sensory data to better comprehend and interpret their surroundings.

Decision-making: With the information at hand, robots should make informed decisions, such as plotting the best paths in navigation tasks.

Action: Robots must execute these decisions, controlling motors or actuators to move or handle objects.

Learning: Through ongoing interactions, robots should continually learn, enhancing the efficacy and adaptability of their actions and decisions.

Today’s LLMs, like ChatGPT, have showcased their aptitude for understanding tasks, planning, and leveraging third-party tools, signifying a stride towards general intelligence. Particularly, once these models gain the ability to handle multimodal data such as images, videos, and audio, they will unify skills in representation, decision-making, and learning, essentially equipping robots with a real “brain.” This capability for perception, sourced from cameras and sensors, combined with action through robotic motion systems, paves the way for embodied intelligence. This evolution is set to drive profound societal changes.

11.4.2 PaLM-E

Google’s PaLM-E project has showcased how powerful LLMs can be in tasks requiring embodied reasoning. PaLM-E, a sophisticated model built solely with a Transformer decoder architecture, undergoes pretraining on multimodal utterances. These utterances blend visual inputs, continuous state estimates, and textual encodings, aligning disparate types of data—images, sensor readings—alongside natural language into a unified vector space.

The unique structure of PaLM-E’s utterances marries text with continuous, multisensory observations, weaving them into intricate multimodal sentences. For instance, consider the query, “What happened between <img 1> and <img 2>?,” where each <img i> represents an image embedding processed by a Vision Transformer. As illustrated in Fig. 11.1, PaLM-E generates responses autoregressively, crafting either direct answers or a series of text-based decisions. These outputs are utilized in a variety of practical applications, from continuous robotic maneuver planning to visual question answering and generating captions for images (Fig. 11.3).

As depicted in Fig. 11.4, the project’s demonstration video illustrates an embodied reasoning task where a robot is tasked with fetching chips from a drawer. This task demands more than just robotic functionality; it requires an understanding of human language and intentions. The robot must also adeptly observe and interact with its surroundings, devising a sequence of subgoals and the steps needed to achieve them.

The left image depicts the robotic arm reaching into an open drawer, where a packet of chips is visible. The right image shows the robotic arm having retrieved the packet of chips from the drawer. The background includes a counter with various items such as condiment bottles and disposable cups. The robotic arm is marked with the number 06.

11.4.3 ChatGPT for robotics

In contrast to the PaLM-E project, Microsoft’s ChatGPT for Robotics (GPTR) operates on a nonmultimodal version of ChatGPT, which does not possess multimodal sensory capabilities. Instead, it relies on auxiliary tools to transform environmental information into textual descriptions that feed into ChatGPT. The process involves crafting prompts that detail task constraints, the weight and size of objects, environmental conditions (like avoiding collisions during navigation), the current status of the robot and its target, along with examples of how to complete tasks (refer to Fig. 11.5).

The process starts with Prompt, APIs plus Objective which is connected to LLM. From LLM, there are two arrows: one leading to Deployment Improvements and another leading to Any task, any robot. Any task, any robot is connected to three tasks: Check Shelves, Arrange colored blocks into the Microsoft logo and Go to the kitchen and find a place to heat up lunch.

Moreover, the process of breaking down tasks and making decisions in ChatGPT requires managing the robot through textual commands. This demands the encapsulation of the robot’s basic action libraries and descriptions of these functions. With the right prompts in place, ChatGPT is capable of generating the necessary code to accomplish targeted tasks, thereby enabling the control of robotic actions through its foundational action libraries.

GPTR stands out as a cost-effective embodied intelligence solution primarily because it eliminates the need for task-specific fine-tuning of LLMs. Leveraging a broad existing knowledge base and sophisticated reasoning abilities, GPTR can directly produce code that controls robots, dramatically cutting down the time and cost usually required for traditional programming.

In conventional robotic programming, each task demands the creation of bespoke control algorithms—a process that’s both time-intensive and reliant on highly skilled engineers. However, GPTR circumvents these hurdles by utilizing a pretrained LLM to swiftly generate effective control code. In doing so, the LLM taps into a wide array of knowledge it has gathered during training, encompassing programming tactics, algorithm design, and optimization techniques, to tackle specific challenges in robotic tasks.

Fig. 11.6 effectively outlines the four crucial steps GPTR follows to perform tasks under diverse conditions: Initially, it involves encapsulating the functions of the library used to control robotic actions, including movements, stops, and acquiring target location data. Following this, a prompt is constructed that encompasses the task and environmental descriptions along with the library functions. Subsequently, a simulation run is conducted, ensuring continuous interaction with ChatGPT to guarantee the task’s objectives are met. Ultimately, the code is deployed directly onto the machinery.

It outlines the four crucial steps GPTR follows to perform tasks under diverse conditions: Initially, it involves encapsulating the functions of the library used to control robotic actions, including movements, stops and acquiring target location data. The steps are as follows: 1. Define a task-relevant robot API library. An example is given where a person wants a robot to cook an omelet and serve it to their grandpa. The API functions include locateobject, movetolocation, cookitem and grab_object. APIs should be easily implementable on the robot and have descriptive text names for the LLM. They can be chained together to form more complex functions. 2. Build prompt following engineering principles. The prompt should consider the robot's goal and the functions available. An example prompt is provided for a home assistant robot to prepare an omelet for an elderly person. 3. User on the loop: iterate on solution quality and safety. This involves using prompt and dialogue with ChatGPT, running simulations and evaluating and improving the solution. 4. Execute! The final step is to execute the task using the robot.

GPTR is adept at handling three distinct types of tasks: The primary task type is zero-shot task planning, where it formulates plans for new tasks without prior specific knowledge. For instance, as depicted in Fig. 11.7, in drone inspections, ChatGPT adeptly interprets user intent from inputs, devising viable inspection strategies and patrol routes for drones. This enables even nontechnical users to oversee drone operations effectively.

The left section shows a close-up view from the base of the wind turbine looking up towards the rotor hub and blades, with a drone flying nearby. In the inset at the bottom left corner, there is a shadow of the wind turbine on the ground. The right section shows the wind turbine from a distance with a drone flying closer to the viewer. The inset at the bottom right corner shows a side view of the wind turbine blade. Both sections have a backdrop of a coastline and ocean.

Furthermore, ChatGPT acts as a natural language interface between users and robots, making it straightforward for nontechnical individuals to interact with robots and steer them through complex tasks. Yet, in real-world scenarios, robots must manage ever-evolving environmental data and tackle uncertainties. Thus, LLMs like GPTR often require integration with other AI technologies, such as reinforcement learning or computer vision, to foster robust and reliable embodied intelligence.

The second category encompasses complex tasks facilitated by interactive dialog. These tasks require ongoing interactions between the robot and the user to meet sophisticated objectives. Such engagements foster intricate control patterns, allowing ChatGPT to assimilate and amalgamate simple control schemes to tackle bigger, more intricate tasks. Moreover, ChatGPT can translate high-level semantic demands for generating control code into actionable, low-level coding tasks, simplifying interactions for nontechnical users.

As illustrated in Fig. 11.8, consider a nontechnical user directing ChatGPT to devise a goal achievement and obstacle avoidance algorithm for a drone equipped with a forward distance sensor. While ChatGPT can compose most parts of the obstacle avoidance algorithm, it still needs user input on the drone’s direction to pinpoint any overlooked steps. Despite feedback being in high-level semantic text, ChatGPT can make targeted code adjustments to refine its solution. These instances highlight how ChatGPT progressively refines its solutions through interactive dialogs, thus enhancing performance in complex scenarios. This method is particularly advantageous for nontechnical users, who can guide ChatGPT using natural language without delving into the technical specifics. Nevertheless, to ensure precise comprehension and adjustments by ChatGPT, users must offer distinct and detailed feedback. Sometimes, iterating on ChatGPT’s improvements might be necessary to reach optimal outcomes.

The course consists of various metal structures, resembling scaffolding, arranged in a pattern. In the background, there is a green square target visible in the distance, which appears to be the drone's destination. The environment suggests an open field with a clear sky and the shadow of a tower is cast on the ground, indicating the presence of a light source in the simulation. The drone is equipped with four rotors and is in flight, navigating through the obstacles. There are no people or other discernible objects in the image.

The third task type involves the perception-action feedback loop, requiring robots to initiate a series of actions based on their environmental perceptions. ChatGPT’s abilities in this domain can be gauged from two distinct angles.

Initially, consider how ChatGPT employs API libraries to establish a perception-action feedback loop. In its code outputs, ChatGPT precisely utilizes perception functionalities such as image capture and object detection to gather essential navigation and control information for robots. This showcases ChatGPT’s prowess in handling visual perception data, ensuring efficient navigation and operation in unfamiliar settings.

Furthermore, assess ChatGPT’s capability to create a sustainable perception-action feedback loop. For this exploration, researchers continuously feed perception information into the model via text dialogs, first translating environmental data into text before submitting it to ChatGPT. This process reveals that ChatGPT can interpret these data and craft appropriate actions based on them, illustrating its adeptness at translating sensory inputs into actionable responses.

In a recent experiment involving the perception-action feedback loop, ChatGPT was equipped with a computer vision model, part of its functional toolkit, to aid in exploring and navigating toward a user-specified object in unfamiliar environments. Utilizing an object detection API with YOLOv8 backend, it identified object boundaries, while ChatGPT was responsible for generating navigation code, estimating objects’ relative angles for approach. When supplemented with depth sensor data, ChatGPT could further refine its algorithm, showcasing its modular adaptability.

As illustrated in Fig. 11.9, the setting was constructed using the Habitat simulator, directing the agent to a designated interest area. This setup tested ChatGPT’s role in a perception-action feedback loop through dialog-based interactions. Environmental perception data—descriptions of visible objects in polar coordinates—was fed into ChatGPT via textual dialog. ChatGPT’s responses were confined to movement distances and turning angles, which the agent executed, producing fresh environmental data in the process.

The image displays a simulated indoor setting resembling a living room with a couch, coffee tables and decorative items. In the foreground, there is a command prompt window with multiple lines of text indicating an action Moving towards couch followed by varying distances in centimeters. The background shows a larger view of the simulated room and in the top right corner, there is a smaller inset image with colored boxes around objects such as a potted plant, dining table and couch, likely representing object detection within the simulation environment.

The trial confirmed the model’s capability to handle straightforward navigation tasks. However, it also highlighted the need for further research into how to precisely depict environmental scenes in text or vector formats for more intricate tasks and settings.

To summarize, the perception-action feedback loop task compels the robot to devise a series of actions driven by its environmental perceptions. Continual input of this environmental data into ChatGPT, coupled with monitoring of its outputs, reveals that the model adeptly interprets observational data and formulates suitable actions accordingly. These findings demonstrate ChatGPT’s potential as a control system within a perception-action feedback loop, facilitating effective navigation in complex environments.

In real-world applications, identifying more effective methods to contextualize scenes is crucial to enhance the model’s environmental comprehension. Moreover, research must continue into optimizing the efficiency and simplicity with which sensory information is fed into the model. Practical scenarios may also bring additional challenges, including computational resource constraints, the model’s real-time performance, and safety and reliability concerns.

Presently, initiatives like Microsoft’s GPTR and Google’s PaLM-E highlight the potential influence of LLMs in robotics. Despite their limitations in reasoning and task breakdown, such as the granularity of specialized robotic APIs for particular tasks, GPTR relies on manually curated prior knowledge to compile action primitives into skill libraries, leading to issues in design granularity and overlooking safety in real-world environments. Nonetheless, leveraging LLMs for embodied intelligence could significantly boost existing productivity, marking this as a field of substantial commercial promise.

11.5 Summary

With the unveiling of GPT-4 and the ongoing development of GPT-5, AI is progressing into a groundbreaking new phase. Future iterations of the GPT series are expected to push the boundaries of knowledge-intensive tasks, utilizing even larger scales of multimodal data and increasingly complex models. Despite these advancements, current LLMs exhibit limitations, particularly in complex reasoning tasks. For instance, their ability to process simple character copy reasoning or arithmetic diminishes significantly with longer strings or numbers. Moreover, their capacity for intricate behavior planning remains somewhat underdeveloped. Yet, as the scale of these models increases, they start to exhibit predictable scale-dependent behaviors.

This situation prompts two crucial inquiries: To what extent can scaling up LLMs address these challenges? And considering the emergent capabilities of these models, what unforeseen abilities might develop with further increases in scale and more diverse multimodal data integration? While there are critiques of the GPT research direction, the abilities displayed by GPT-4—from understanding across various modalities and domains to its potential in intelligent agency and task planning in robotics—signal the dawn of a new era in general AI. The future has arrived, and embracing it is our only option.
